1201 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Donahue v. Fair Employment & Housing Com.


No. B052118


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE.


13 Cal. App. 4th 350; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 32; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1373; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14633



November 27, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted February 27, 1992 (S024538), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 2093.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 1 Cal. App. 4th 387 and 7 Cal. App. 4th 1498.)]
Review Dismissed September 30, 1993 and cause Remanded to the Court of Appeal, Second District, Division Five, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5274.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C754458, David P. Yaffe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court remanding the matter to the FEHC is affirmed, but modified in that the FEHC is directed to dismiss the complaint against the Donahues. The Donahues are entitled to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant housing commission challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent landlord's petition for writ of mandate, remanded the matter back to appellant and ordered that it set aside its decision on whether respondent committed discrimination in refusing to rent to a cohabiting couple and to permit further briefing on the matter.


OVERVIEW: Unmarried cohabiting couple brought an action against respondent landlord, under Cal. Gov't Code § 12955, which prohibited discrimination in housing based on marital status when respondent refused to rent an apartment to them because it would have compromised respondent's sincerely held religious belief that fornication and its facilitation was sin. Appellant housing commission found respondent had violated § 12955 and awarded damages against respondent. Respondent appealed. The trial court remanded the case back to appellant and ordered appellant to permit further briefing on whether cohabiting single people were covered by §12955. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed the remand, but modified it by ordering appellant to dismiss the complaint against respondent. The court found that California used a balancing test and compelling state interest test and that respondent was entitled to an exemption under Cal. Const. amend. art. I, § 4 because the state's interest in protecting cohabiting unmarried couples was not of paramount concern to justify impinging on respondent's right to free exercise of his religious beliefs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's remand, but modified it by directing appellant housing commission to dismiss the complaint against respondent landlord because respondent was exempt from the housing discrimination laws because the state's interest in protecting unmarried cohabiting couples from discrimination was not sufficient enough to outweigh respondent's right to free exercise of religion under the California constitution.

 

1202 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



United Air Lines, Inc. v. County of San Diego


No. D012688


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


1 Cal. App. 4th 418; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 212; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1374; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14669



November 27, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied December 24, 1991, Reported at 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1457.
Review Denied March 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1242.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 603873, Thomas Oliver LaVoy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of the Airlines is reversed. The trial court is directed to enter judgment in favor of the County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County, (California), which rendered summary judgment in favor of respondent airlines and refunded taxes assessed on what appellant determined to be respondents' taxable possessory interests in the use of the airport.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county made possessory interest tax assessments against respondent airlines based on airport landing facility use. Respondents brought an action against appellant for a refund of these taxes. The trial court rendered summary judgment in favor of respondents. On appeal, the court held that under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 21, there could be multiple exclusive concurrent yet independent users of the same property. The court held that the possessory tax was valid because respondents' right of possession in the airport landing facilities was sufficiently exclusive. The court held that the taxes were permitted under 49 U.S.C.S. app. § 1513(b), because they were calculated based on the actual annual landing fees paid by each respondent. The court held that there was a rational basis for placing commercial and noncommercial aircraft in different tax classes, and thus respondents were not denied equal protection. The court held that the assessments were authorized because there was a change in ownership by virtue of the renewal or extension of respondents' possessory interests in the landing facilities. The court held that that valuation method did not include enterprise value.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment rendered by the trial court in favor of respondent airlines in their action against appellant county to recover taxes assessed on possessory interests in the use of the airport. The court directed the trial court to enter judgment in favor of appellant because the possessory tax assessments were valid and not preempted by federal law.

 

1203 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Weidenfeller v. Star & Garter


No. D011485, No. D011599


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


1 Cal. App. 4th 1; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 14; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1326; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14377



November 21, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the section pertaining to Star and Garter's appeal, including the concurring opinion.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 587241, Alpha L. Montgomery, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff assault victim sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego (California), which attributed 75 percent of fault to plaintiff's assailant and 20 percent to defendants, bar owners and others, and 5 percent to plaintiff and, pursuant to Proposition 51, the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1431, used those percentages to reduce awards to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff assault victim was attacked in the parking lot of defendant bar owners and others'. Plaintiff brought suit against defendants, alleging that their failure to provide adequate lighting and security presence substantially caused his injuries. The trial jury found that defendants' negligence proximately caused plaintiff's injuries and attributed 75 percent fault to the assailant, 20 percent to defendants, and 5 percent to plaintiff and used those percentages to reduce damage awards. Defendants appealed and plaintiff cross-appealed. The court affirmed the judgment because the trial court properly applied Proposition 51, the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1431.2. The court held that § 1431 did not allow plaintiff to transfer the intentional actor, assailant's, responsibility to the negligent tortfeasor, defendants. The court also held that § 1431.2 was not contrary to public policy because a consequence of the statute was that persons injured by an insolvent tortfeasor would not be able to obtain full recovery. Finally, the court held that the jury applied a proper comparative fault standard and understood fully how to compare the tortfeasors' conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which reduced plaintiff assault victim's damage awards using the jury's attribution of 75 percent fault to the assailant, 20 percent fault to defendant bar owners and others and 5 percent to plaintiff, because the relevant statute did not allow plaintiff to transfer the intentional actor's responsibility to the negligent tortfeasor.

 

1204 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Murtha


No. A049473.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two.


235 Cal. App. 3d 1688; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 788; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1508; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14312



November 20, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted December 16, 1991. (See 14 Cal. App. 4th 1112)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. C-27594, Richard M. Harris, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which denied his motion to quash a search warrant and suppress the seized evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted, based upon a plea of nolo contendere, of residential burglary. On appeal, appellant contended that the trial court erroneously denied his motion to quash the search warrant, because the affidavit prepared in support thereof included evidence of an illegally recorded telephone conversation. The court affirmed appellant's conviction. However, the court held that the Omnibus Crime Control and Safe Streets Act, 18 U.S.C.S. § 2510 et seq., provided no room for an interpretation that would permit the use of the admittedly illegal recordings of appellant's telephone conversation to support the search warrant, and that to permit the use thereof would be directly inconsistent with the statutory objective to guard against invasions to personal privacy. Nevertheless, the motion to suppress was properly denied, because the police officer that obtained the warrant relied in good faith on the magistrate's erroneous determination that the recording could be used to support the search warrant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and held that although the search warrant was improperly based on a telephone conversation recorded in violation of the Omnibus Crime Control and Safe Streets Act, the motion to suppress was properly denied, because the police officer relied in good faith on the magistrate's erroneous ruling that the recording could be used to support the warrant.

 

1205 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Alberto R.


No. D012369


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


235 Cal. App. 3d 1309; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 348; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1284; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8954; 91 Daily Journal DAR 13870



November 7, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J157882, Runston G. Maino, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which imposed an enhanced sentence for defendant's participation in gang-related offenses in violation of the Street Terrorism Enforcement and Prevention Act, Cal. Penal Code § 186.22(b). Defendant was convicted of attempted murder and assault with a firearm in connection with a drive-by shooting.


OVERVIEW: After a drive-by shooting, defendant was convicted of attempted murder and assault with a firearm. Defendant was also found to have engaged in gang-related offenses pursuant to the Street Terrorism Enforcement and Prevention Act, Cal. Penal Code § 186.22. On appeal, defendant asserted that section 186.22 was unconstitutional in that it was vague and overbroad. The court upheld the constitutionality of the statute and affirmed defendant's conviction. According to the court, to withstand a claim of facial vagueness based on due process considerations, a statute had to be sufficiently definite to provide adequate notice of the conduct proscribed and had to provide sufficiently definite guidelines for the police to prevent arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. The court held that § 186.22 carefully circumscribed the conduct to which it applied in that a person could not be made criminally liable under it unless he or she acted with the intention of promoting, furthering, or assisting the commission of a felony. The court further stated that § 186.22 did not prohibit membership in gangs, rather it prohibited promotion, furtherance, or assistance in any felonious criminal conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempted murder and assault with a firearm. Also, the court affirmed the sentence enhancement based on a finding that defendant participated in gang-related offenses because the Street Terrorism Enforcement and Prevention Act was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The statute carefully circumscribed the conduct to which it applied.

 

1206 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Las Palmas Associates v. Las Palmas Center Associates


No. B051688


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


235 Cal. App. 3d 1220; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 301; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1280; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8904; 91 Daily Journal DAR 13688



November 5, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 11, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C575906, Arthur Baldonado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to reflect our decision to reduce respondents' award of fraud damages to $ 232,393 and the award of punitive damages to $ 2 million and is otherwise affirmed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for its determination and award of attorney's fees incurred by respondents in defense of appellants' declaratory relief action. Each side is to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sellers sought review of a jury verdict entered in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded respondent buyers damages for fraud and breach of contract, plus punitive damages, in their cross-complaint filed against appellants regarding a dispute over appellants' duty to guaranty the rent of two tenants.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sellers built and sold a shopping center to respondent buyers. When a dispute arose concerning appellants' duty to guaranty the rents of two tenants, appellants filed an action for declaratory relief and respondents filed a cross-complaint for fraud and breach of contract. A jury rendered verdicts that awarded respondents damages for breach of contract and fraud, plus additional punitive damages. The trial court separately denied appellants declaratory relief and appellants sought review of that determination and of the damages awards. The court modified to judgment to reduce the fraud and punitive damages awards and affirmed the remainder of the judgment, holding that respondents had met their burden of proving fraud by clear and convincing evidence, and that there was substantial evidence supporting the jury's punitive damages award. The court found that sellers had made guarantees without an intent to perform them, and systematically avoided performing them. The court further held that evidence of appellants' worth was valid evidence to consider in making a punitive damages award.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to reduce respondent buyers' awards of fraud damages and punitive damages and otherwise affirmed the judgment against appellant sellers, holding that there was substantial evidence proving that appellants committed fraud.

 

1207 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brewer


No. A051318


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


235 Cal. App. 3d 909; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 146; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1263; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8805; 91 Daily Journal DAR 13512



October 30, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 92207, Michael E. Ballachey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence and dismissed the action, which involved a charge of possession of cocaine base in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possessing cocaine base in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350. A police officer had observed defendant drinking beer in front of a liquor store and, after detaining and searching him, seized cocaine from him. The city had an ordinance which prohibited the consumption of alcoholic beverages in various public places, including a public way, but the public way portion of the ordinance had been held to be unconstitutionally vague. Accordingly, the lower court granted defendant's motion to suppress the evidence and dismissed the action, holding that because the public way language of the ordinance had been held to be unconstitutional, the officer had no basis to detain and search defendant. On appeal, the court reversed the lower court's order of dismissal, holding that even though a portion of the ordinance had been found to be unconstitutional, the remainder of the ordinance was still valid, including its provisions prohibiting the consumption of alcohol on public sidewalks, and thus the officer was entitled to rely on the ordinance as the basis for initiating the detention.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order of dismissal, holding that the police officer's search and seizure of defendant based on a municipal ordinance found unconstitutional in part was valid because the ordinance, bereft of its unconstitutional portion, was still valid and supported the officer's actions.

 

1208 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gamez


No. G009572


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


235 Cal. App. 3d 957; 286 Cal. Rptr. 894; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1275; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8774; 91 Daily Journal DAR 13572



October 30, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976(b), parts IV, V, VI, VII and VIII in the majority opinion are not published. Also not published is part II in the concurring opinion of Wallin, J.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-76286, Luis A. Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The stay is removed from the enhancement imposed for the personal use of a firearm (§ 12022.5) in count I, and the enhancement imposed for discharging a weapon from a vehicle causing great bodily injury (§ 12022.55) attendant to that count is ordered stricken. The enhancements imposed for the personal use of a firearm (§ 12022.5) in counts III, IV and V are ordered stricken, and the consecutive sentence imposed in count VII is ordered stayed per section 654. (§ 1260.) The superior court clerk is directed to modify the abstract of judgment accordingly and transmit it to the Department of Corrections and other appropriate agencies. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered judgment on defendant's convictions for various crimes, including attempted murder and assault with a firearm and enhanced the sentences for his crimes under Cal. Penal Code § 186.22(b) because defendant was found to have committed the crimes in association with a criminal street gang.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of several crimes, including attempted murder and assault with a firearm and his sentences were enhanced under Cal. Penal Code § 186.22 for committing the crimes in association with a criminal street gang. Defendant and amici curiae challenged the enhancements. The reviewing court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that the admission of expert testimony of police officers regarding the particular gang and its conduct was permissible because such information was beyond common knowledge, there was a strong foundation for the officers' opinions and the opinions were based upon personal observation and experience, not mere hearsay. The court held that § 186.22 was not vague or overbroad in violation of U.S. Const. amend. I because the statute prohibited membership in a criminal street gang and targeted the conduct of engaging in criminal activity. The requirement that defendant commit the crime for the benefit of or in the association with the gang and with the specific intent to promote the gang in any criminal conduct was sufficient to appease concerns regarding a due process violation based upon "pure" association.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entry of judgment on defendant's convictions for attempted murder, assault with a firearm and other offenses because the admission of expert testimony regarding gangs did not violate the evidence code or confrontation clause, there was sufficient evidence that the crimes were committed in association with a criminal gang, and the enhancement for gang activity was not vague, overbroad, nor violative of due process.

 

1209 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Jerabek v. Public Employment Relations Bd.


No. C009380


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


2 Cal. App. 4th 1298; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 181; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1521; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1060; 143 L.R.R.M. 2717; 92 Daily Journal DAR 1798



October 29, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] ORDERED PUBLISHED JANUARY 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 507208, Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the "concurrent notice with escrow" provisions of California Code of Regulations, title 8, sections 32992, subdivision (c)(2) and 32995, subdivision (a)(2) comport with Hudson and adequately protect the First Amendment and due process rights of agency fee payers. The judgment is reversed, interveners to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervener labor organizations challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which found that the regulations authorizing notice with escrow procedures for nonmember agency fees, promulgated by defendant Public Employment Relations Board, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, §§ 32992(c)(2) and 32995(a)(2), were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff teachers sought a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief pursuant to the Ralph C. Dills Act, Cal. Gov. Code § 3512 et seq., that defendant Public Employment Relations Board's regulations regarding the collection of agency fees from nonmember employees violated constitutional safeguards. On cross-motions for summary judgment, the trial court upheld most of the regulations but found the "concurrent notice with escrow" provisions, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, §§ 32992(c)(2) and 32995(a)(2), to be unconstitutional. Intervener labor organizations appealed and the reviewing court reversed, finding the regulations protected the U.S. Const. amend. I rights of plaintiffs. The court found that any funds collected under § 32992(c)(2) were placed in an escrow account pending objection by nonmembers and that the union did not have access to those funds to use them for impermissible purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of plaintiff teachers. The court held that the regulations regarding the collection of agency fees from nonmember employees promulgated by defendant Public Employment Relations Board's adequately protected the First Amendment and due process rights of plaintiff teachers.

 

1210 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Anderson


No. B057289


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


235 Cal. App. 3d 586; 286 Cal. Rptr. 734; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1223; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8526; Copy. L. Rep. (CCH) P26,849; 91 Daily Journal DAR 13093; 21 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1223



 October 23, 1991 
October 23, 1991


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 8, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 22, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. MA001417, Margaret M. Grignon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) conviction for violating Cal. Penal Code § 653w by selling pirate recordings on the basis that statute violated U. S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction, based on evidence he possessed for sale some 4,500 pirate recordings, for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653w. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the statute, contending that it violated the U. S. Const. amend. I, that it was unconstitutionally overbroad, and that it was preempted by federal copyright laws. The court affirmed the conviction because a reasonable relationship existed between the statute and the state's interest in preventing deception of consumers, defendant had no standing to assert an overbreadth argument based on conceivable unconstitutional applications, and there was no conflict with federal copyright laws.


OUTCOME: Conviction affirmed because a reasonable relationship existed between the statute and the state's interest in preventing deception of consumers.

 

1211 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Shiseido Cosmetics (america) v. Franchise Tax Bd.


Nos. C008051, C009273


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


235 Cal. App. 3d 478; 286 Cal. Rptr. 690; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1206; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8451; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12958



October 21, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - All portions of this opinion are certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III and IV of the Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 20, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 336238, 512689, Jeffrey L. Gunther and Ronald B. Robie, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tax payer sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which consolidated two suits involving franchise taxes and granted summary judgment and judgment on the pleadings to respondent Franchise Tax Board, based upon failure to exhaust administrative remedies and untimeliness.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer was an American company wholly owned by a Japanese corporation. Appellant sought refund of franchise taxes assessed by defendant Franchise Tax Board pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 25101, using the unitary tax method of worldwide combined reporting (WWCR) . Appellant paid the taxes, noting "paid under protest" on its checks, and claimed that application of WWCR violated the U.S. Constitution. In the first action filed by appellants, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondent holding that appellant had not exhausted administrative remedies before filing its suit. In the second action, filed after appellant had submitted a refund claim, the trial court held that the refund claim was untimely. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that, as to the summary judgment, appellant was required to first pay the tax and then follow statutory procedures for recovery. As to the dismissal for lack of timeliness, the court held that the notation on the back of the check did not constitute an imperfect claim for refund that would toll statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed because the tax refund suit was barred by the failure to exhaust constitutionally compelled administrative remedies and no timely, sufficient refund claim was filed.

 

1212 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Peretto v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. A052735


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


235 Cal. App. 3d 449; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 392; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1215; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12945; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8444



October 21, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 23, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 147504, William H. Stephens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to deny Peretto's petition and reinstate the Department of Motor Vehicles' order of suspension. The department shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of motor vehicles sought review of a judgment for respondent driver by the Superior Court of Marin County (California) reversing the suspension of respondent's driver's license. Appellant asserted that the superior court's equal protection analysis was erroneous.


OVERVIEW: Respondent driver's license was suspended pursuant to an administrative hearing in which respondent was found to be driving under the influence of alcohol, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152. Respondent sought a writ of mandate, which was granted. Appellant state department of motor vehicle sought review of the order directing appellant to set aside respondent's suspension. Respondent claimed in rebuttal that the statutory scheme violated his equal protection rights because the scheme distinguished between some commercial licensees and other drivers by creating a narrow exception governing the period of license suspension. Respondent asserted that a commercial licensee who never had a previous drunk driving conviction or license suspension, and who was driving a noncommercial vehicle with the forbidden blood-alcohol level was able to receive a lesser term of suspension. The court reversed and remanded the order and directed the superior court to reinstate the order of suspension, because there was some rational basis for the difference in treatment, and the presuspension administrative hearing available to drivers comported with the requirements of due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgment of the trial court in favor of appellant department of motor vehicles regarding procedures for suspending the driver's license of respondent driver, and held that the procedures governing suspension and differentiation between a narrow group of commercial and noncommercial licenses did not violate equal protection principles. The court held the procedures were rationally related to an important state goal of safety.

 

1213 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Albion River Watershed Protection Ass'n v. Department of Forestry & Fire Protection


No. A048704.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two.


235 Cal. App. 3d 358; 286 Cal. Rptr. 573; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1205; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8420; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12888



October 18, 1991 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 7, 1991. Review Denied January 16, 1992 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 58116, James F. King, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant environmental group challenged respondent forestry and fire protection department's approval of three timber harvest plans through a petition for writ of mandamus, which was denied by the Superior Court No. 58116 of Mendocino County (California).


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, logging company, proposed three timber harvesting plans, all three of which were approved by respondent forestry and fire protection department. Only one plan received site specific comments and objections, which were submitted by a private citizen. After the three plans were approved, appellant environmental group was formed and challenged respondent's approval of all three plans. The trial court dismissed appellant's action on the basis that appellant lacked standing because it failed to exhaust its administrative remedies. The court affirmed respondent's approval of two plans, but remanded the matter to the trial court on the third plan, holding that under the Friends of Mammoth exception, a plaintiff who had participated in proceedings at the administrative level need not personally have asserted the issue upon which review was sought so long as some other person similarly participating therein had raised the issue. The court found that the trial court must determine if appellant could assert the site specific comments and objections submitted by the private citizen to the third plan.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent forestry and fire protection department's approval of timber harvest plan (THP) 100 and 145, and reversed respondent's approval of THP 114, holding that it must be remanded to the trial court to determine whether appellant environmental group could bring itself within the Friends of Mammoth exception so as to assert in the litigation the site specific comments and objections submitted in opposition.

 

1214 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Harroman Co. v. Town of Tiburon


No. A050216


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


235 Cal. App. 3d 388; 1 Cal. Rptr. 2d 72; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1209; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8427; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12896



October 18, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 15, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 144193, William H. Stephens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments denying the petition for writ of administrative mandamus and the request for declaratory relief are affirmed. Pursuant to the nonpublished portion of our opinion, the judgment dismissing count seven alleging inverse condemnation and count eight alleging unreasonable precondemnation activity is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court to allow plaintiffs an opportunity to amend their complaint for inverse condemnation. The parties are to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (Cal.), which denied plaintiffs' petition for writ of administrative mandamus and sustained defendant town's demurrer to plaintiffs' request for declaratory relief in plaintiff's action for review of defendant's denial of plaintiffs' application for approval of a residential development plan.


OVERVIEW: After denial of their application for approval of a master subdivision plan by defendant town, plaintiff landowners filed an action for declaratory relief and a petition for a writ of administrative mandamus against defendant. The trial court denied the petition and sustained defendant's demurrer, and plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed and held that when plaintiffs filed a tentative subdivision map during revision of defendant's general plan, the proposal must have been evaluated against the draft general plan under consideration rather than the existing general plan. No land development may proceed unless it conforms to a valid general plan. However, an exception exists, and development may be approved during an extension under Cal. Gov't Code § 65302.6 so long as that development conforms with both the existing general plan and the proposed revision. Defendant should not be required to approve a development proposal based upon an abated or suspended general plan.


OUTCOME: The judgments denying plaintiff landowners' petition for writ of administrative mandamus against defendant town and plaintiffs' request for declaratory relief were affirmed because plaintiffs' application was properly reviewed against defendant's proposed general plan rather than the existing plan.

 

1215 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ivester


Nos. D009731, D013585


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


235 Cal. App. 3d 328; 286 Cal. Rptr. 540; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1191; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8394; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12844



October 17, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR94542, Bernard E. Revak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), convicting him of unlawfully manufacturing methamphetamine in excess of one pound. Defendant contended that although he did not file the statement required by Cal. Penal Code § 1237.5, his contention that his guilty plea was unknowingly and involuntarily made should be considered to determine if counsel was ineffective in failing to file it.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to unlawfully manufacturing methamphetamine in excess of one pound. On appeal, he contended his guilty plea was unknowingly and involuntarily made but failed to file a statement including the grounds for his contentions required by Cal. Penal Code § 1237.5. He also filed a writ of habeas corpus claiming his pleas were induced by counsel's inaccurate statement that the court would review his motion to disclose the identity of a confidential informant. The court affirmed the trial court's conviction and denied the writ of habeas corpus. Although defendant failed to file the statement required by § 1237.5, the court considered the substance of his contentions to determine whether his counsel's failure to file the statement prejudiced his case. The court held there was no prejudice where defendant's testimony that he "had a methamphetamine lab going in the residence" was a sufficient factual basis for his plea and his claim that he did not understand the nature of the offense was unsupported by any facts. On the issue of inducement, the court held there was no evidence that counsel's statement was the critical factor in defendant's decision to plead guilty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of unlawfully manufacturing methamphetamine in excess of one pound. The court held that although defendant did not file a statement required to effect an appeal of his contentions that his guilty pleas were unknowingly and involuntarily made, they should be examined to determine if counsel's failure to file the statement prejudiced his case. The court determined it did not.

 

1216 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re David L.


No. C010112


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


234 Cal. App. 3d 1655; 286 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1172; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8237; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12566



October 10, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 16, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 84902, James L. Long, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the juvenile court of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which charged him with misdemeanor battery, sustained an allegation that he had threatened to shoot another person, and dismissed an allegation of attempt to dissuade a victim from reporting a crime. Defendant contended that the evidence did not make out a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the order of the juvenile court that charged him with misdemeanor battery, sustained the allegation that he had threatened to shoot another person, and dismissed the allegation of attempt to dissuade a victim from reporting a crime. Defendant contended that the evidence did not make out a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422, which made it a crime to threaten another with death or great bodily harm, whether directly or through a third party. The judgment was affirmed. Defendant intended the victim to feel threatened, and he intended that an intermediary convey the threat to the victim. Defendant's threat followed a series of hostile encounters with the victim, culminating in a fight at school. Under the circumstances, the victim's fear for his safety and the safety of his family was reasonable. The finding by the juvenile court that defendant violated § 422 was supported by substantial evidence. The threat was sufficient if it induced a sustained fear. Defendant's threat to shoot the victim was not on its face and under the circumstances in which it was made either conditional or in jest. According to testimony, it was without equivocation or ambiguity.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed. The finding by the juvenile court that defendant violated the statute was supported by substantial evidence. Defendant induced sustained fear, and his threat was neither conditional nor in jest, but was without equivocation or ambiguity. The victim feared for himself and his family.

 

1217 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Ewing v. City of Carmel-By-The-Sea


No. H007702


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


234 Cal. App. 3d 1579; 286 Cal. Rptr. 382; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1173; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8193; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12504



October 9, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 5, 1991, and appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 8, 1992. Baxter, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. M 21130, Harkjoon Paik, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which found in favor of defendant city, finding constitutional Carmel-By-The-Sea, Cal., Ordinance No. 89-17, which prohibited transient commercial use of residential property for remuneration for less than 30 consecutive days.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted a zoning ordinance, Carmel-By-The-Sea, Cal., Ordinance No. 89-73, which prohibited the use of residential property for transient commercial purposes for less than 30 consecutive days. Plaintiff homeowners affected by the ordinance brought suit against defendant, alleging that the ordinance was unconstitutional and was barred by the doctrine of collateral estoppel. The trial court found the ordinance to be constitutional and the court affirmed. The trial court found the collateral estoppel doctrine to be inapplicable because the public interest exception to the doctrine permitted the relitigation of zoning ordinances. The court also found the ordinance to be constitutional because the city had a legitimate governmental purpose in maintaining the residential character of its neighborhoods and the ordinance, in prohibiting the short term commercial use of residential property, was reasonably related to that purpose. The court also found that the minimal diminution of ownership rights caused by the ordinance was outweighed by the public interest in maintaining permanent residential areas.


OUTCOME: The judgment that a city ordinance prohibiting short term transient commercial use of residential property was lawful was affirmed, because the ordinance was reasonably related to the governmental interest in maintaining the residential character of the zoned district, and because the diminution in plaintiffs' ownership rights were outweighed by the public interest in maintaining residential neighborhoods.

 

1218 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Anna J. v. Mark C.


No. G010225


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


12 Cal. App. 4th 977; 286 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1162; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12433



October 8, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted January 23, 1992 (S023721), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 291.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 234 Cal. App. 3d 1557 and 6 Cal. App. 4th 521.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. X-633190 and AD-57638, Richard N. Parslow, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Thorny questions about the rights of a "gestational surrogate," remedies in the event of breach of a surrogacy agreement, terms of payment and termination of pregnancy cry out for legislative guidelines. To the extent these issues present questions of law, they are matters for legislative resolution subject to constitutional restraint. They should not be settled by the judiciary applying its own ideas of what is  [***2] good "public policy." Our system of government does not make the courts de facto "philosopher-kings."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff surrogate mother appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), in favor of defendants natural father and natural mother, ruling they were the child's "genetic, biological and natural" father and mother. It also ruled that plaintiff had no "parental" rights to the child and that the surrogacy contract was legal and enforceable against plaintiff. The court further terminated the order allowing visitation.


OVERVIEW: In a case of first impression to ascertain who was the legal mother of a child, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of defendants natural father and natural mother, ruling they were the child's genetic, biological, natural and, therefore, legal, parents. The court found that plaintiff surrogate mother was not the natural mother because the only blood test evidence before the trial court flatly excluded plaintiff, who offered nothing to show the blood tests were inaccurate. Under Cal. Civ. Code §§ 7015, 7003, and 7004, and Cal. Evid. Code §§ 621 and 895, anyone excluded by blood tests was not the natural or legal parent. Plaintiff's due process claim could not deprive defendants of their traditional parental relationship. Plaintiff's equal protection rights had not been violated because there was no discrimination based on gender and the discrimination was rational and not arbitrary. The court did not need to decide whether the surrogacy contract was enforceable or to determine visitation because plaintiff did not seek to enforce the contract and had not applied for nonparent visitation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court because defendants natural father and natural mother were the child's "genetic, biological and natural" and, therefore, legal parents. The court did not need to decide surrogacy contract enforceability or visitation because plaintiff surrogate mother sought parental rights independently of the contract and had not sought visitation.

 

1219 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jaspal


No. F012992


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


234 Cal. App. 3d 1446; 286 Cal. Rptr. 337; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1167; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8143; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12377



October 7, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III through IX.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 15, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 38780, Robert T. Baca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the trial court for retrial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant convict sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), in which appellant was found guilty of and sentenced for first degree murder with a fire arm enhancement in a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convict was convicted of first degree murder with use of a firearm in the shooting death of his roommate. Appellant fled to England, was recaptured, and returned after an extradition hearing. At the extradition hearing, appellant's witnesses testified that appellant was in England at the time of the murder, while at his trial, appellant testified that he was present but did not commit the crime. The trial court allowed the prosecutor to cross-examine appellant, over objections, on his silence during the extradition hearing when his witnesses testified falsely on his behalf. The court reversed the conviction and ordered a new trial, holding that appellant's U.S. Const. amend. V right against self-incrimination was violated, because his silence at the extradition hearing was used against at the trial and was improperly interpreted as adopted admissions. The court found that the violation was prejudicial because the prosecutor's comments were used to bolster the weak credibility of the people's witnesses, and also "touched a live nerve" in the defense because the prosecutor's argument was that appellant's defense in England was inconsistent with his trial defense.


OUTCOME: The judgment that appellant convict was guilty of first degree murder was reversed and remanded for a new trial, because the appellant's silence at an earlier extradition hearing, in which his witnesses provided false testimony at to his whereabouts at the time of the murder, was used against him at trial in violation of his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination.

 

1220 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Scharf v. Regents of Univ. of Cal.


No. A044630


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


234 Cal. App. 3d 1393; 286 Cal. Rptr. 227; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1156; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8082



October 4, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 29, 1991, and appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 8, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 5444685, Henry Ramsey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, faculty members denied tenure, the University Council, and an organization of teachers, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondents, Regents of the University of California and officials, finding no triable issues of material fact as to whether the challenged peer review procedure denied appellants due process of law or violated their rights of privacy.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, faculty members denied tenure, the University Council, and a teachers' organization, commenced an action by filing a petition for writ of mandate and complaint against respondents, the Regents of the University of California and administrative officials. The superior court granted respondents' motions for summary judgment and judgment on the pleadings, finding that Cal. Educ. Code § 92612, which set out procedures for faculty review, was unconstitutional, that appellants failed to state causes of action on the privacy and due process claims, and that there were no factual issues to determine. Appellants sought review of the judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the superior court's judgment, finding that § 92614 conflicted with Cal. Const. art. IX, § 9, which granted the University of California immunity from legislative regulation, and that, therefore, respondents' refusal to violate the privacy of informants in the faculty review process was permissible. Further, the court found respondents' procedures non-violative of the right to privacy granted by Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and that appellants did not present any triable issues that would preclude summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, finding that the evaluation of academic personnel is within the area of autonomy granted to respondents under the California Constitution and that the challenged procedures did not on their face violate the due process clause of the United States Constitution or the privacy provision of the California Constitution. Further, the court agreed that there were no triable issues of material fact.

 

1221 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Board of Supervisors v. Sacramento Local Agency Formation Com.


No. C006792


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


6 Cal. App. 4th 475; 286 Cal. Rptr. 171; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1144; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12197



October 1, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted December 19, 1991 (S023805)
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 234 Cal. App. 3d 1231.)] (See 3 Cal. 4th 903)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 358798, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, affirmed in part and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff supervisors appealed a judgment entered in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that declared that the Cortese-Knox Local Government Reorganization Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 56000 et seq., was constitutional as applied and awarded attorney fees to real party in interest, an incorporation project. Defendant formation commission cross-appealed to challenge the timeliness of the writ petition.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff supervisors appealed a judgment that declared that the Cortese-Knox Local Government Reorganization Act (Cortese-Knox Act), Cal. Gov't Code § 56000 et seq., was constitutional as applied and also awarded attorney fees to real party in interest, an incorporation project. Defendant formation commission challenged the timeliness of the writ petition. The controversy involved legal challenges against the real party in interest's efforts to incorporate a new city comprised of unincorporated areas in Sacramento County. The holding of the trial court was reversed when the court held that the Cortese-Knox Act was unconstitutional as applied because equal protection guarantees required all residents of the unincorporated areas of the county, and not just the residents of the proposed city, to be accorded the right to vote on the issue of incorporation. This determination required invalidation of the attorney fees awarded to the real party in interest at trial, because it could no longer be deemed the prevailing party. The trial court's judgment was affirmed in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the trial court's judgment which declared a government reorganization statute to be constitutional and awarded attorney fees to respondent incorporation project. The court remanded the cause with directions to enter a declaration that equal protection required the franchise on the issue of incorporation to be extended to all residents of the unincorporated area. The judgment was affirmed in all other respects.
