1251 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



San Bernardino County Dept. of Public Social Services v. Superior Court


No. E009021


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


232 Cal. App. 3d 188; 283 Cal. Rptr. 332; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 812; 19 Media L. Rep. 1545



July 12, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, Nos. J-120219, J-120220, J-120221, J-120222, J-120223, J-120224 and J-120225, Frank O. Tetley, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ issue directing the juvenile court to vacate its order granting the press a conditional right to attend the hearings in this proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner department of public social services sought a writ of mandate from an order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that allowed a newspaper to attend a series of well-publicized juvenile dependency hearings.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the constitutionally protected public right of access to trials and pretrial proceedings in criminal cases did not extend to juvenile dependency proceedings. However, members of the press were persons having a direct and legitimate interest in the work of the court and may be permitted to attend such proceedings in the exercise of the juvenile court's discretion under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 346. In this case, however, the juvenile court abused its discretion in the newspaper to attend the proceedings subject to limitations on its right to investigate and publish. The case involved allegations of child abuse and child neglect. The public enjoyed a U.S. Const. amend. I right to attend criminal trials. However juvenile proceedings were usually private. Juvenile proceedings have not been historically open. Accordingly, the court found that the First Amendment right of access did not extend to juvenile dependency proceedings and issued a peremptory writ directing the juvenile court to vacate its order.


OUTCOME: A peremptory writ was issued directing the juvenile court to vacate its order granting the press a conditional right to attend the dependency hearings because the juvenile court was without power to restrict the press's right to investigate information that it had lawfully obtained.

 

1252 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Duffy v. Cal. State Pers. Bd.


No. C006810


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


232 Cal. App. 3d 1; 283 Cal. Rptr. 622; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 777; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5373; 91 Daily Journal DAR 8278



July 9, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 2, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 3, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 358548, John J. Boskovich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The minimum qualification requiring priests to be ordained, accredited by, and in good standing by the Roman Catholic Church is not unconstitutional under state or federal Constitutions and is therefore not void ab initio. Because Father Duffy cannot meet these minimum qualifications, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents are awarded costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant priest challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that denied appellant's appointment to a prison chaplain position, based upon respondent state personnel board's decision that he did not meet the qualifications because he was not a Roman Catholic priest in good standing. Appellant contended that his U.S. Const. amend. I rights were violated under the establishment clause.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether respondent state personnel board could allow the corrections department to limit certain publicly paid chaplaincy positions to persons ordained, accredited by, and in good standing, with the Roman Catholic Church, without violating the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I. Appellant priest applied for and was denied a position as a prison priest because he was not a priest in good standing. Appeals to the administrative body still resulted in denial, and appellant sought judicial review by writ of mandate. Doctrinal entanglement involved government in religion's very spirit, in its decisions on core matters of belief and ritual. The judgment was affirmed because the only means by which Roman Catholic inmates could exercise fundamental aspects of their religious life such as instruction, mass, receipt of sacraments, and other religious rites, was by having access to a priest ordained, accredited by, and in good standing with the Roman Catholic Church. The state's current requirements for Catholic chaplaincy positions assured such access for Roman Catholic inmates and did not violate the establishment clause.


OUTCOME: The judgment that denied appellant priest's appointment to a prison chaplain position was affirmed because appellant was not a priest in good standing with the Roman Catholic Church. The court held that respondent state personnel board's requirement did not violate the establishment clause, where it had a valid purpose to assist Roman Catholic inmates, and the qualifications for the position were therefore valid.

 

1253 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Rippberger


No. A047810


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


231 Cal. App. 3d 1667; 283 Cal. Rptr. 111; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 773; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5249; 91 Daily Journal DAR 8235



July 5, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts VIII, IX and X.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 13301-C, Lloyd von der Mehden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions for felony child endangerment from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), arguing that the state failed to prove that defendants acted willfully in causing the death of their child, where they were Christian Scientists who were just practicing their faith.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed from convictions of felony child endangerment. The convictions were based on the death of their daughter, after a 15-day illness during which defendants withheld medical attention and treatment on the basis of their beliefs as Christian Scientists. Defendants argued that there was no evidence of willful conduct on their part. A finding of criminal negligence was made by the application of the objective test of whether a reasonable person in defendants' positions would have been aware of the risk involved. If the trier of fact determined that, on an objective analysis, a reasonable person in that position would have been aware of the risk, then defendants were presumed to have had such an awareness. Criminal negligence could be found even when a defendant acted with a sincere good faith belief that his or her actions posed no risk. The conviction was affirmed because a sincere belief in the efficacy of Christian Science spiritual healing practices was not a defense to a charge of criminal negligence, as the relevant inquiry turned not on defendants' subjective intent to heal, but on the objective reasonableness of their course of conduct.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions for felony child endangerment were affirmed because defendants' subjective beliefs were not an issue in the criminal negligence action. The court held that the test to be applied in determining criminal negligence was based on the reasonable person standard.

Chatterjee v. Kizer


No. B050632


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


231 Cal. App. 3d 1348; 283 Cal. Rptr. 60; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 740; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5215; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7792



June 28, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of contentions III and IV and discussion parts III and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C717373, John Zebrowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order denying her petition for writ of mandate from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to compel defendant Director of the Department of Health Services to set aside his order denying Medi-Cal benefits to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff appealed from an order denying her petition for writ of mandate to compel defendant as Director of the Department of Health Services to set aside his order denying her Medi-Cal benefits. An administrative law judge concluded that plaintiff was disabled. However, defendant did not adopt the proposed decision but ordered another hearing. Plaintiff argued that an independent review showed that the trial court erred prejudicially by basing its decision on the erroneous conclusion that a claimant must be disabled for 12 consecutive months to be eligible for Medi-Cal benefits. Eligibility for Medi-Cal benefits was governed by Cal. Code Regs. tit. 22. The court found that it was both the disability impairment and inability to engage in any substantial gainful activity which must last or be expected to last 12 months or more, not merely the impairment. The impairment must continue to be severe and disabling for the entire time period, not merely existent. The judgment was affirmed because the disability as well as the impairment must last 12 months and that was not the case here.


OUTCOME: The order denying plaintiff Medi-Cal benefits was affirmed because plaintiff was not disabled and therefore not entitled to benefits.

People v. Hernandez


No. B053330


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


231 Cal. App. 3d 1376; 283 Cal. Rptr. 81; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 744; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5188; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7902



June 28, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 16, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 3, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 89P08994, Alice E. Altoon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of making telephone calls with the intent to annoy, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653m(a) and (b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions for making telephone calls with the intent to annoy, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653m(a) and (b). The court affirmed the convictions because § 653m(a) and (b) did not deny free speech rights guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2. The court found that the statute was not directed at particular groups or viewpoints, and was narrowly drawn to prohibit only limited kinds of telephone calls. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad because anonymous calls were permitted, provided the caller did not intend to annoy the recipient, and annoying calls were permitted, as long as the caller identified himself. The court ruled that language prohibiting threats to inflict injury was not unconstitutionally vague under the circumstances. The court determined that the trial court did not err in giving the jury a common dictionary definition of the word "obscene." The definition of "obscene" as predominantly dealing with sex in a manner appealing to the prurient interest was inapplicable to the telephone harassment statute because the First Amendment was not implicated in the prohibited conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for making telephone calls with the intent to annoy, holding that the telephone harassment statute did not deny defendant his rights to free speech because the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague. The court also held that the trial court did not err by instructing the jury with the common dictionary definition of "obscene."

 

1256 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



O'Hara v. Storer Communications


Nos. D011490, D011534


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


231 Cal. App. 3d 1101; 282 Cal. Rptr. 712; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 730; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5154; 91 Daily Journal DAR 8064; 19 Media L. Rep. 1225



June 27, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 24, 1991, and the petition of defendants and appellants for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 3, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 529168, James R. Milliken and Alpha L. Montgomery, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it denied prejudgment interest. The matter is remanded to the superior court with directions that the judgment be amended to include prejudgment interest pursuant to section 3291. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiff and defendant sought review of a judgment for the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in slander action. Plaintiff asserted she was improperly limited to special damages under Cal. Civ. Code § 48a and improperly denied prejudgment interest under Cal. Civ. Code § 3291. Defendant asserted plaintiff was improperly allowed to establish loss of income from inability to work due to stress resulting from slander.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a judgment in slander action that limited plaintiff's damages to special damages when she failed to made an adequate demand for a retraction under Cal. Civ. Code § 48a, but did reverse the denial of appellant's request for prejudgment interest. Defendants broadcast a television news report that mistakenly identified plaintiff as an alleged prostitute. Plaintiff's attorney sent a letter to defendants demanding a retraction, but specifying that it must be approved by plaintiff's attorney. Attorney never responded to defendants' attempts to discuss retraction and plaintiff sued for slander. The court held that plaintiff was properly limited to special damages when the letter was only a conditional demand for a retraction. The court held that the trial court properly allowed plaintiff to establish special damages for loss of income due to inability to work as a result of depression and emotional distress caused by slander. The court held that slander was an action for damages for personal injury and that plaintiff was entitled to prejudgment interest under Cal. Civ. Code § 3291. The court remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that limited plaintiff to special damages when her letter to defendants was only a conditional demand for retraction. The court affirmed plaintiff's right to establish damages for loss of income from inability to work due to emotional distress caused by slander. The court held that slander was an action for damages for personal injury, qualified for prejudgment interest, and remanded for that determination.

Long Beach Equities v. County of Ventura


Nos. B045047, B046558


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


231 Cal. App. 3d 1016; 282 Cal. Rptr. 877; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 734; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5130; 91 Daily Journal DAR 8130



June 27, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 25, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 26, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 103364, Bruce A. Thompson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer, appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) which sustained defendant county's demurrer to several counts of plaintiff's amended complaint, but overruled respondent city's demurrer on counts for inverse condemnation, denial of due process and declaratory injunctive relief. Respondent city petitioned for a writ of mandate to overturn the trial court's denial of its demurrer.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff developers, brought suit against respondents, county and city, seeking damages for, inter alia, inverse condemnation. Plaintiff contended that respondents' land use regulations would so greatly delay its development plan as to render them economically infeasible. The trial court sustained respondent county's demurrer. Respondent city's demurrer was sustained as to several counts of plaintiff's complaint, however the trial court overruled its demurrer on counts for inverse condemnation, denial of due process and declaratory and injunctive relief. Plaintiff appealed the judgment entered on respondent county's demurrer and other issues. Respondent city filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to vacate its order overruling its demurrer. The court affirmed the judgment for respondent county and granted respondent city's petition for a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to enter an order sustaining its demurrer without leave to amend. The court found that respondents' growth management ordinance was constitutional. Plaintiff had not stated facts which supported recovery for inverse condemnation as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: In consolidated appeals the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court for defendant county's demurrer as to several counts of plaintiff's complaint, but which overruled the demurrer on counts of inverse condemnation, denial of due process and declaratory injunctive relief. The court also issued a mandate to the trial court to enter a new and different order sustaining petitioner city's demurrer in its entirety without leave to amend.

Tahoe Regional Planning Agency v. King


No. C005345.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


231 Cal. App. 3d 885; 283 Cal. Rptr. 243; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 732; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5142; 91 Daily Journal DAR 8077



June 26, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted July 25, 1991 (See 233 Cal. App. 3d 1365)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. SLT1587, Lloyd B. Hamilton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant regional planning agency appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), granting a motion for summary judgment in favor of appellee advertisers, and declaring a portion of its sign ordinance, Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, Ordinance No. 24, invalid pursuant to the Tahoe Regional Planning Compact, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 66800 - 66801.


OVERVIEW: Appellee advertisers owned real property on which they placed billboards in noncompliance with appellant regional planning agency's sign ordinance, Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, Ordinance No. 24, § 5.10, compelling the removal of off-premise commercial billboard signs with a five-year pre-removal amortization period to recoup costs in lieu of just compensation. In an action initiated by appellant, summary judgment was granted for appellees. At issue on appeal was whether appellant was statutorily compelled to compensate appellees for removal of their signs under the federal Highway Beautification Act (HBA), 23 U.S.C.S. § 131, or constitutionally compelled to pay just compensation for a taking of appellees' property under U.S. Const. amend. V. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case, holding that summary judgment was inappropriate because there were triable issues of material fact as to whether the ordinance constituted a taking in its application. The HBA did not preempt the ordinance, nor did the ordinance constitute a taking on its face. A U.S. Const. amend. I challenge to the ordinance's validity was averted by limiting its reach to off-premise commercial signs.


OUTCOME: Because there were triable issues of material fact as to whether appellant regional planning agency's ordinance as applied effectuated a taking of appellee advertisers' property, summary judgment was inappropriate; judgment was reversed, and the case remanded. The Highway Beautification Act did not limit appellant's authority to compel the removal of nonconforming signs, and the ordinance, on its face, did not effectuate a taking.

In re Alvernaz


No. D012971


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 1059; 282 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 709; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7818



June 25, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied, July 2, 1991. Review Granted, October 17, 1991.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 231 Cal. App. 3d 792.)] (See 2 Cal. 4th 924)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. HC11143 and CR87143, Bernard E. Revak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude, therefore, that Alvernaz's defense counsel's error in advising him as to the possible consequences of his not guilty plea, although perhaps negligent in terms of legal practice, as not meeting the standard of practice in the community, did not result in deprivation of any constitutional or other right to which Alvernaz was entitled, and hence did not from a legal point of view cause prejudice. We therefore deny the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner renewed his request for habeas corpus relief after being denied habeas relief by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), from his conviction and sentence for robbery, burglary, and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, with a weapon and a prior conviction enhancement, claiming that mistakes of counsel prejudicially deprived him of his right to assistance of counsel under U.S. Const. amend. VI and Cal. Const. art. I, § 15.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner renewed his request for a writ of habeas corpus after his petition was denied by the trial court. He sought reversal of his criminal conviction and sentence upon the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel, the result of which was his entry of a plea of not guilty and submission to a jury trial. His position was that the mistakes of his counsel during the plea bargaining stages caused him to refuse a plea bargain, enter a plea of guilty, and suffer a penalty of incarceration for a minimum of 16 years, 7 1/2 months, rather than the maximum time in custody of 8 years calculated by his counsel. The court denied the petition because it concluded that the participation in a fair jury trial preserved all of petitioner's basic constitutional trial rights, thereby rendering any error of counsel at the plea bargaining stages legally harmless.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for habeas corpus. The court concluded that defense counsel's error in advising petitioner of the possible consequences of a plea did not cause prejudice, as it did not result in deprivation of any constitutional or other right to which petitioner was entitled.

 

1260 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bowen


No. C007726


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


231 Cal. App. 3d 783; 283 Cal. Rptr. 35; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 717; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4956; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7675



June 25, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, the Introduction, part I and the Disposition are certified for publication.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yuba County, No. 6925, Dennis J. Buckley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court is directed to modify the abstract of judgment to correctly indicate defendant's presentence credits. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which convicted defendant of second degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second-degree murder and sought review. He contended that he was illegally tried by a superior court judge who did not live in the county where he sat, in violation of Cal. Gov't Code § 69502 and that his conviction was void for lack of jurisdiction. The state contended that the statute was unconstitutional. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The constitutionality of the statute was not a relevant consideration in the conviction, because it was unrelated to defendant's guilt or innocence. Furthermore, because the judge was a de facto officer, defendant was required to raise the issue in a collateral action. Finally, the actions of a de facto officer, if done within the scope and by the apparent authority of office, were as valid and binding as if he were the officer legally elected and qualified for the office.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant's conviction for second degree murder was upheld, because the temporary trial judge who presided over defendant's proceedings did not lack authority to enter such judgment; hence the conviction was not void for lack of jurisdiction.

 

1261 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Huening v. Eu


No. C008543


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


231 Cal. App. 3d 766; 282 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 718; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4952; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7768



June 25, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 363615, Anthony DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bill opponents challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), in respondent bill proponents' mandamus action. Respondents contended that Cal. Elec. Code § 3564.1 was invalid because it violated U.S. Const. amend. I and it amended the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 81000 et seq., without having been enacted as required by that act.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bill opponents sought review of the judgment of the trial court in respondent bill proponents' mandamus action to have a portion of appellants' rebuttal language removed from a ballot petition because it used a proponent company's name without its consent as required by Cal. Elec. Code § 3564.1. The court reversed and held that the election bill was invalid, because its enactment did not comply with the procedure specified in the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 81000 et seq. (Act), which governed the amendment of the Act. Under Cal. Gov't Code § 81012, an amendment occurred when a new section affected the application of the original statute or impliedly modified its provisions. Although the election bill was not contained within the provisions of the Act itself, it affected ballot arguments and the content of ballot pamphlets. Having amended the Act, the election bill was invalid, because it was not enacted with the concurrence of at least two-thirds of the membership of each house of the legislature or by the voters as required by Cal. Gov't Code § 81012.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. Election bill opposed by appellant bill opponents was invalid, because it amended the Political Reform Act of 1974 but was not enacted with the concurrence of at least two-thirds of the membership of each house of the legislature or by the voters as required that act.

Meilicke v. County of Los Angeles


No. B020736.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One.


231 Cal. App. 3d 1306; 282 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 747; 1991 AMC 2781; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5173; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7798



June 23, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 3, 1991 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14, rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC56172, Robert C. Nye, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the County of Los Angeles, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of plaintiff boat owner for deprivation of his boat without due process of law, violation of civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, trespass, and false arrest. Plaintiff challenged the nonsuit in favor of defendants county and sheriff on his state law causes of action, and from the denial of prejudgment interest.


OVERVIEW: In a dispute over the attempted repossession of plaintiff's boat, defendant county appealed the judgment granted in favor of plaintiff for deprivation of his boat without due process of law, violation of his civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, trespass, and false arrest. Plaintiff challenged the nonsuit in favor of defendants county and sheriff. The court affirmed the judgment and held that plaintiff's rights under the Ship Mortgage Act, 46 U.S.C.S. § 951, protected him from the taking of his boat without the proper procedures. Defendants' policy which allowed a third party to take immediate possession of the boat over plaintiff's objection without due process violated plaintiff's right under § 1983. The court held that defendants were liable for plaintiff's losses because if defendants had recognized there could be no self-help repossession under a preferred ship mortgage, plaintiff would have retained possession of his boat and avoided injuries. The court affirmed the nonsuit regarding defendant sheriff and held that because there was not a clearly established law regarding repossession, defendant sheriff could not have violated it, and he was entitled to good faith immunity.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because defendants county and sheriffs' policy that allowed self-help in repossession of a preferred ship mortgage violated plaintiff boat owner's constitutional rights, defendants' actions caused plaintiff's damages, and defendant sheriff was properly granted immunity because he qualified for good faith immunity.

In re Shereece B.


No. H007309


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


231 Cal. App. 3d 613; 282 Cal. Rptr. 430; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 686; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4901; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7526



June 21, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey Country, No. A 2219, John M. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an alleged natural father of a minor, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which terminated his parental rights upon a petition filed for such termination under Cal. Civ. Code § 7017, by respondent, the county's department of social services; appellant claimed the trial court lacked jurisdiction, and if the order was properly entered, it violated his constitutional equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, a county's department of social services, filed a petition under Cal. Civ. Code § 7017 to terminate the parental rights of appellant, who was incarcerated, as to his minor daughter. The § 7017 petition noted termination proceedings against the mother, and alleged that the child had no presumed father as defined by Cal. Civ. Code § 7004, but it alleged (and the mother agreed) that appellant was the natural father. The trial court terminated appellant's parental rights, concluding that such was in the child's best interest. The court agreed with respondent that a trial court could terminate a natural father's parental rights under Cal. Civ. Code § 7006(c) before the formal commencement of an adoption proceeding, and that under the circumstances of appellant's situation, § 7006(c) directed the trial court to determine his rights as an alleged natural father according to the provisions of § 7017(d). Appellant was also not denied equal protection, despite receiving different treatment than that of the mother by the trial court, as appellant and the mother were not similarly situated, and appellant never had any sort of relationship with the child. The order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Termination of the parental rights of appellant, an alleged natural father, was affirmed because the trial court was indeed authorized to make such a termination before formal commencement of an adoption proceeding, and appellant's incarcerated status required such a determination. Appellant also received equal protection by the trial court, despite the treatment he was afforded by the trial court that was different than that of the mother.

People v. Superior Court (Broderick)


No. D014253


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


231 Cal. App. 3d 584; 282 Cal. Rptr. 418; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 687; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4827; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7490



June 21, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR112048, Thomas J. Whelan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ issue directing the court to vacate its order quashing the subpoenas duces tecum. The court is further directed to reconsider its finding Betty generally waived her psychotherapist-patient privilege and determine as to each doctor whether the privilege has been waived and as to what material. Such determination will require in camera review of the material sought. The court is directed to make findings with respect to the discoverability of specific material from each doctor to aid in any further appellate review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought a writ of mandamus or prohibition directed to respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California), compelling it to vacate an order quashing subpoenas duces tecum for records from various mental health professionals who had treated respondent, and either testified for the defense or whose records were relied upon by experts testifying for the defense, in respondent's first trial for murder.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with murder. In respondent's first trial that ended in a mistrial, she presented expert witnesses who testified as to her mental state at the time of and before the killings. The defense argued that respondent's responsibility for the killings should be mitigated or excused. Some of the defense experts testified that, in arriving at their opinions, they relied on the work of other doctors who had examined or treated respondent in the past. The state caused subpoenas duces tecum to issue to the mental health doctors upon whom respondent's expert witnesses in the first criminal trial had relied. The trial court quashed the subpoenas, on the basis that respondent's right against self-incrimination was violated by impermissibly lightening petitioner's burden. The state sought a writ of mandate or prohibition compelling the trial court to vacate its order. The court granted the writ petition, holding that respondent waived the psychotherapist-patient privilege by allowing the experts who relied on the allegedly privileged information to testify at the first trial.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's petition for a writ of mandamus directing that the trial court vacate its order quashing the subpoenas duces tecum for respondent's psychiatric records, holding respondent had waived the psychotherapist-patient privilege with respect to any communications between her and psychotherapists who testified or with respect to testifying experts who relied on such communications.

In re Marriage of Hyon & Kirschner


No. B051961


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


231 Cal. App. 3d 449; 282 Cal. Rptr. 408; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 690; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4700; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7392



June 20, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. FS5584, Harry E. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the trial court is ordered to reconsider the RURESA reimbursement claim subject to the three-year statute of limitations and respondent's ability to pay. Appellant is awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which found the Revised Uniform Reciprocal Enforcement Support Act, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1650 et seq., reimbursement was barred by a Florida dissolution judgment which reserved jurisdiction on child support but never set a support amount.


OVERVIEW: Respondent father obtained a Florida dissolution. The children were placed in foster care and wife was awarded spousal support with continuing jurisdiction reserved over the children. No child support orders were made because the children remained in foster care. Respondent moved to California and worked as a correctional officer. The Florida Department of Health and Rehabilitative Services provided the children with AFDC benefits. Florida initiated a Revised Uniform Reciprocal Enforcement Support Act (RURESA), Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1650 et seq., petition to collect accrued spousal support, obtain child support, and recoup the AFDC benefits. California brought an action to obtain the relief requested. After a noticed hearing the trial court ordered respondent to pay the spousal support arrearage and start child support payments. The AFDC reimbursement claim was denied, because the dissolution judgment constituted a support order for zero dollars pursuant to Florida law. On appeal, the court reversed because California law controlled and ordered reconsideration of the RURESA reimbursement claim subject to the three year statute of limitations and respondent's ability to pay.


OUTCOME: The denial of AFDC benefits was reversed and remanded, because the trial court incorrectly applied Florida, not California, law and the trial court was ordered to reconsider the RURESA reimbursement claim subject to the three year statute of limitations and respondent father's ability to pay.

Schuhart v. Pinguelo


No. A046211


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


230 Cal. App. 3d 1599; 282 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 602; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4385; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6781



June 7, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 636048-3, Dawn B. Girard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion, including recalculation of the penalties and attorney's fees should Schuhart prevail in his action for foreclosure. The Pinguelos shall recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted respondent bondholder's motion for summary judgment in a foreclosure action which included increased statutory penalties. Appellant argued that the increased penalties improperly altered the parties' contract.


OVERVIEW: Respondent bondholder sought the sale of parcels of real property in order to collect principal, interest, and penalties due for nonpayment on the property which secured his bonds. In granting respondent's motion for summary judgment the trial court also utilized respondent's means of calculating penalties increasing the interest rate to 2 percent for penalties imposed after January 1, 1981 pursuant to a concurrent amendment of Cal. Sts. and High. Code § 6442 and compounding the interest on a monthly basis. Appellant property owner sought review and the court reversed the grant of summary judgment for respondent, finding that the issuance of assessment bonds created a contract between the parties and the statutory amendment increasing the penalty could not change the obligations of one party. Additionally, the language of the statute did not express intent to compound penalties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment, finding that the amendment to the statute should not be used to impose a greater obligation to one of the parties in what was a contractual relationship, and the trial court erred by compounding the interest on the penalties.

Pinney v. Phillips


No. C007052


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


230 Cal. App. 3d 1570; 281 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 596; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4295; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6743



June 6, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1991. Kennard, J., and Baxter, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 351243, Michael J. Virga, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order directing the Registrar to pay Pinney's litigation expenses under Code of Civil Procedure section 1028.5 is reversed. The judgment granting Pinney's petition for a writ of mandate is affirmed. Each party shall bear his/its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant registrar sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent contractor's petition for writ of administrative mandamus, and directed appellant to set aside its decision revoking respondent's contractor's license for failing to produce business records after written demand was made under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7111.


OVERVIEW: Respondent contractor failed to produce business records after a written demand pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7111 by appellant registrar. An administrative law judge found that respondent had failed to comply with appellant's written request for the production of business records, and ordered his license revoked. The trial court granted respondent's petition for writ of administrative mandamus, and ordered appellant to set aside its decision. The court affirmed the writ of mandamus and held that appellant could not impose discipline for respondent's failure to produce records which had neither been subpoenaed nor seized under a warrant. The court determined that § 7111 could not constitutionally be construed to authorize a warrantless inspection or production of records without a subpoena and opportunity for judicial review before discipline was imposed. The court concluded that respondent's business could not fairly be classified as closely regulated, and the exception to the warrant requirement applicable to pervasively regulated industries did not apply. Nothing in the statute's inspection program provided a constitutionally adequate substitute for a warrant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting respondent contractor's petition for a writ of administrative mandamus because appellant registrar could not revoke respondent's contractor's license for respondent's failure to produce records which had not been subpoenaed nor seized under a warrant.

People v. Campbell


No. D011347


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


230 Cal. App. 3d 1432; 281 Cal. Rptr. 870; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 581; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4250; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6607



June 4, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III, IV, V and VI.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 21, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR103976, Charles R. Hayes and Bernard E. Revak, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Campbell's count 11 conviction for possession of a concealed firearm by an ex-felon is reversed. The superior court is directed to amend Campbell's abstract of judgment to strike the punishment for the count 11 conviction and Werner's abstract of judgment to strike reference to imposition and stay of the firearm enhancement allegation in reference to count 8 (robbery of Walls) and to correctly reflect the true finding of section 12022, subdivision (a) with its stay for count 3 (robbery of Castillejos) instead of a section 12022.5 stay, and to forward both to the proper authorities. As so modified, the judgments in all other respects are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted and sentenced them for various robberies under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and for false imprisonment under Cal. Penal Code §§ 236-237.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted by jury verdict of multiple robberies under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and false imprisonment under Cal. Penal Code §§ 236-237, arising from a crime spree in two counties. Defendants challenged their convictions and asserted that the trial court lacked jurisdiction pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 786. The court affirmed the convictions of defendants and held that the trial court had territorial jurisdiction. The court held that there was sufficient evidence of preliminary arrangements in one county, which led to the commission of offenses in another county, to confer territorial jurisdiction. The court also found that defendants had waived their right to raise issues concerning vicinage, because they failed to make an appropriate timely objection to the jury panel in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendants of various robberies and false imprisonment because the preliminary arrangements made by defendants in the county were overt acts in furtherance of the conspiracy to commit robbery in another county and therefore were sufficient to confer territorial jurisdiction to the trial court.

Utah Property & Casualty Ins. etc. Assn. v. United Services Auto. Assn.


No. C008392


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


230 Cal. App. 3d 1010; 281 Cal. Rptr. 917; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 545; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4126; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6377



May 29, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 506781, Anthony DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state insurance guaranty association appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) holding that the time limitation for insolvency coverage in the Uninsured Motorist Act, Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2, was incorporated by law into provisions of a policy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state insurance guaranty association appealed from a trial court's judgment that a statutory time limitation for insolvency coverage was incorporated by law into the uninsured motorist provisions of an insurance policy. Appellant, which stood in the shoes of an insolvent insurer in a wrongful-death action, contended that respondent, the decedent's insurer, was liable for the balance of a stipulated judgment because an insolvency protection clause in the policy placed no restrictions on when an insurer had to become insolvent. The appellate court agreed and reversed the trial court's judgment. The court held respondent was not entitled to deny coverage under an insolvency protection clause by asserting a statutory time limit that respondent had failed to write into its policy.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed because respondent insurer was not entitled to deny coverage under an insolvency protection clause by asserting a statutory time limit that respondent failed to write into its policy.

City of Woodlake v. Logan


No. F014631


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


230 Cal. App. 3d 1058; 282 Cal. Rptr. 27; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 538; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4075; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6540



May 29, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 140220, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to grant Woodlake's motion for summary judgment and to enter judgment thereon. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which concluded that voter approval requirements for local general tax enactments as applied to appellant's utility tax were constitutional, and granted summary judgment to respondent county auditor in appellant's action to have respondent release withheld property taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city filed a petition for writ of mandate and complaint for declaratory relief seeking release of property taxes withheld by respondent county auditor and challenging the constitutionality of statutes requiring voter approval for local general tax enactments. The trial court found the statutory initiatives constitutional and granted summary judgment to respondent. Appellant sought review. The court held that the voter approval requirement for local general tax enactments adopted by the electorate as part of statewide statutory initiative Proposition 62, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 53723 & 53724, was unconstitutional. The court held that the enforcement provisions, Cal. Gov't Code § 53728, reducing a local government's share of property taxes on a dollar-for-dollar basis for general taxes enacted and collected without voter approval, were unconstitutional. The court held that the unconstitutional portions were severable. The court concluded that appellant's utility tax was validly enacted and that respondent was improperly withholding property taxes to offset the utility tax. The court reversed the judgment with directions to grant appellant's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to respondent auditor and directed the trial court to grant appellant city's motion for summary judgment because voter approval requirements were unconstitutional, appellant's general utility tax was validly enacted, and respondent was improperly withholding property taxes to offset the utility tax.

Mauro B. v. Superior Court


No. G010218


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


230 Cal. App. 3d 949; 281 Cal. Rptr. 507; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 546; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4187; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6336



May 28, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. AD56043, AD56498 and A153698, Robert J. Polis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Relief by extraordinary writ is inappropriate in this case. The alternative writ is dissolved, and the petition for writ of mandate or prohibition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner putative father challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied his request to declare a parent and child relationship, and allowed real parties in interest, adopting parents, to adopt the minor child. Petitioner filed a motion to vacate. An alternative writ was issued and set for hearing. Real parties in interest filed a demurrer and return to the writ on grounds that the petition was not timely.


OVERVIEW: The superior court issued a judgment under the Uniform Parentage Act, Cal. Civ. Code, § 7000 et seq., denying petitioner putative father's request to declare a parent and child relationship existed between himself and a minor child, and allowing real parties in interest, adopting parents, to adopt the minor without petitioner's consent. Petitioner filed the motion to vacate the judgment. An alternative writ was issued and set for hearing. Real parties in interest filed a demurrer and return to the writ claiming that the petition was not timely filed. The court found that the lower court's decision was an appealable judgment, that petitioner failed to file a notice of appeal within the time provided by law, and that the petition contained no factual allegations explaining why an appeal was not taken or justified the delay in filing it. The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, and held that relief by extraordinary writ was inappropriate, that the alternative writ was dissolved, and the petition for writ of mandate or prohibition was dismissed.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the superior court was affirmed in favor of real parties in interest, adopting parents. Relief by extraordinary writ was improper, because petitioner putative father failed to file a notice of appeal within the time provided by law, and the petition contained no factual allegations explaining why an appeal was not taken or justified the delay in filing it. The alternative writ was dissolved, and the petition for writ was dismissed.

Meeks v. Superior Court


No. C009744.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


230 Cal. App. 3d 698; 281 Cal. Rptr. 796; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 529; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4055; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6255



May 24, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted August 15, 1991 (S021708)
Submission of additional briefing, otherwise required by rule 29.3, California Rules of Court, is deferred pending further order of the court.
Appointment of counsel is deferred pending disposition of a lead case. (See rule 29.2(c), California Rules of Court.
Review Dismissed October 31, 1991 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 3d Dist. ( rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 98350, William R. Ridgeway, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted the state's motion for reciprocal discovery, pursuant to the Crime Victims Justice Reform Act, Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, in defendant's criminal trial for robbery. Defendant filed a writ of prohibition to restrain enforcement of the state's motion and a stay was granted.


OVERVIEW: In criminal trial for robbery, defendant filed a writ of prohibition to restrain enforcement of the state's motion for reciprocal discovery, pursuant to the Crime Victims Justice Reform Act, Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The trial court granted the state's motion for discovery, and a stay was granted to review the writ. The court analyzed the statutory scheme, which provided sanctions if parties did not follow the reciprocal discovery in criminal cases, Cal. Pen. Code §§ 1054.1-1054.7. The court determined that the enforcement of the discovery provisions applied equally to both defendant and the state, and that neither party was required to disclose work-product or privileged information. The court further determined that the provisions were constitutional and did not violate defendant's right to be free from self-incrimination, pursuant to U.S. Const. amend V. The court denied the writ of prohibition and vacated the stay.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's writ of prohibition to restrain enforcement of the state's motion for reciprocal discovery pursuant to the Crime Victims Justice Reform Act. The court held that reciprocal discovery in criminal cases applied equally to both defendant and the state and that neither party was required to disclose work-product or privileged information. The court held that the provisions were constitutional and vacated the stay.

In re Marriage of Dade


No. A043619


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


230 Cal. App. 3d 621; 281 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 543; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3908; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6221



May 24, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 24, 1991, Reported at 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 696, and respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 178570, Norman Spellberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The August 12, 1988, and December 20, 1988, orders of the trial court are reversed. Appellant shall recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife, whose appeal was pursued by the county, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), granting respondent ex-husband's motion to recuse the district attorney from representing appellant in child support modification proceedings, and finding that Cal. Civ. Code § 4720(e) and Cal. Civ. Code § 4721(e) were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney assumed the representation of appellant ex-wife, whose appeal was pursued by the county, in an action to obtain an increase in child support. The trial court granted respondent ex-husband's motion to recuse the district attorney and determined that Cal. Civ. Code § 4720(e) and Cal. Civ. Code § 4721(e), were unconstitutional. The trial court granted the district attorney's motion to vacate appellant's abandonment of her appeal. On appeal, the court reversed the orders of the trial court. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it vacated appellant's abandonment of her appeal because the appeal raised issues of continuing public interest. The trial court erred in recusing the district attorney because Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11475.1 authorized the district attorney to take appropriate action to ensure that children received adequate support, regardless of the financial circumstances of the parents. The trial court erred when it found § 4720(e) and § 4721(e) unconstitutional. Section 4720(e) and § 4721(e) did not raise equal protection concerns because both parents were treated equally with regard to consideration of the income of the current spouse.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court recusing the district attorney from representing appellant ex-wife. The district attorney was authorized to assist appellant in obtaining child support. The court reversed the trial court's determination of the unconstitutionality of provisions of the Agnos Child Support Standards Act of 1984, because they did not violate equal protection.

 

1274 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Golden Cheese Co. v. Voss


No. E007837


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


230 Cal. App. 3d 727; 281 Cal. Rptr. 602; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 553; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3930; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6252



May 24, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 200374, Ronald T. Deissler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order sustaining demurrer without leave to amend and dismissing action, entered as a judgment on January 12, 1990, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff cheese company challenged the order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendant director of food and agriculture department without leave to amend and dismissed an action over a taking of plaintiff's business.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff cheese company brought suit against defendant director of food and agriculture department over an order setting the minimum price to be paid by cheese manufacturers to producers for class 4b market milk. Plaintiff alleged that the order constituted a taking by inverse condemnation, contending that even lawful orders can give rise to inverse condemnation claims. The lower court sustained defendant's demurrer and dismissed the action. The court affirmed the judgment. Plaintiff did not state a cause of action for inverse condemnation, and the valid use of police power did not support a cause of action in inverse condemnation for lost business. Plaintiff had no reasonable expectations to a particular price level and such interference with property rights arose from a public program to promote the common good. Inverse condemnation damages were only recoverable for a regulatory taking. The court further held that in light of a recently decided companion case, the principles of res judicata and collateral estoppel under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1908 and 1911 also required the court to hold that the order complained of could not be the basis for an inverse condemnation action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order that sustained the demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed action, and held that a valid use of police power did not support a cause of action in inverse condemnation for lost business and interference with property rights from a public program that adjusted the benefits and burdens of economic life to promote the common good did not constitute a taking requiring government compensation.

Golden Cheese Co. v. Voss


No. E007838


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


230 Cal. App. 3d 547; 281 Cal. Rptr. 587; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 548; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4062; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6184



May 23, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of plaintiffs and appellants for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 200372, Ronald T. Deissler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying appellants' petition for writ of mandate is affirmed in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, cheese manufacturers, challenged denial of their petition for a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California). They claimed that an amendment to milk marketing plans under Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 61801 et seq., issued by respondent, Department of Food and Agriculture, and supported by intervenor, dairymen's association, was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, cheese manufacturers, brought an action against respondent, Department of Food and Agriculture (department), challenging the validity of its amendment to Cal. Food & Agric. § 61801 et seq., on milk marketing plans. The department's actions were supported by intervenor, dairymen's association. The trial court declined to grant appellants' petition for a writ of mandate, and appellants claimed that the trial court erred in upholding the amendments. The court affirmed in all respects, holding that the department had no responsibility under § 62062(b) to set prices that guaranteed an adequate supply of cheese or other products to the consumer. It held that the department's interpretation of its duty was correct and constitutional and that the department did not act in an arbitrary and capricious manner in establishing the minimum price formula or in making a cheese specific formula. The court held that it had no basis to set aside the department's discretionary decision. While § 62062(c) required that the department consider that the prices it established had a sound economic relationship to each other, appellants did not demonstrate that the department failed to do so.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in all respects the trial court's judgment denying the petition for a writ of mandate of appellants, cheese manufacturers, to compel the trial court to overturn the milk marketing plan amendment promulgated by respondent, Department of Food and Agriculture, and supported by intervenor, dairymen's association. The court found that respondent's actions were neither arbitrary nor unsupported by the evidence.

Del Monte v. Deukmejian


No. B040513.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


1 Cal. App. 4th 1213; 281 Cal. Rptr. 380; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 507; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6075; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3874



May 23, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Review Granted August 22, 1991 (S021805)
Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequest History Table. Previous cite 230 Cal. App. 3d 462.) (See 1 Cal. 4th 1009 (DeMonte v. Wilson)).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA001096, Barnet M. Cooperman, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of May 23, 1991, previously Reported at 230 Cal. App. 3d 462.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant veterans sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petition for peremptory writ of mandate/complaint for declaratory relief of Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 980, which conditioned eligibility for statutory benefits upon appellants' California residency status at the time of entry into active military duty. Respondents, governor and associated parties, contended that statute was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Under Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 980, veterans residing in the state qualified for a number of state benefits for themselves and their families, but eligibility was conditioned on the veteran having been either a native of or a bona fide resident of the state at the time he or she entered active military service. Appellant veterans petitioned for peremptory writ of mandate/complaint for declaratory relief, which challenged the constitutionality of such provisions. Respondents, governor and associated parties, contended that the statute was constitutional. The trial court denied appellants' petition, and they sought review. The court held that the state's program was not rationally related to the state's goal of compensation for disruption of a veteran's life by call to duty while living in the state. The court held that the state's plan of preferring established California resident veterans over otherwise bona fide resident veterans was violative of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court with instructions to remand.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which denied appellant veterans' petition for writ of mandate/complaint for declaratory relief in favor of respondents, governor and associated parties. The court held that the state's program of favoring veterans who were residents of the state at time of service over other resident veterans was not rationally related to the goal and that the preference violated the equal protection clause.

 

1277 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Bjornestad v. Hulse


No. C007526


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 1568; 281 Cal. Rptr. 548; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 504; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4003; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6225



May 22, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 19, 1991, and the opinion was modified to read as printed, Reported at 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 712. Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Placer County, No. 86397, James D. Garbolino, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Under AB 3548, Sierra does not constitute a special limited purpose district whose activities disproportionately affect landowners as a group. Consequently, the five present Sierra director positions -- all of which were elected pursuant to the pre-AB 3548 landowner-only voting scheme -- will have to be the subject of a special election. In this regard, subdivision (e) of section 30700.6 is unconstitutional but severable from the rest of AB 3548. AB 3548's enfranchisement of Sierra's nonresident landowners does not unconstitutionally dilute the votes of Sierra's residents in contravention of the equal protection guaranties of the California and federal Constitutions. Nor does the electoral scheme of AB 3548 violate article I, section 22, or article II, sections 1 through 3, of the California Constitution, by enfranchising nonresident landowners and the legal representatives [***2] of corporations or estates which own land in Sierra. We remand this matter to the trial court to structure a special election for the Sierra board of directors in accord with the dictates of AB 3548. We also remand the matter to the trial court for it to determine the issues regarding attorney fees at the trial and the appellate levels as delineated in this opinion in section 5 of the Discussion. Plaintiffs are awarded their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water district sought review by the California Supreme Court, which remanded to the appeals court with directions to vacate an opinion which held, in favor of respondent residents, that a landowner-only voting provision of Cal. Water Code § 30700.6 violated the equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and to reconsider the matter in light of Assembly Bill No. 3548, which amended § 30700.6.


OVERVIEW: The California Supreme Court remanded to the court, in favor of respondent residents, holding that a landowner-only voting provision of Cal. Water Code § 30700.6 violated the equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and to reconsider the matter in light of Assembly Bill No. 3548, which amended § 30700.6. The court held that Cal. Water Code § 30700.6 6 was unconstitutional but severable from the rest of Assembly Bill No. 3548, and remanded to the trial court to structure a special election of the directors of appellant water district, who had been elected by landowners only, and to determine the issues regarding attorney fees, which were awarded to respondent residents. The court held that respondents had a vital stake in the district and could not be denied a vote. The court held that appellant did not constitute a special limited purpose district whose activities disproportionately affected landowners as a group. The court held that enfranchisement of nonresident landowners and legal representatives did not unconstitutionally dilute the votes of respondents.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court with instructions to structure a special election of appellant water district's directors and held that the portion of the California Water Code which violated respondent residents' equal protection guarantees was severable from its amendment. The court held that allowing nonresident landowners to vote did not unconstitutionally dilute the votes of respondents. Respondents were awarded attorney fees.

 

1278 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Homestead Sav. v. Darmiento


No. B041160


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


230 Cal. App. 3d 424; 281 Cal. Rptr. 367; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 512; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3862; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6094



May 22, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C640545, Stephen M. Lachs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Defendant Darmiento is to recover his costs on appeal from plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bank appealed entry of summary judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of defendant bona fide purchaser for value. Plaintiff claimed that its due process rights under federal and state law had been violated by the conclusive presumption that a trustee at a trust sale of land had complied with statutory notice requirements established under Cal. Civ. Code § 2924.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff bank brought suit against defendant, bona fide purchaser for value, to set aside a nonjudicial foreclosure sale of property which plaintiff had an interest in under a junior deed of trust. Defendant filed a motion for summary judgment that was granted by the trial court because defendant was entitled to the conclusive presumption in Cal. Civ. Code § 2924, that the trustee had complied with statutory notice requirements. Plaintiff appealed, claiming that its due process rights under federal and state law had been violated. The court affirmed. The court held that the effect of § 2924 was to deny a party judicial process to challenge whether the trustee, a private party, had complied with the notice requirements against a bona fide purchaser. This was not state action, so plaintiff was not deprived of due process of law. Even if there were state action, § 2924 was rationally related to its primary goal, the protection of the trustor. Because defendant was entitled to the benefit of the conclusive presumption, plaintiff's lien was extinguished by the trustee's deed, and defendant's title was free and clear of the junior interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entry of summary judgment in favor of defendant, bona fide purchaser for value. It held that plaintiff bank's due process rights under federal and state law had not been violated by the conclusive presumption of the trustee's compliance with the statutory notice requirements at the trustee's sale of land to plaintiff. The court held that the trustee's compliance with the notice requirements was not state action.

 

1279 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Silver


No. B045046


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


230 Cal. App. 3d 389; 281 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 494; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3801; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5993



May 21, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR24214, Robert C. Bradley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which convicted him of possession for sale and sale of methylenedioxymethamphetamine, an analog of methamphetamine, a controlled substance, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11378, 11379, and 11401.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession for sale and sale of methylenedioxymethamphetamine (MDMA), an analog of methamphetamine, a controlled substance, under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11378, 11379, and 11401. Defendant appealed his conviction and argued that § 11401 was unconstitutionally vague, as it defined a controlled substance analog as having a chemical structure "substantially similar" to a controlled substance, or having an effect on the central nervous system "substantially similar" to a controlled substance. The court affirmed the conviction and held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court stated that the term "substantially similar" had no scientific meaning but that the California Constitution did not require scientific or mathematical precision. Section 11401 was reasonably certain so that persons of common intelligence need not guess at its meaning. The court held that the jury was qualified to determine the credibility of expert witnesses' testimony. There had been sufficient evidence for the jury to believe that MDMA was substantially similar to methamphetamine to convict defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the lower court that convicted defendant of possession for sale and sale of methylenedioxymethamphetamine, an analog of methamphetamine, a controlled substance, because the statute under which defendant was convicted was not unconstitutionally vague.

People v. McKelvey


No. B050637


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


230 Cal. App. 3d 399; 281 Cal. Rptr. 359; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 497; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3733; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6000



May 21, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR24684, Robert J. Soares, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) for neglect of a dependent adult pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 368.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of neglect of a dependent adult pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 368, for neglect of his mother, a multiple sclerosis victim. The trial court found overwhelming evidence that defendant was responsible for his mother's care and allowed her to suffer and become injured. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that even though the first clause of § 368 was not very clear, the defendant came under the second clause of the statute. The court found that the trial court decided that defendant was responsible for his mother's care, so the court did not need to review the statute further. The court held that sufficient evidence supported the trial court's finding that defendant's conduct was a gross or culpable departure from the ordinary standard of due care and was criminally negligent within § 368. The court found that the defendant was responsible for the deplorable conditions in which emergency medical persons discovered the defendant's mother.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in the conviction of defendant for neglect of a dependent adult. The court held that defendant was responsible for his mother's care and was responsible for the deplorable conditions in which emergency medical persons discovered defendant's mother, which led to her suffering, injury, and death.
