Abbett Elec. Corp. v. Cal. Fed. Sav. & Loan Ass'n


No. A049514


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


230 Cal. App. 3d 355; 281 Cal. Rptr. 362; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 527; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3729; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5964



May 21, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 781840, Maxine M. Chesney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed to the extent that it includes attorney's fees in the mechanic's lien, and the case is remanded for modification of the judgment in light of this opinion. Appellants are entitled to their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two lenders who recorded deeds of trust against the subject property, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which included attorney fees in an amount awarded respondent electric contractor for its judgment resulting from an underlying breach of contract action.


OVERVIEW: Respondent electric contractor filed a breach of contract action against a property owner for work performed but not paid for. Judgment was awarded to respondent, along with its costs and attorney fees to be included in a mechanic's lien in its favor. Appellants, two lenders who recorded deeds of trust against the subject property, intervened in respondent's foreclosure action against the property owner, alleging that respondent's mechanic's lien should not include attorney fees in its judgment amount. The court reversed the lower court's judgment to the extent that it included attorney fees in the mechanic's lien. The court held that attorney fees were available to a prevailing litigant only under a contractual agreement or under some statutory authorization. Respondent, absent some contractual or statutory provision providing for such, was not entitled to have attorney fees included within its judgment against the property owner.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment to the extent that it included attorney fees in the mechanic's lien and remanded the case for modification because attorney fees were not authorized by any contract or statute with regard to the dispute between appellants, two lenders who recorded deeds of trust against the subject property, and respondent electric contractor.

Morris v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. D011704


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


231 Cal. App. 3d 151; 282 Cal. Rptr. 130; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 619; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4497; 91 Daily Journal DAR 6924



May 17, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Ordered Published June 11, 1991.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 11, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 607419, Phillip D. Sharp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate seeking an order commanding defendant Department of Motor Vehicles to annul its order under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 13352(a)(5), 23170 that revoked plaintiff's driver's license for three years for having had his third drunk driving conviction within seven years.


OVERVIEW: Following an order by defendant Department of Motor Vehicles, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code §§ 13352 (a)(5) and 23170, which revoked plaintiff driver's license for three years for having had his third drunk driving conviction with seven years, plaintiff petitioned for a writ of mandate to annul such order on the basis that one of his prior drunk driving convictions was unconstitutional. The writ was denied and plaintiff appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The court applied the principle of stare decisis and held that a writ of mandate did not lie against defendant for the purpose of determining the validity of the prior conviction, because the petition for a writ of mandate to review defendant's administrative act in performing its mandatory function of revoking the license was not a proceeding in which additional sanctions could have been imposed against plaintiff by reason of a prior conviction, and defendant had neither the duty nor the power to pass on the validity of the prior conviction. Plaintiff was not precluded from challenging the constitutional validity of his prior conviction, however, mandamus against respondent was not the proper procedure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and held that a writ of mandate did not lie against defendant Department of Motor Vehicles for the purpose of determining the validity of a prior conviction, because defendant had neither the duty nor the power to pass on the validity of the prior conviction.

 

1283 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Alexander D. v. State Bd. of Dental Examiners


No. A048932


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


231 Cal. App. 3d 92; 282 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 621; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4481; 91 Daily Journal DAR 7037



May 13, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] ORDERED PUBLISHED June 12, 1991.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 12, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 914637, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dentist challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that denied his motion for preliminary injunction to enjoin respondent Board of Dental Examiners from ordering him to submit to a psychiatric examination.


OVERVIEW: Appellant dentist challenged the decision of the trial court that denied his motion for preliminary injunction to enjoin respondent board of dental examiners from ordering him to submit to a psychiatric examination pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 820. Appellant contended that his due process rights were violated by respondent's order, and that § 820 was unconstitutional in that it included no reasonable cause standards or guidelines. The court determined that appellant's due process rights were not violated, as the purpose of the exam was investigatory. The court further held that investigation was confidential, appellant's risk of deprivation was remote as any discipline would be the result of a separate adjudicatory hearing accompanied by due process protections, and appellant had full opportunity for rebuttal at the hearing. Additionally the court determined that respondent's interest would be severely impacted by requiring full due process protections for every preliminary investigation. The court also held that the respondent's petition established reasonable cause to order the evaluation. Accordingly the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order compelling was affirmed because the exam was investigatory and confidential and respondent was statutorily permitted to advance its investigatory function without compromising appellant's right to due process.

 

1284 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Casella v. City of Morgan Hill


No. H006972


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


230 Cal. App. 3d 43; 280 Cal. Rptr. 876; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 473; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3531; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5577



May 10, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review was denied July 17, 1991. Baxter, J., did not participate therein. Panelli, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 689717, Peter G. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mobilehome park owners sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which sustained appellee city's demurrer because appellants failed to state facts sufficient to state a cause of action for taking without just compensation, and dismissed appellants' complaint on their election not to amend.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, owners of a mobilehome park, challenged appellee city's mobilehome rent control ordinance, claiming that its lack of a "vacancy decontrol" provision, which allowed rents to be returned to market levels after vacancy, effected a taking of their property without just compensation. The trial court sustained appellee's demurrer, and dismissed the complaint on appellants' election not to amend. On review, the court found that appellants did not make a facial challenge to the rent control ordinance; they challenged only the effect of its lack of a "vacancy decontrol" provision. They did not allege, however, that the ordinance failed to provide them a fair and reasonable return on their investment, or denied them economically viable use of their land. Thus, even accepting the allegations of appellants' complaint as true, the court concluded that the pleadings stated facts insufficient to constitute a taking. Accordingly, the trial court's dismissal order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order of dismissal was affirmed because appellant mobilehome park owners could not make out a claim for a taking without just compensation where they failed to allege that appellee city's ordinance denied them a fair and reasonable return on their investment, or that the ordinance denied them economically viable use of their land.

 

1285 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Carlson


No. F013310


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 1330; 280 Cal. Rptr. 840; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 446; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3358; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5431



May 6, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 30, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 22, 1991. Panelli, J., and Kennard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 37023, Edward P. Moffat II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a provision of a child custody and visitation order issued by the Superior Court, Madera County (California) prohibiting appellant or respondent from moving the minor children of their marriage outside of California without the prior written consent of the other party or further court order.


OVERVIEW: The lower court dissolved appellant and respondent's marriage. The parties then went to trial on the issue of child custody and visitation. The lower court found that it was in the best interests of the children to remain in the state to maintain contact with respondent and entered an order prohibiting each parent from taking the children out of the state without the prior consent of the other or court approval. Appellant challenged, contending that the lower court's restraining order was an abuse of discretion, and the impairment of respondent's visitation and his consequent loss of contact with the children were insufficient as a matter of law to justify the order. The court held that the lower court did not err when it considered the effect appellant's contemplated move would have on respondent's exercise of visitation, and respondent, as a noncustodial parent, did not have an affirmative burden to prove the move would be detrimental to the children in order to obtain the restraining order. Thus, the lower court did not abuse its discretion by imposing the restriction because there was no evidence demonstrating it was in the best interest of the children to move.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's child custody and visitation order, finding that the lower court did not abuse its discretion by imposing the restriction because respondent's loss of the practical ability to exercise visitation was relevant in deciding whether appellant should be restrained from moving the children to a different geographical area.

Baca v. Minier


No. F014359


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 1253; 280 Cal. Rptr. 810; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 421; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3253; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5265



May 2, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of all of part I but the first sentence of the first paragraph thereof and part V.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified May 24, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, Nos. 39805 and 8315, Edward P. Moffat II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the trial court with directions to set a court date for a forfeiture hearing as required by law and in accordance with the findings and conclusions expressed herein, with notice to all parties. Appellant is awarded costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Madera County (California) that denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate, which sought to quash respondent district attorney's non-judicial forfeiture pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11470 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Officers of the sheriff's department executed a search warrant at the home of appellant. The officers seized personal property valued at $ 64,500. Appellant was personally served with notice of the seizure that same day. Over ten days later, appellant filed a verified claim of interest in the superior court. The next month, officers executed another search warrant, seized additional property valued at $ 25,000, and posted notice at appellant's residence. Appellant then signed a claim pursuant to § 11488.5 to an interest in the property seized in the second search. Appellant also filed a motion to return the property. Subsequently, respondent district attorney filed declarations of forfeiture pursuant to § 11488.4(j) as to both lots of personal property. The matter was continued pending the criminal case against appellant. The following year, appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate, seeking to quash respondent's nonjudicial forfeiture. The superior court denied the petition, and appellant sought review. On review, the court reversed and remanded because the notice of forfeiture respondent provided to appellant was out of date and facially inadequate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied appellant individual's petition for a writ of mandate, and remanded with directions to set a court date for a forfeiture hearing, because the notice of forfeiture respondent district attorney gave appellant was inadequate on its face and thus improper.

 

1287 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Djekich


No. D010824


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


229 Cal. App. 3d 1213; 280 Cal. Rptr. 824; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 439; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3241; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5232



May 2, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County, No. H54935, David W. Ryan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owner challenged his conviction by Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County (California) for 10 misdemeanor counts of violating San Diego County, Cal., Zoning Ordinance § 1006(a). Defendant was placed on probation and ordered to pay a $ 1000 fine for each count. Defendant appealed to the appellate department of the superior court, which affirmed. The case was certified to the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant property owner converted a property zoned for single family residences into four separate units. He was charged with violating San Diego County, Cal., Zoning Ordinance § 4310(a), maintaining dwelling units in excess of one dwelling unit per lot and San Diego County, Cal., Zoning Ordinance § 1006(a), using property that was zoned single-family residential for another use. Under a plea bargain, he pled no contest to 10 counts of violating § 1006(a) and was placed on probation, conditioned upon payment of a fine. He appealed. His conviction was affirmed and the case was certified. The court ordered transfer and considered defendant's untimely notice of appeal as a habeas corpus petition. The court denied the petition and held that defendant's conviction and sentence for multiple offenses under § 1006(a) did not violate San Diego County, Cal., Zoning Ordinance § 654. The legislature statutorily divided continuous criminal conduct under § 1006(a) into a series of separately punishable acts. Defendant was properly convicted of and punished for multiple separate and distinct offenses under the same penal provision without violating § 654's multiple punishment prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court treated the appeal as a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, because defendant property owner's notice of appeal was untimely. The court denied the petition. Defendant was properly convicted of and punished for multiple offenses under the zoning ordinance without violating the penal code's multiple punishment prohibition. The legislature expressly declared that each day of continuous criminal conduct could be separately punished.

 

1288 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Jason V.


No. F014131


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 1168; 280 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 424; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3199; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5183



May 1, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, Nos. 69493 and 69494, Leonard M. McGillivray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders directing appellants Earl and Erline Applegate to reimburse Kern County for support of the minors are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant legal guardians of minors sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Kern County (California) directing appellants to reimburse respondent county in the amount of $ 100 per month per minor as contribution to the minors' support. As part of its dispositional orders, the juvenile court directed that the minors remain placed in foster care outside appellants' home. Appellants objected to the order of reimbursement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant legal guardians of minors sought review of an order directing appellants to reimburse respondent county in the amount of $ 100 per month per minor as contributions to the minors' support where, as part of its dispositional orders, the juvenile court directed that the minors remain placed in foster care outside appellants' home. The order for reimbursement was imposed pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 903. Appellants objected to the order of reimbursement. Appellants were identified in the matter as the maternal aunt of the minors and her husband. Appellants argued that "parent" in § 903 meant mother or father and did not include legal guardians and thus the order requiring reimbursement to respondent was without statutory basis. Respondent urged that the term "parent," in § 903, included legal guardians. The court reversed the reimbursement order on grounds that, based on principles of statutory construction, § 903 did not impose liability on appellants for costs of supporting the minors ordered to out-of-home placement as they were not the parents of the minors, the estate of a parent, or the estate of the minors.


OUTCOME: The order directing appellant legal guardians to reimburse respondent county for support of minors, ordered to out-of-home placement by the juvenile court, was reversed as the provisions of the welfare and institutions code imposing liability for costs of supporting the minors ordered to out-of-home placement did not extend to appellant legal guardians as they were not the parents of minors, the estate of a parent, or the estate of the minors.

1290 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Library One


No. B053254


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


229 Cal. App. 3d 973; 280 Cal. Rptr. 400; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 401; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3144; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4952



April 30, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CRA 27541, Larry S. Knupp, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal (order sustaining demurrer) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that sustained a demurrer in favor of defendants with respect to a criminal complaint alleging violations of the law in connection with an adult bookstore in violation of L.A. County, Cal., Code, and state law.


OVERVIEW: The state appealed the judgment entered after the trial court sustained a demurrer in favor of the defendants with respect to a criminal complaint alleging numerous violations of the L.A. County, Cal., Code in connection with the operation of an adult bookstore and picture arcade. The trial court dismissed the action because it found that the code unconstitutionally failed to state a specified brief period within which a business license or conditional use permit had to be granted or denied. On appeal, the court stated that if the statute was not severable, then the void part tainted the remainder and the whole became a nullity. The code failed to provide any specified time within which an application for a business license or a conditional use permit must be granted or denied, it contained the same vice as a statute delegating excessive administrative discretion, and constituted an invalid prior restraint. The judgment was affirmed because various provisions of the code regulating the maintenance of picture arcades define potential offenders in terms of licensee, they could not be severed from the invalid licensing provisions and similarly were unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The demurrer in favor of defendants was affirmed with respect to a criminal complaint alleging violations of the law in connection with the operation of an adult bookstore because the county code was invalid as a prior restraint.

 

1291 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Arcadia Unified School Dist. v. Department of Education


No. C008489


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


235 Cal. App. 3d 1840; 280 Cal. Rptr. 463; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 397; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4939



April 29, 1991 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted July 11, 1991 (S021288). (See 2 Cal. 4th 251).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 361147, Michael J. Virga, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of April 29, 1991, previously Reported at 229 Cal. App. 3d 913.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school districts sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which agreed with respondent department of education's decision that appellants were not entitled to charge pupils fees for transportation to schools because Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5 violated the free school guarantee in Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5.


OVERVIEW: Respondent department of education issued an advisory informing all school districts in the state, including appellant school districts, to stop charging school bus transportation fees because Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5, which authorized the charges, was unconstitutional. Appellants and respondent joined in a submission of the controversy to the trial court pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1138, and intervenor taxpayer was allowed to intervene. The trial court held that § 39807.5 violated the "free school" guarantee in Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5. The court reversed. Transportation fees, which were not charged for transporting indigent or handicapped pupils, did not violate the free school guarantee of the constitution because transportation was not an integral fundamental part of education, was not a necessary element of any school's activity, and did not impose any cost upon the free education of the poor and handicapped. Access to school bus transportation was not a fundamental interest protected by Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). The imposition of transportation fees was rationally based on a need to conserve resources, and did not result in a suspect classification.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of respondent department of education was reversed. The court held that the statute that authorized appellant school districts to charge pupils fees for transportation to schools did not violate the "free school" or the equal protection guarantees in the California constitution.

 

1292 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sepulveda


No. E007847


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


229 Cal. App. 3d 868; 280 Cal. Rptr. 778; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 425; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3012; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4886



April 26, 1991 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 1, 1991 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Consts., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3617.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR50411, Duke D. Rouse, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part with directions; affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) which convicted defendant of four counts of food stamp and welfare fraud in violation of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980(c)(2) and nine counts of perjury in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 118 and sentenced defendant to a term of five years and four months.


OVERVIEW: In 1987, defendant did not report income while receiving Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) and was convicted on two counts under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980(c)(2), one for receiving AFDC and one for receiving food stamps, by means of false statements. Defendant was also convicted of six counts of perjury under Cal. Penal Code § 118. In 1988, defendant was convicted of two counts under § 10980(c)(2) and three counts of perjury under § 118, for failing to report that her two children were no longer living with her. On appeal, the court held that only one conviction under § 10980(c)(2) was proper for each time period, 1987 and 1988, because the improper receipt of AFDC and food stamps arose from one act of fraud that was committed in 1987 and again in 1988. The judgment was reversed and remanded to resentence defendant for one conviction for the 1987 events and one for the 1988 events. The court also held that § 10980(c)(2) was not unconstitutionally vague. The separate perjury convictions under § 118 were not precluded by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10980, and a conviction for perjury that was committed during each time period was allowed under Cal. Penal Code § 654.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order convicting defendant of four counts of welfare fraud, two for receiving welfare funds and two for receiving food stamps, because the receipt of welfare funds and food stamps arose from the same act of fraud, in two separate years. The case was remanded to resentence defendant on only two counts. The perjury convictions were affirmed because a separate conviction was proper for each act of perjury.

 

1293 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Scott


No. B020765


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


229 Cal. App. 3d 707; 280 Cal. Rptr. 274; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 388; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2955; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4752



April 24, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 1, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A752444, Henry P. Nelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which convicted him of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 189, with firearm enhancements, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(a), and special circumstance enhancements, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 189, with firearm enhancements, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(a), and special circumstance enhancements, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3), and was sentenced to state prison for life without possibility of parole. On appeal, appellant argued that his murder conviction should be reversed because he was tried for felony murder but was charged with murder with malice, that the jury was not given proper instructions, and that there was insufficient evidence of the intent to kill requisite to the special circumstances allegations. The court affirmed appellant's convictions and held that the information charging appellant with murder was sufficient to charge either a violation of felony murder or murder with malice; thus, the fact that appellant was charged with murder with malice but convicted of the felony murder was without consequence. There was also no error in the murder instructions or in not instructing the jury on unanimity, and the evidence was substantial that appellant intended to aid in the killing of the four victims.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting appellant of murder, with firearm enhancements and special circumstance enhancements, was affirmed. The information charging appellant with murder was sufficient to charge either a violation of felony murder or murder with malice, so the fact that appellant was charged with murder with malice and was convicted of felony murder was without consequence.

 

1294 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Yates v. Law Offices of Samuel Shore


No. B045427


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


229 Cal. App. 3d 583; 280 Cal. Rptr. 316; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 375; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2873; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4632



April 22, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 1, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 703854, John Zebrowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorneys challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that appellants had improperly calculated a contingent fee due appellants under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146 from a wrongful death judgment in favor of respondents, widow and children, and that appellants had improperly charged respondents hourly fees of another attorney with whom appellants associated for an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorneys represented respondents, widow and children, in a wrongful death action. Appellants divided the judgment into the number of shares represented by the number of respondents and applied the percentages mandated by Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146 in order to calculate their fees. Respondents filed suit and the trial court found that appellants had incorrectly calculated the fees. Appellants sought review and the court affirmed. The court noted that § 6146 was ambiguous. The court determined, however, that the most reasonable construction of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act of 1973 was to require that the percentage contingent fee limitations of § 6146 be applied to the wrongful death recovery as a whole, instead of to individualized shares. The court also determined that appellants' attempt to bill respondents hourly fees of an attorney with whom appellants associated for an appeal was improper because § 6146 did not permit appellants to charge additional fees, whether for themselves or for associated counsel, for the appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of respondents, widow and children, was affirmed because the most reasonable construction of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act of 1973 was that statutory contingent fee limitations applied to the recovery in the wrongful death action as a whole rather than to individualized shares, and appellants were not permitted to charge additional fees for an appeal, either for themselves or for associated counsel.

California Auto. Assigned Risk Plan v. Gillespie


No. B050033.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


23 Cal. App. 4th 975; 280 Cal. Rptr. 217; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 366; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4473



April 18, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review Granted July 24, 1991 (S016766)
[Reprinted as modified to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table, Previous cites 229 Cal. App. 3d 514 and 235 Cal. App. 3d 1825 and 7 Cal. App. 4th 266 and 13 Cal. App. 4th 212 and 18 Cal. App. 4th 1220.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C755745, Miriam A. Vogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to dismiss the action. Appellant is awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state insurance commissioner challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted the petition of respondents, an organization of insurance companies and one of its members, for a writ of administrative mandamus directing appellant to grant respondents' request to increase "assigned risk" policy rates by 112 percent.


OVERVIEW: After appellant state insurance commission had rejected a request by respondents, an organization of insurance companies and one of its members, for a 112 percent increase in insurance rates for "assigned risk" policies, respondents obtained from the superior court a writ of administrative mandamus directing the increase in rates. The court of appeal reversed because appellant could justifiably conclude both that assigned risk insurers were not entitled to a fair rate of return on assigned risk policies considered separately from the insurers' rate of return on all policy lines and that the rate increase request should be tabled pending administrative hearings on methods for determining a fair rate of return.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal vacated the mandamus directing a 112 percent rate increase for assigned risk policies. Appellant state insurance commissioner's decision to withhold the increase was justified both by her rejection of the premise that insurers were entitled to a fair rate of return on assigned risk policies as an insular category and by her desire to wait for the outcome of pending administrative hearings before deciding on any increase.

County of Fresno v. Lehman


No. F013637


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 340; 280 Cal. Rptr. 310; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 351; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2757; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4466



April 17, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 388123-2, Gene M. Gomes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents are awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which sustained respondent state's demurrer and dismissed appellant's petition for a writ of mandate in its action for a declaratory judgment holding that it was entitled to reimbursement for attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county filed a declaratory action against respondent state, contending that it was entitled to reimbursement of attorney fees it had expended to litigate issues in a test claim pertaining to a state-mandated program under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. Respondent contended that the statute of limitations barred the action, and the trial court sustained respondent's demurrer. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed, finding that, although the trial court erred when it found the statute of limitations barred the action, the demurrer was properly sustained because, under Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 did not constitute a state-mandated program because it did not effectuate fundamental public policies embodied in constitutional or statutory provisions. Therefore, respondent was not required to reimburse appellant for legal expenses in test case litigation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial's court decision to sustain respondent state's demurrer because the statute at issue did not constitute a state-mandated program that necessitated the reimbursement of legal fees expended by appellant county in a test case.

Northwest Fin. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. D011121


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


229 Cal. App. 3d 198; 280 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 339; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2598; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4190



April 10, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 11, 1991. Kennard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 611092, Herbert B. Hoffman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained a demurrer in favor of respondents, State Board of Equalization and San Diego County, in appellant's action alleging that the real property "acquisition value" taxation system established by Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(a), violated equal protection and the right to travel.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation filed a complaint against respondents, State Board of Equalization and San Diego County, alleging that Proposition 13's "acquisition value" taxation system, Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(a), which set tax rates for real property based on the acquisition price, not the current value, was unconstitutional because it resulted in great disparities in tax liability between property owners with comparable properties. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer to the complaint, and the court affirmed. The court found that equal protection was not violated because the state had placed taxpayers into a rationally-based classification system in order to effectuate the legitimate state purpose of protecting real property owners from increased taxes based on increased property values in an inflationary real estate market. The court also found that the right to travel was not implicated because travel would be inhibited to no greater extent by the new system, which established a more fixed and stable measure of taxation, than that imposed by the old system of unconstrained taxation based on current values.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the demurrer in favor of respondents, State Board of Equalization and San Diego County, finding that the "acquisition value" tax system did not violate equal protection because the state had a rationally-based classification system related to a legitimate state purpose. The court found that the right to travel was not implicated by the law because travel was inhibited no more by the new system than it was by the old.

 

1298 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Bradley


No. B051955


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


7 Cal. App. 4th 236; 279 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 343; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4123



April 9, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review Granted July 25, 1991 (S021118).
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 229 Cal. App. 3d 80 and 235 Cal. App. 3d 1765.)] (See 4 Cal. 4th 389)



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged, the peremptory writ of mandamus is denied and the temporary stay, issued on August 9, 1990, is hereby dissolved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners state and city elected officials brought an original mandamus proceeding before the court, to invalidate and enjoin the implementation and enforcement provision of a campaign reform measure against respondents city officials.


OVERVIEW: The voters of the City of Los Angeles adopted a campaign reform measure. Petitioners state and city elected officials brought an original mandamus action pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 56(a) against respondent city officials to enjoin the implementation and enforcement of the measure. Petitioner contended that Cal. Gov't Code § 85300 prohibited the use of public money for the financing of local as well as state political campaigns, making the measure adopted by the voters invalid and unenforceable. The court held that petitioners were not entitled to relief in mandamus because the decision of the voters of Los Angeles to utilize tax dollars as part their political campaign and electoral ethics reform legislation was purely a municipal affair and not matter of statewide concern. Local government did not lack the power to legislate with respect to local municipal affairs of a municipality. The measure did not impact Cal. Gov't Code § 85300 and was valid and enforceable against the attack of petitioners.


OUTCOME: Petitioners state and city elected officials were not entitled to mandamus relief because the measure challenged concerned a purely municipal affair. The challenged measure was not impacted by a state statute which addressed the expenditure of public funds for the purpose of seeking elective office because the citizens of other jurisdictions could claim no burden from, nor any legitimate interest in such legislation.

 

1299 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Faubel v. Superior Court


No. F014648


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


235 Cal. App. 3d 256; 279 Cal. Rptr. 658; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 322; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3893



April 2, 1991 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified April 8, 1991; Review Granted June 20, 1991 (S020534)
Review dism. January 30, 1992 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 5th Dist. ( rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 29115, John P. Moran, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of April 2, 1991, previously Reported at 228 Cal. App. 3d 1515.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) that rejected defendant's constitutional challenges to Proposition 115 and denied his motion to set aside an information.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendant's constitutional challenges to Proposition 115. The court held that federal law did not require a state to conduct a preliminary hearing to determine probable cause prior to prosecution by information. The court held that the provision of Cal. Penal Code § 872(b), which permitted the use of hearsay evidence at a preliminary hearing, did not deny defendant due process. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 872(b) did not violate U.S. Const. amend. VI because the confrontation clause is a trial right that did not apply to preliminary hearings. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 872(b) did not deny equal protection because it did not deprive the defendant of any rights he would have otherwise been afforded had the grand jury-indictment procedure been used. The court that Cal. Penal Code § 872 and Cal. Evid. Code § 1203.1 did not violate the state constitution. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 866 did not restrict the magistrate's independent exercise of discretion or violate Cal. Const. art I, § 28(b). The court also found that defendant's offer of proof pursuant to Cal. Penal Code 866(a) was deficient.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court order because Proposition 115 and the statutes enacted pursuant to Proposition 115 did not deny defendant of his constitutional rights of due process, the right to confront witnesses, or equal protection and the trial court did not err in refusing defendant's request to call the victim as a witness during his preliminary hearing because defendant's offer of proof was deficient.

 

1300 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ivey


No. A048455


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


228 Cal. App. 3d 1423; 279 Cal. Rptr. 554; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 311; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2298; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2398; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3750



March 29, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 9845-C, Conrad L. Cox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court erred in denying defendant's suppression motion. The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which convicted appellant of possession of cocaine, and denied appellant's motion to suppress evidence of the contraband.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession of cocaine. The trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress evidence of the contraband. Appellant contended on appeal that his arrest was based on a recalled arrest warrant and was unlawful, and the evidence seized as a consequence should have been suppressed. The state conceded that appellant's U.S. Const. amend. IV rights were violated by the arrest on the recalled warrant. The court disagreed with the state's argument that exclusion was not a proper remedy because the police officers acted in good faith on the recalled warrant. The court held that the police officers could not rely on the good faith exception in validating an arrest if the arrest warrant was negligently delayed in being recalled, and the warrant was erroneously shown as still outstanding. The court found that the trial court erred in denying appellant's motion to suppress evidence. The court reversed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction of possession of cocaine because the trial court erred in denying appellant's motion to suppress evidence of the contraband, as police officers could not rely on the good faith exception in validating an arrest if the arrest warrant was negligently delayed in being recalled, and the warrant was erroneously shown as still outstanding.

 

1301 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Clark v. Burleigh


No. H007141


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


7 Cal. App. 4th 162; 279 Cal. Rptr. 333; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 301; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3677



March 28, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified April 25, 1991. Review Granted July 11, 1991 (S020854)
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 228 Cal. App. 3d 1369 and 235 Cal. App. 3d 215.)] (See 4 Cal. 4th 474)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 90060, John F. Herlihy, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is reversed. Costs to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) in a declaratory judgment action that was filed by respondent registrar of voters to determine whether Cal. Elec. Code § 10012.1 was constitutional and whether appellant's candidate statement was prohibited by § 10012.1.


OVERVIEW: Respondent registrar of voters filed a declaratory judgment action to determine whether Cal. Elec. Code § 10012.1 was constitutional. Section 10012.1 restricted information that could be contained in a candidate's statement that was included in a voter's information pamphlet published by the county. It prohibited a statement from referring to other candidates for judicial office. The trial court found that § 10012.1 was constitutional and that the statement of appellant candidate was prohibited by § 10012.1. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court because § 10012.1 violated U.S. Const. amend. I. The court held that the voter's information pamphlet was a limited public forum that restricted the candidates' statements based upon content. The court recognized that § 10012.1 furthered a compelling interest of preserving judicial integrity and impartiality by prohibiting judicial candidates from engaging in political promises or representations but found that § 10012.1 violated the First Amendment because it was not narrowly tailored to achieve that interest and it was a prior restraint of speech.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in the declaratory action judgment filed by respondent registrar of voters against appellant candidate because Cal. Elec. Code § 10012.1 violated the First Amendment of the United States Constitution. It was overbroad and not narrowly drawn to effectuate a compelling interest and it operated as a prior restraint of speech.

 

1302 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


State Bd. of Control v. Superior Court


No. C009882.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


235 Cal. App. 3d 185; 279 Cal. Rptr. 413; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 292; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3621



March 26, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 23, 1991 (S020391)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 364707, Fred K. Morrison, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of March 26, 1991, previously Reported at 228 Cal. App. 3d 185.



DISPOSITION: Writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State Board of Control challenged, by writ of review, an order from respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which directed it to disclose records containing the details of earthquake claims settlements under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 997-997.6.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest newspaper opposed issuance of the writ of review on the ground that petitioner State Board of Control's claim does not rise to the level of jurisdictional error, the standard of review of a writ of review. The court held that there was no case that repudiated the doctrine that an express constitutional grant of appellate jurisdiction could not be impaired by statute. This action under the Public Records Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 6250 et seq., for an order compelling release of records was a "cause" and thereby within Cal. Const. art VI, § 10 because it was the kind of case over which the superior courts had by statute been given original jurisdiction in Cal. Gov't Code § 6258. Because the first sentence of Cal. Gov't Code § 6259(c) forbade an appeal in all public records act cases, it was void as in conflict with the grant of appellate jurisdiction in Cal. Const. art VI, § 11. Because the writ of review was thereby precluded as the means of review of the order in this case, the petition for writ of review was denied. The court noted that petitioner had an appellate remedy by appeal.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute, which prohibited review by appeal of cases arising under the Public Records Act, conflicted with and was invalidated by the express grant of appellate jurisdiction in the California Constitution. Thus, the petitioner's writ of review was denied, but the court noted that petitioner State Board of Control had an appellate remedy by appeal.

 

1303 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City of Rancho Cucamonga v. Mackzum


No. E007876


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


228 Cal. App. 3d 929; 279 Cal. Rptr. 220; 46 Cal. Rptr. 2d 448; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 275; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2072; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3330



March 20, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted June 27, 1991 (S020738)
Review Dismissed and Reporter of Decisions is directed to publish the opn. in the Official Reports. (See subdivision (c) of rule 978, California Rules of Court, Reported at 1991 Cal. LEXIS 4226.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 198595, John H. Barnard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The respondents are to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants cities challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found that Cal. Gov't. Code, § 26912, and former Cal. Rev. & Tax Code, § 2237; now Cal. Rev. & Tax Code, §§ 93, 95 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that Cal. Gov't. Code § 26912, and former Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 2237; now Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 93, 95 was constitutional. The subject legislation was enacted to implement Proposition 13, now Cal. Const., art. XIII A. Appellant challenged the judgment. The court stated that it would uphold the trial court's ruling if it were correct under any applicable theory of law. The court then noted that the subject legislation had not violated the tax situs rule, the principle of uniformity in tax collection, or the statewide concern doctrine. Moreover, Cal. Const., art. XIII A prevailed over the preexisting taxing power of charter cities. Thus, although Proposition 13 may have modified the California Constitution, it did not abrogate or repeal its home rule provision. As the subject legislation was consistent with the purpose of Proposition 13, it was constitutional, even if it conflicted with Cal. Const., art. XI, § 5 as it existed before Proposition 13 was enacted. Additionally, the subject legislation did not result in a denial of equal protection of the laws. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that found the subject legislation constitutional as it did not violated the tax situs rule, principle of uniformity in tax collection, or statewide concern doctrine, and it had not abrogated or repealed home rule provision of the state constitution.

 

1304 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Adoption of Kay C.


No. H006084


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


228 Cal. App. 3d 741; 278 Cal. Rptr. 907; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 247; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1937; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3195



March 18, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied June 20, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. A7808, George W. Bonney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We hold section 227b does not violate the due process or equal protection clauses of the federal and state Constitutions. Accordingly, the order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California) that granted respondent adopters' petition, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 227b, to set aside their adoption of appellant.


OVERVIEW: In 1981, appellant minor, then six years old, was placed in a foster home due to the inability of her natural parents to care for her. Two years later, her natural parents relinquished their parental rights. The following year, an adoption agency placed appellant in respondent adopters' home, even though a doctor who had examined appellant recommended against adoption. Respondents were not given the doctor's report. Nor were respondents informed of the social worker's doubts the adoption would be successful. The next year, appellant began therapy, and the decree of adoption was issued. Following the adoption, appellant's disruptive conduct became more pronounced. Eventually, appellant was hospitalized, and respondents filed a petition to set aside the adoption, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 227b. The trial court granted the petition. In response, appellant sought review, contending 227b was unconstitutional. On review, the court affirmed because § 227b did not violate the due process or equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted respondent adopters' petition to set aside the adoption because the applicable statutory provision did not violate the due process or equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitutions, and the evidence supported setting aside the adoption was in the best interests of appellant minor.
