Drexel v. Mann


No. H007204


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


13 Cal. App. 4th 170; 278 Cal. Rptr. 887; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 229; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1899; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3121



March 15, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review Granted July 11, 1991 (S020662)
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table Previous cite 228 Cal. App. 3d 630 and 235 Cal. App. 3d 95 and 7 Cal. App. 4th 98.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 699410, James W. Ware, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a candidate for election to the office of district attorney, challenged a peremptory writ of mandate from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) directing the registrar of voters, pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10013.5, to delete material from appellant's candidate statement prepared for the official voter's pamphlet.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a candidate for election to the office of district attorney, produced material for his candidate's statement in the official voter's pamphlet. Pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10013.5, appellant's statements were challenged by a voter as false, misleading, or inconsistent with statutory requirements. The trial court agreed and issued a mandate directing the deletion of certain material from appellant's statement. Appellant sought review, contending that Cal. Elec. Code § 10013.5 constituted an unconstitutional prior restraint on campaign speech in a limited public forum. The court disagreed. In sum, the court concluded that the official voter's pamphlet was a unique vehicle of expression, that the state had a compelling interest in prohibiting false or misleading material, and that the Legislature had struck a constitutionally permissible balance in section 10013.5. Moreover, the court was persuaded that the statute was valid because the person who challenged the candidate statement must satisfy a clear and convincing standard.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the California statute authorizing the deletion of certain false or misleading material from a candidate's statement in the official voter's pamphlet was constitutional because the state had struck an appropriate balance between its interest in prohibiting false or misleading statements and the candidate's free speech.

 

1306 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Fuss v. Superior Court


No. E008621


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


228 Cal. App. 3d 556; 279 Cal. Rptr. 46; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 257; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2021; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3068



March 14, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. RCV053486, Roberta A. McPeters, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to require respondent Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) to dismiss the complaint brought by real party in interest to establish his paternity of an unborn child with whom petitioner was then pregnant, on the basis that the putative father lacked standing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to require respondent trial court to dismiss the complaint brought by real party in interest to establish paternity of the child with whom petitioner was then pregnant. The issue before the court was whether a putative father, who brought an action under Cal. Civ. Code § 7006(c) and (f), lost his right to maintain the action when another man became the child's presumed natural father by marrying the mother before the birth of the child. The court denied the writ of mandate. The court stated that the evidence established a rebuttable presumption under Cal. Civ. Code § 7004(a)(1) that petitioner's husband was the child's natural father; thus, the trial court erred in finding that he was not. The court held, however, that the trial court rendered the correct decision as § 7006(c) permitted the action to determine paternity prior to the child's birth, that the action was brought before the marriage when the child had no presumed father, and the presumption of paternity in petitioner's husband did not destroy real party's right to continue the action. The presumption could then be rebutted by clear and convincing evidence, Cal. Civ. Code § 7004(b).


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of mandate because the presumption of paternity in petitioner's husband did not destroy the right of the real party in interest to continue his action to determine paternity of petitioner's unborn child.

 

1307 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



County of Lake v. Smith


No. A046835


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


228 Cal. App. 3d 214; 278 Cal. Rptr. 809; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 238; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1763; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2849; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5613



March 8, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 3, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. 17806, Robert L. Crone, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties disputed how the low-water mark of a lake should be determined for purposes of establishing the common boundary line separating privately owned littoral real property from the area of state-owned real property. Appellants, county and trustee, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Lake County (California), which held that the low-water mark of a lake was the level of zero as surveyed by respondent state lands commission.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, county and trustee, sought review of a judgment holding that the low-water mark of a lake was the level of zero as surveyed by respondent state lands commission. On appeal, the issue was how the low-water mark of a lake should be determined for purposes of establishing the common boundary line separating privately owned littoral real property from the area of state owned real property. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the low-water mark which formed the boundary was not some extraordinary lowest level reached during a severe drought, but was instead a regular or ordinary level which property owners could use for practical purposes in setting a realistic boundary. The court held that shorezone ownership should be determined by taking into account the realities of the present condition of the littoral area and should not be based upon some past low level of a lake that was irrelevant to current conditions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the low-water mark that formed the boundary was not an extraordinary lowest level but was a regular or ordinary level that property owners could rely upon for a practical boundary.

 

1308 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Neary v. Regents of University of California


No. A044284


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


7 Cal. App. 4th 73; 278 Cal. Rptr. 773; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 241; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1655; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2746



March 5, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review Granted May 23, 1991 (S020515), Reported at 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2240.
[Reprinted as modified to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 228 Cal. App. 3d 131 and 235 Cal. App. 3d 46.)] (See 3 Cal. 4th 273)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 5258390, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The joint application for reversal is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, university board of regents and individual veterinarians, and respondent cattle rancher jointly applied for a stipulated reversal of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which found that appellants libeled respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent cattle rancher filed a complaint which alleged that state and federal agencies killed certain heifers and calves when the agencies improperly applied insecticides that poisoned pregnant heifers. The parties agreed to have the matter investigated by appellants, university board of regents and individual veterinarians. Appellants published a report that falsely stated that respondent's cattle were killed as a result of his incompetence, not by insecticide poisoning. After a trial, the jury found that appellants libeled respondent and awarded compensatory damages. The parties, upon reaching a settlement, jointly requested that the court reverse the trial court's judgment and remand for dismissal with prejudice. Appellants advised the court that stipulated reversal was a condition precedent to their pending settlement. Denying the joint application for reversal, the court determined that reversal of a judgment not thought to be legally erroneous to effectuate a settlement was adjudicative and would undermine the integrity of the entire judicial system.


OUTCOME: The court denied the parties' joint motion for a reversal, holding that reversal of a judgment not thought to be legally erroneous would be an improper means of settlement.

 

1309 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Christopher H.


No. B048484


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


227 Cal. App. 3d 1567; 278 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 175; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1569; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2477



February 28, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 22, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. J027879 and J027880, Alvin B. Calof, Temporary Judge. +


+ Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The orders are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of orders from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which suppressed evidence seized by private security guards and dismissed petitions against respondent minors brought under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, which alleged respondents were guilty of carrying concealed firearms without a license and without written permission of a parent or guardian.


OVERVIEW: Respondent minors were stopped by two shopping mall security guards while on an access road at the outer border of the shopping mall. Respondents were both carrying concealed handguns, which were seized by the security guards. The state brought petitions against respondents under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, which alleged respondents were guilty of carrying concealed firearms without a license and without written permission of a parent or guardian. The trial court granted respondents' motion to suppress the handguns because they were seized in violation of their U.S. Const. amend. IV rights. The trial court then dismissed the petitions. On appeal, the court reversed the orders, holding that there was no state action because the private security officers who stopped and searched respondents were not state officials, they obtained no aid from state officials in stopping and searching respondents, and their conduct was not otherwise chargeable to the state. The court further held that because no state action was involved, the Fourth Amendment proscriptions did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders suppressing the seizure of handguns and dismissing petitions against respondent juveniles, holding that the Fourth Amendment protection against unlawful searches and seizures applied to governmental action only, but not to the actions of private security personnel. Therefore, the trial court improperly suppressed the handguns seized from respondents.

 

1310 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Catelli


No. C004884


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 1434; 278 Cal. Rptr. 452; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 157; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1531; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2423



February 26, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III through XVI.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 80850, Steven H. Rodda, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed in part with directions to the trial court to (1) reduce the sodomy conviction (count XLVIII) to attempted sodomy, for a consecutive term of three years, and strike the ancillary section 12022.3 use enhancement; (2) strike the great bodily injury enhancement ancillary to count XXXVIII; (3) prepare a new abstract of judgment setting forth defendant's sentence, 546 years in state prison; and (4) forward a certified copy of the abstract to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted him of 60 sex crimes committed against three victims in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 261(2), 286(c), 288(b), 288a(c), 289(a) and sentenced him to 557 years in state prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of sex crimes and appealed his conviction. Defendant argued that admission of the tape recording and the undercover officer's testimony violated his U.S. Const. amend. VI rights because the prosecutor had an informant arrange his jailhouse conversation with the officer. The court reversed in part and reduced defendant's sentence to 546 years and directed the trial court to reduce the sodomy conviction to attempted sodomy and strike the enhancement for great bodily injury and the use enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.3. The court affirmed in part and held that the officer clearly undertook a course of conduct deliberately designed to elicit incriminating statements from defendant and therefore, violated defendant's U.S. Const. amend. VI, right to counsel. However, the court held that even if the jury had not heard the evidence concerning defendant's effort to dissuade the victims from testifying, its verdicts would have been the same. Further, the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction under Cal. Penal Code §§ 288a and 288(b) and the court held that the term "sexual organ" included a man's scrotum as well as his penis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part, reduced defendant's sodomy conviction to attempted sodomy and reduced his sentence to 546 years. The court affirmed in all other regards and held that, while the undercover officer violated defendant's Sixth Amendment right to counsel and the trial court erred by admitting the incriminating statements, the jury would have reached the same decision even if it had not heard this evidence.

 

1311 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City of Santee v. Superior Court


No. D012941


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


228 Cal. App. 3d 713; 279 Cal. Rptr. 22; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 253; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2015; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3266



February 25, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 19, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 600824, Judith Lynnette Haller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ issue directing the superior court to vacate its order denying the City's motion for summary judgment and summary adjudication of specific issues, to enter an order granting the City summary adjudication that administrative mandamus constituted J.M.R.'s exclusive remedy for challenging the propriety of the conditions imposed by the City and its failure to pursue that judicial remedy estops it from challenging indirectly those conditions in its declaratory relief action, and to conduct further proceedings so as to address the merits of the City's remaining contentions supporting its request for summary judgment. The stay issued by this court on September 14, 1990, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city filed a petition for a preemptory writ of mandate regarding an order of respondent trial court, which denied its motion for summary judgment in an action for fraud, breach of mandatory duty, and civil conspiracy brought by the applicant against petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city granted a permit to the applicant to construct three new buildings, on the condition that he construct a divider wall and a driveway. The applicant did not appeal the decision, but built the buildings without constructing the wall or driveway. The applicant then brought an action against petitioner for fraud, breach of mandatory duty, and civil conspiracy. Respondent trial court denied petitioner's motion for summary judgment. Petitioner then sought a preemptory writ of mandate to require respondent to vacate its order denying summary judgment. The appellate court granted the writ. In so ruling, the appellate court held that the applicant's exclusive remedy for judicial review of the permit was a proceeding for administrative mandamus, under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. 1094.5. Therefore, the applicant was estopped from challenging the validity of the permit's conditions, regardless of the context in which the challenge was made. As the trial court did not address the merits of applicant's fraud claim, the appellate court also directed the trial court to make a ruling on remand.


OUTCOME: The appellate court granted petitioner city's petition for a preemptory writ of mandate and directed respondent trial court to vacate its order denying summary judgment to petitioner in an action by the applicant for fraud, breach of mandatory duty, and civil conspiracy, as administrative mandamus constituted the applicant's exclusive remedy for judicial review of a permit issued by petitioner, and remanded for decision on the fraud claim.

 

1312 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Wandick


No. F012670


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 918; 278 Cal. Rptr. 274; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 138; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1356; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2115



February 19, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 38171, Robert T. Baca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The convictions on counts one, two and three are affirmed and the cause is remanded for resentencing only on those counts in accord with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of the manufacture and possession of rock cocaine, maintaining a place for the sale of controlled substances, possessing a firearm with an obliterated identification number, and committing a felony while armed with a firearm. He sought review of his convictions by the Superior Court of Kern County (California) arguing improper jury instruction, the language of a statute, and its definition of "armed."


OVERVIEW: Defendant, convicted of drug and weapons charges, challenged only the weapons convictions. On appeal, he contended that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that possession of a weapon with a defaced serial number created the presumption that the possessor defaced the number; that the sentence could be enhanced pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022(a) rather than § 12022(b), and that it could find defendant was armed within the meaning of §12022(a) if the weapon was available. The court held that even if the jury found defendant possessed the revolver, that fact alone would not support a finding that defendant obliterated the numbers, and that although there was a misstatement of the enhancement statute, defendant had nevertheless been notified that a four-year enhancement would be sought, because he was personally armed with a gun, and the enhancement was the same regardless of the finding that he was in violation of § 12022(b). The court determined that the jury instruction definition of "armed" was proper. The court affirmed defendant's convictions on three counts and reversed on the count for possessing a firearm with an obliterated identification number.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for manufacture and possession of rock cocaine and for maintaining a place for the sale of controlled substances. The court reversed and remanded on the count for possessing a firearm with an obliterated identification number.

 

1313 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Green


No. A047437


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


227 Cal. App. 3d 692; 278 Cal. Rptr. 140; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 220; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1118



February 11, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 133241, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 133241 (California), of participation in a criminal street gang, claiming the statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to several crimes, including participation in a criminal street gang under Cal. Penal Code § 186.22(a). Appellant challenged only his § 186.22(a) conviction, asserting the statute was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The court affirmed the conviction, The court held that § 186.22(a), which prohibited active participation in criminal street gangs, was not unconstitutionally vague because its provisions were sufficiently definite and provided sufficiently definite guidelines for the police to prevent arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement. Further, the court held that appellant's guilty plea did not admit anything which would cause him to lack standing to make his attack on the basic provisions of § 186.22(a), which, if found vague, would invalidate the entire statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, holding that appellant's guilty plea did not preclude his attack on the basic provisions of the California Penal Code which made participation in a criminal street gang a crime. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because its provisions were sufficiently definite and provided sufficiently definite guidelines for the police.

 

1314 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Chem. Specialties Mfrs. Ass'n v. Deukmejian


No. A048489


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


227 Cal. App. 3d 663; 278 Cal. Rptr. 128; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 215; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1121



February 8, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 913903, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the petition for writ of mandamus is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to grant the petition. Appellants are awarded costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied a petition for writ of mandamus seeking to halt implementation of Proposition 105, the "Public's Right to Know Act," as violative of the single-subject rule.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner voters challenged a judgment that denied a writ of mandamus seeking to halt implementation of Proposition 105, the "Public's Right to Know Act," as violative of the single-subject rule. The court determined that the provisions of Proposition 105 were neither functionally related to one another nor reasonably germane to one another or the objects of the enactment. The initiative required unrelated state agencies to determine which household products were toxic, establish a rating system for insurance policies, and ascertain which health care facilities had the most serious operating violations. The purported reason for all the provisions--providing the public with accurate information in advertising--was so overbroad that an unlimited array of provisions could be considered germane. The government did not meet its burden of demonstrating unreasonable delay or prejudice. The court determined that the public's interest in adherence to the single-subject requirement should not have been subordinated to its interest in economical government and, therefore, a denial of the petition on the basis of laches was erroneous. The order was reversed and remanded to grant the writ.


OUTCOME: The order denying the petition for writ of mandamus was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court with directions to grant the petition. Petitioner citizens were awarded costs on appeal. The initiatives violated the constitutional mandate of having only one subject in each piece of legislation, an interest that was more important than economical government.

Hartman v. Kenyon


No. H006600


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 413; 277 Cal. Rptr. 765; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 81; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 905; 91 Daily Journal DAR 1442



January 30, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 24, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 111594, Samuel S. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant election recall proponent sought a review of the decision by the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent City Clerk of the City of Santa Cruz to certify that three recall petitions he submitted contained the requisite number of signatures of eligible registered voters.


OVERVIEW: Appellant election recall proponent sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent City Clerk of the City of Santa Cruz to certify that three recall petitions he submitted contained the requisite number of signatures of eligible registered voters. Appellant sought leave of respondent to file amended circulator declarations after respondent had rejected approximately 120 signatures on each petition for irregularities in the declarations. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the writ of mandate and found that respondent had no statutory authority to accept amended circulator declarations. The court viewed the legislature's decision to eliminate supplemental petitions as an indication of their intent to bring finality, certainty, and uniformity to the recall process and as such the court was not allowed to enlarge the scope of a procedural statute when the provisions were clear. The court concluded that respondent did not err in following the mandate of Cal. Elec. Code § 45 and disqualified the signatures of signers who listed a different residence address on the petition from the address they listed on their voter registration affidavit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision denying appellant election recall proponent's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent City Clerk of the City of Santa Cruz to certify that three petitions he submitted contained the requires number of signatures. The court concluded that respondent had no statutory authority to accept amended circulator declarations and that other signatures had been properly rejected.

People v. Methey


Nos. D010422, D012007


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


227 Cal. App. 3d 349; 277 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 83; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 962; 91 Daily Journal DAR 1393



January 30, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 13, 1991, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1991. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Baxter, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRE99755, Robert E. May, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed; petition for writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his motion to suppress evidence. Defendant claimed that the evidence suppressed in prior filings was res judicata, or that defendant's counsel was ineffective for not raising res judicata and collateral estoppel at hearing on the motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of a controlled substance. He filed a motion to suppress, which was granted. The case was dismissed and later refiled. Defendant filed the same motion to suppress, which was granted. The case was dismissed, refiled, and defendant's motion to suppress was denied. Defendant entered into a plea agreement and appealed, claiming that it was error to deny his motion to suppress in the third filing and that his counsel was ineffective because he did not raise res judicata and collateral estoppel in the third case. The court affirmed the conviction and found that given the probable cause to arrest which existed at the time defendant produced the marijuana, a search incident to arrest in order to determine whether further contraband existed was appropriate. The court also found that defendant's counsel did not prejudice his rights by failing to assert res judicata and collateral estoppel arguments that had been rejected by the court.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction for possession of a controlled substance was affirmed and the petition for writ denied, because the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence, and his counsel did not prejudice his rights by failing to assert res judicata and collateral estoppel arguments previously rejected by the court.

Liquid Chemical Corp. v. Department of Health Services


No. F012645


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 1682; 279 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 84



January 30, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I of the Discussion.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kings County, No. 46880, Peter M. Schultz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is affirmed. Respondent is awarded its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, fertilizer manufacturer and its president, challenged an order from the Superior Court of Kings County (California), which denied appellants' petition for writ of mandate and affirmed a decision in an enforcement action taken by respondent Department of Health Services under the California Hazardous Waste Control Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25100 et seq.


OVERVIEW: An administrative law judge ruled in favor of respondent Department of Health Services in its enforcement action for violations of the California Hazardous Waste Control Act (HWCA), Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25100 et seq. by appellants, fertilizer manufacturer and its president. Appellants were fined for the violations and their petition for writ of mandate was denied by the trial court. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that a lapse of federal authorization to operate a state program did not destroy respondent's authority to continue operating its state mandated program under 51 Fed. Reg. 4128 (1986). The court held that the materials appellants' used were hazardous waste under the former Cal. Code Regs. tit. 22, §§ 30(9) and (11), and there was no exemption as raw materials under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25143.2(e). The court held that appellant president was personally liable for the fine under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25187 because he ran the day-to-day operations of the company, and he was aware that he faced personal liability for failure to comply with the HWCA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellants, fertilizer manufacturer and its president, petition for writ of mandate, and a decision in respondent Department of Health Services enforcement action under the California Hazardous Waste Control Act. Respondent had jurisdiction to enforce and operate its state program. The materials appellants used were hazardous waste, and appellant president was personally liable for a fine.

Mounts v. Uyeda


No. A047385


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


227 Cal. App. 3d 111; 277 Cal. Rptr. 730; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 100; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 855; 91 Daily Journal DAR 1335



January 30, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 337769, John G. Schwartz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sought review of summary judgment from Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) granted in favor of respondent insured in appellant's personal injury action. Appellant contended the trial court erred in ruling that her action was barred by the statute of limitations pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured party sought review from the superior court of summary judgment that was granted in favor of respondent insured in appellant's personal injury action. Appellant contended the trial court erred in ruling that her action was barred by the statute of limitations pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340. The court held that the conduct attributed to respondent was independent of and unrelated to the operation of his vehicle and as a consequence, appellant's injury did not arise out of the operation of a motor vehicle, and her complaint was timely filed. The court also found that there was no interaction between Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 351 and the commerce clause, and hence no conflict between them. The court concluded that absent a conflict, there was no constitutional infirmity to the application of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 35. The summary judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment granted in favor of respondent insured in appellant respondent's personal injury action was reversed. The court held that the conduct attributed to respondent was independent of and unrelated to the operation of his vehicle and as a consequence, appellant's injury did not arise out of the operation of a motor vehicle, and her complaint was timely filed.

Foster v. Bd. of Medical Quality Assurance


No. C007162


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


227 Cal. App. 3d 1606; 278 Cal. Rptr. 117; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 150; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2229



January 24, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 359104, Michael J. Virga, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which declined to issue a writ of mandate to compel respondent medical review board to set aside its decision to suspend his medical license for 90 days for unprofessional conduct under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 2234, 2261. The trial court found that respondent's decision was properly reached and supported by the evidence.


OVERVIEW: The hospital that employed appellant physician adopted a bylaw requiring its staff members to maintain medical malpractice. Appellant then created his own insurance policy in a nonexistent insurance company, signed a verification form stating that it was valid, and continued to practice at the hospital. Respondent medical review board suspended appellant's medical license for 90 days for unprofessional conduct under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 2234, 2261. The lower court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent to set aside its decision. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that appellant's conduct fell within §§ 2234, 2261 because his intentional dishonesty demonstrated a fundamental lack of moral character that was incompatible with the honesty required to properly maintain the doctor-patient relationship. The court held that the falsified documents were indirectly related to appellant's medical practice and indirectly threatened public welfare. The court also held that §§ 2234, 2261 were not vague as applied because appellant was not penalized for mere failure to comply with the bylaw, but in order to protect the public from further dishonesty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant physician's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent medical review board to set aside its decision suspending appellant's medical license for 90 days for unprofessional conduct. The court held that respondent's decision was supported by the evidence where appellant intentionally falsified documents to indicate that he had liability insurance, as required by a hospital bylaw.

In re Ge M.


No. F013360


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


226 Cal. App. 3d 1519; 277 Cal. Rptr. 554; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 55; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 758; 91 Daily Journal DAR 1006



January 22, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 66213, Phillip A. Silva, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), that adjudged him a ward of the juvenile court based on a finding that he had violated Cal. Penal Code § 422, by making terrorist threats and Cal. Penal Code § 417(a)(2), by drawing or exhibiting a firearm in a rude, angry, or threatening manner.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was involved in a confrontation with the victim. Defendant drew a gun and threatened the victim. Defendant was adjudged a ward of the juvenile court because he had violated Cal. Penal Code § 422, for making terrorist threats, and Cal. Penal Code § 417(a)(2), for drawing or exhibiting firearm in a rude, angry, or threatening manner. Defendant appealed and contented that § 422 was inapplicable because it was not shown in the trial court that his conduct was motivated by membership in a street gang. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment that defendant violated § 422 and § 417(a)(2) because neither the plain meaning nor the legislative history of § 422 supported defendant's conclusion that street gang membership was an element of the offense. The court found defendant had too narrowly construed the legislative purpose when he contended that because § 422 was amended and reenacted as part of the bill that enacted the California Street Terrorism Enforcement and Prevention Act, Cal. Penal Code §186.20 to § 186.27, which criminalized participation in street gangs and increased punishment for offenses committed with a gang, that § 422 required gang membership.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that adjudicated defendant a ward of the juvenile court for his terrorist threats and drawing or exhibiting a firearm in a threatening manner because the language of a terrorist threat statute was clear that there was no requirement of gang membership and the statute made no reference to criminal gang activity.

 

1321 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Marblehead v. City of San Clemente


No. G007605


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


226 Cal. App. 3d 1504; 277 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 59; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 736; 91 Daily Journal DAR 1005



January 22, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 31, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. X-551182, John C. Woolley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the lower court is affirmed. Respondent shall recover its reasonable costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant intervenor citizens group sought review of the grant of a writ of mandate to plaintiff land development company by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which invalidated a voters-adopted initiative that the court held violated the taking and equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. V and amend. XIV, and exceeded the electorate's initiative power on several grounds.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff land development company petitioned for a writ of mandate, a complaint for declaratory relief and injunctive relief of a measure which was sponsored by appellant intervenor citizens group and adopted by the electorate. The measure focused on city planning in view of growth and traffic congestion, but instead of directly amending the city general plan, it instructed defendant city to enact legislation complying with the measure. When a writ of preemptory mandate restraining the measure's implementation was granted plaintiff, appellant challenged the writ. On appeal, the court held that pursuant to Cal. Const. art. II, § 11, the initiative and referendum powers of the electors could only have been exercised under procedures approved by the legislature, and that Cal. Const. art. II, § 8 defined an initiative as the electors' power to propose statutes and constitutional amendments, and to adopt or reject them. Since the measure at issue did not amend defendant's general plan, but directed the city council to implement the general plan and a zoning ordinance, it was an invalid exercise of the initiative power. The court, therefore, affirmed the preemptory writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting the writ of mandate to plaintiff land development company was affirmed, and the electorate-approved initiative was invalidated because the court held that the initiative failed to satisfy constitutional requirements and constituted an invalid exercise of the electorate's initiative power. Since those defects affected the entire measure, the court held, the initiative's severance clause could not have saved any part of it.

Stratton v. Stratton


No. G009014


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


226 Cal. App. 3d 1392; 277 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 51; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 643; 91 Daily Journal DAR 875



January 17, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 543055, Theodore E. Millard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review after the Superior Court of Orange County (California) entered a child support judgment, requiring appellant to pay $ 151 per month.


OVERVIEW: After appellant husband was served with a Revised Uniform Reciprocal Enforcement of Support Act petition, the matter proceeded to a contested hearing. Appellant demanded that the matter be heard by a superior court judge, but a commissioner determined that he was empowered to hear the matter and recommended child support for respondent wife be set at $ 151 per month. Subsequently, a superior court judge reviewed the recommendation and approved it. Appellant sought review, claiming that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 640.1(c) violated Cal. Const. art. VI, § 21. The court affirmed. The court found that § 640.1 was a special reference, and thus survived constitutional scrutiny. The hearing procedure was statutorily provided, and the superior court judge was mandated to independently review the commissioner's decision and to rehear the matter if necessary. The court found that the superior court judge made a final determination. Thus, appellant and respondent were afforded an opportunity to have their matter determined by a constitutionally sanctioned judicial officer. Accordingly, the court affirmed the child support judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's child support judgment because the commissioner's recommendation was allowable under statutory law, and thus did not violate the state's constitution. The commissioner's recommendation was a special reference that could be ordered without appellant husband's or respondent wife's consent.

Bailey v. City of Nat'l City


No. D011272

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandamus contending that the action of respondent city council in upholding his termination was an abuse of discretion and unsupported by the evidence.

1324 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hernandez


No. F013021


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


226 Cal. App. 3d 1374; 277 Cal. Rptr. 444; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 41; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 581; 91 Daily Journal DAR 868



January 16, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I, II, IV, and V.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 26721, Howard R. Broadman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We remand this matter for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed herein. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court, Tulare County (California), which ordered him and his co-defendant to make restitution to the victim and be jointly and severally liable for the restitution. Restitution was a condition of probation following defendant's plea of nolo contendere to the charges of sending a false bomb and filing a false report with the fire department concerning the planting of the bomb.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with sending a false bomb in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 148.1(d) and filing a false report with the fire department concerning the planting of the bomb in violation of § 148.1(a). Defendant was granted probation, ordered to spend time in jail, to make restitution along with his co-defendant to the victim, and to pay a fine and penalty assessment. The trial court's order imposed joint and several liability on defendant and co-defendant for the restitution as a condition of probation. On appeal, the court ordered resentencing so as to eliminate the joint and several liability condition and issue a new order. The court found that defendant's probation conditions were dependent upon the actions of his co-defendant in making restitution. Thus, the condition imposed was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. Also, requiring joint and several restitution was an attempt to address civil liability, which the trial court could not do in this case.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the matter remanded for resentencing because the imposition of joint and several liability as a condition of probation made it difficult to determine whether the condition had been violated, and defendant would not know the extent of his probationary conditions. The trial court was ordered to eliminate the joint and several liability portion of the restitution order and issue a new order. The rest of the judgment was affirmed.

 

1325 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ordonez


No. B034210


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


226 Cal. App. 3d 1207; 277 Cal. Rptr. 382; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 34; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 527; 91 Daily Journal DAR 692



January 15, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 28, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A539261, James H. Piatt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The abstract of judgment is modified by staying the sentence imposed for second degree murder until the completion of the term imposed for aggravated kidnapping, at which time the stay shall become permanent. The judgment is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of aggravated kidnapping in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 209(a), and second-degree felony murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated kidnapping under Cal. Penal Code § 209(a) and first-degree felony murder. He moved for a new trial on the ground that the jury had been instructed it could find him guilty of first-degree murder committed during a kidnap for ransom, which was not included in the first-degree felony murder statute in effect when the crime was committed. The trial court agreed that the instruction was erroneous but denied the motion for new trial and reduced the first-degree murder conviction to second-degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to concurrent terms of imprisonment. The court affirmed and held that kidnapping for ransom involved a substantial risk of death to the victim and therefore supported a conviction of second-degree felony murder. The court held that because defendant's conduct as a participant in the aggravated kidnapping and the second-degree murder were indistinguishable, he could not be punished for both crimes under Cal. Penal Code § 654. The court modified defendant's sentence by staying the sentence for second-degree murder until the completion of his sentence for aggravated kidnapping.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for aggravated kidnapping and second-degree felony murder and held that because kidnapping posed a substantial risk of death to the victim, it supported a conviction for second-degree felony murder. The court modified defendant's sentence to stay his sentence for second-degree murder until the completion of his sentence for aggravated kidnapping.

 

1326 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Andre P.


No. F013287


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


226 Cal. App. 3d 1164; 277 Cal. Rptr. 363; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 33; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 540; 91 Daily Journal DAR 687



January 14, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 62401, Leonard M. McGillivray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), determining that appellant had violated Cal. Penal Code § 148 and was subject to commitment to the jurisdiction of the California Youth Authority. Appellant claimed that § 148 was unconstitutionally overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a juvenile, was charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 148 when he fled from a police officer seeking to question him about a curfew violation. The lower court found that appellant violated § 148 and committed him to the youth authority. Appellant sought review, claiming that § 148 was facially overbroad and unconstitutional. The court held that § 148 was not facially overbroad, finding that it historically was applied to non-speech conduct; that appellant made no empirical showing that § 148 was often being applied to protected speech; that persons of ordinary intelligence did not need to guess at the statute's meaning; and that the small possibility of § 148 being applied to protected conduct was an insufficient basis for invalidation, especially because the statute could be narrowly construed to avoid any chilling effect. Because appellant's conduct fell within that proscribed by § 148, the court held that he lacked standing to assert facial invalidity. The court declined to determine whether appellant's failure to raise the constitutional challenge below prevented appellate review in the first instance.


OUTCOME: The court rejected appellant juvenile's claim that a statute prohibiting unlawful resistance, delay, or obstruction of a police officer was unconstitutionally overbroad. Appellant did not have standing to assert overbreadth because appellant's specific conduct, fleeing a police officer, was prohibited, and appellant made no showing that the statute reached a substantial amount of constitutionally protected conduct.

 

1327 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Urbaniak v. Newton


No. A045593


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


226 Cal. App. 3d 1128; 277 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 73; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 455; 91 Daily Journal DAR 706



January 14, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 870679, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment dismissing the third cause of action against Dr. Newton is reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. Costs to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the administrator of decedent's estate, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), granting summary judgment to respondents, a doctor and his practice, an insurer, and its counsel, on a claim that respondents' dissemination of a medical report revealing decedent's HIV infection was tortious and violated decedent's right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Decedent was examined by respondent doctor under arrangement with respondent insurer, in connection with a workers' compensation claim, and decedent told respondent's nurse that he was HIV-positive. That information was disclosed to and by respondent insurer and its counsel in the workers' compensation case. Decedent filed suit against respondents, claiming invasion of the right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The lower court granted summary judgment. Decedent and, after his death, appellant administrator of decedent's estate sought review. The court affirmed dismissal of all claims against respondents insurer and counsel, finding the claims barred by the privilege, under Cal. Civil Code § 47(2), for statements published in a judicial proceeding. The court also affirmed dismissal of emotional distress claims against respondent doctor because his conduct was not "outrageous." However, the court reversed the dismissal as to the claim of invasion of privacy, finding that decedent reasonably anticipated that his HIV status would not be disclosed and that respondent doctor's disclosure of that information was not privileged as a matter of law, but raised an issue for trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the order granting summary judgment against appellant administrator of decedent's estate and in favor of respondents, a doctor and his practice, its insurer, and counsel. The court reversed the summary judgment as to appellant's claim that respondent doctor violated decedent's right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, finding that there was a triable issue as to an improper use of properly obtained information.

 

1328 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Peppard v. Carpinteria


No. B047242.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six.


226 Cal. App. 3d 812; 278 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 2; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 223; 91 Daily Journal DAR 245



January 3, 1991.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified February 1, 1991.
Review Denied March 21, 1991 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 176535, James M. Slater, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mobilehome park owners challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which sustained respondent city's demurrer in an inverse condemnation proceeding. Appellants' action challenged the constitutionality of respondent's rent control ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mobilehome park owners brought an action against respondent city for inverse condemnation, alleging that respondent's rent control ordinance was an unlawful taking of appellants' property without payment of just compensation. Appellants also sought a declaration that they had the right to raise rents to market levels at the time of vacancy. The trial court granted respondent's demurrer to the first amended complaint without leave to amend because it did not state a cause of action, appellants were collaterally estopped from raising the issues, and the claims were barred by the statute of limitations. On appeal, the court held that respondent's rent control ordinance was not an unconstitutional taking because it was reasonably calculated to further respondent's interest in eliminating excessive rents for spaces in mobilehome parks and protecting the investment of mobilehome owners. Further, it did not deny appellants an economically viable use of their land. Appellants' inverse condemnation claim was barred by the statute of limitations of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 318, because the action was brought more than five years after the provisions at issue were originally enacted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which sustained respondent city's demurrer in appellant mobilehome park owners' action for inverse condemnation. Respondent's rent control ordinance did not constitute an unlawful taking of appellants' property where it furthered respondent's legitimate interests and provided appellants with a reasonable return on their property.

 

1329 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Moore


No. A048558.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two.


226 Cal. App. 3d 783; 277 Cal. Rptr. 82; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 6; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 199; 91 Daily Journal DAR 196



January 2, 1991 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


*  Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County, No. 11045, J. Michael Brown, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which, following revocation of his probation, imposed a prison sentence upon him that granted conduct credit under Cal. Penal Code § 4019 for time spent in jail, but not for time spent in an alcohol recovery center.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the sentence imposed upon him after revocation of his probation, which granted him conduct credit for time in prison pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4019, but not for time spent in an alcohol recovery center. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that § 4019, which authorized conduct credit for prison time but not for time spent in nonpenal institutions, did not violate the equal protection provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, or U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court held that § 4019 did not affect two similarly situated groups in an unequal manner, because a prisoner receiving treatment in an alcohol recovery center, where the focus was on diagnosis and treatment, was not similarly situated to a person incarcerated in a penal institution, where the focus was on detention. The court held that the distinction between the two groups was not an arbitrary classification where conduct credit to prisoners encouraged good behavior by the promise of early release in relation to a fixed term, and a prisoner in a treatment center had other incentives for good behavior, including avoiding alternate jail time or an extension of time in the treatment center.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence, holding that the statute granting conduct credit for time served in prison but not in an alcohol recovery center did not violate the equal protection provisions of the California and United States Constitutions, because a prisoner being detained was not similarly situated to one being treated, and prisoner in treatment had other incentives for good conduct.

 

1330 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Love v. Superior Court


No. A050880


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


226 Cal. App. 3d 736; 276 Cal. Rptr. 660; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1363; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 109; 91 Daily Journal DAR 141



December 28, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 14, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 60955, Lucy Kelly McCabe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged; the petition for peremptory writ is denied. Pending finality of this opinion, the orders requiring testing remain stayed pursuant to the California Supreme Court's order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, convicted of soliciting an act of prostitution, filed a petition for writ of mandate from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), alleging that the statutory requirement that they undergo AIDS testing after their convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 647 (b) was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. IV and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, convicted of soliciting an act of prostitution under Cal. Penal Code § 647 (b), petitioned for a writ of mandate, alleging that the requirement that all those convicted of § 647 (b) be tested for AIDS was unconstitutional. The court held that the testing requirements were legal under U.S. Const. amend. IV. In applying the "special needs" doctrine, the court weighed the heightened governmental need for the action against the individual's privacy expectation and whether the need justified the intrusion. The court found that the public need to prevent AIDS outweighed the intrusion of collecting and testing an individual's blood. In addition, it found that the legislative scheme protected the subject individual's privacy by limiting dissemination of the test results to attorneys involved in prosecution of crimes with a penalty enhancement for sex offenses committed by those who knew they had AIDS. Due process concerns were not implicated because testing only applied to those convicted of soliciting an act of prostitution under Cal. Penal Code § 647 (b).


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of mandate filed by petitioners, convicted of soliciting an act of prostitution, holding that the AIDS testing requirements were constitutional under the "special needs" doctrine of the Fourth Amendment and also comported with due process requirements.

Balmoral Hotel Tenants Assn. v. Lee


No. A043696


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


226 Cal. App. 3d 686; 276 Cal. Rptr. 640; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1366; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 13; 91 Daily Journal DAR 40



December 27, 1990  
December 27, 1990, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions directed to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 28, 1991. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Kennard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 854669 and 864398, Ollie Marie-Victoire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the case remanded to the trial court for proceedings consistent with the view herein expressed. Costs to appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of a residential hotel, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that concluded appellants had violated a city ordinance prohibiting wrongful eviction and had repeatedly violated Cal. Civ. Code § 789.3 and that awarded damages totaling more than $ 4,000,000 in favor of respondent tenants.


OVERVIEW: Respondent tenants brought suit against appellants, owners of a residential hotel, for wrongful eviction under San Francisco Administrative Code § 37.9 (ordinance) and Cal. Civ. Code § 789.3. The jury found in favor of respondents and awarded damages in excess of $ 4,000,000. After the trial court denied their motion for new trial and for judgment notwithstanding the verdict, appellants filed a notice of appeal and contended that the trebling of the actual damages awarded, including the damages for mental anguish, resulted in an unconstitutionally excessive penalty. The court held that while the term "actual damages" was generally synonymous with compensatory damages, the mandatory trebling of damages for mental suffering could under some circumstances produce constitutionally excessive penalties. The court held that the treble damages provided for in the ordinance were intended to provide sufficient economic incentive for aggrieved persons to bring suit and that the trebling of damages for mental anguish was not reasonably related to that purpose. The court, therefore, reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that awarded respondent tenants treble damages for mental suffering because it found that the trebling of the damages for mental suffering was not reasonably related to the purpose of the ordinance and resulted in an excessive penalty.

 

1332 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



R. H. Macy & Co. v. Contra Costa County


No. A049789


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


226 Cal. App. 3d 352; 276 Cal. Rptr. 530; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1358; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9211; 90 Daily Journal DAR 14600



December 19, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 28, 1991. Kennard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. C89-04568, Richard S. Flier, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The United States Supreme Court expressly recognized and then refused to tinker with Proposition 13 in its recent Allegheny decision. That leaves untampered the California Supreme Court's Amador decision that Proposition 13 violates neither the equal protection clause nor the right to travel. With this long-settled law of California, we may neither tinker nor tamper. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) which entered judgment in favor of respondent county in an action for declaratory relief and a refund of taxes paid based on the assessed value of the real property at the time of purchase.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, property owners, challenged the constitutionality of the change in ownership provisions of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(a) (proposition 13). Appellants claimed that the provision violated the equal protection clause and interfered with interstate commerce. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that proposition 13 neither violated equal protection nor the right to travel. The court held that taxation of property at its acquisition value rather than its current value rested on a rational basis and fulfilled a legitimate legislative goal. The issue was adjudicated previously, and the provision was held constitutional. The court held that under stare decisis, the court was required to follow the precedent that the proposition was not unconstitutional, as the proposition was neutral and uniform in its application and did not discriminate directly or indirectly against residency. There was no iron rule of equality in imposing taxes, and the states were free to grant partial or total exemptions upon grounds of reasonable state policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent county because the acquisition value approach to assessing value of property for taxes was not wholly arbitrary or irrational and the measure did not violate either equal protection principles or the right to travel as it was neutral and uniform in its application.

 

1333 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Am. Bankers Ins. Co.


No. F012720


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


225 Cal. App. 3d 1378; 276 Cal. Rptr. 210; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1259; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8877; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13848



December 3, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 379635-6, Steven M. Vartabedian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent shall have its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bail surety company challenged the order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), that denied its motion to vacate a bail forfeiture entered pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1305.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bail surety company posted bail for defendant who appeared at arraignment when the trial court set dates for two pretrial hearings and the trial. The trial court did not expressly order defendant to be present at any of the dates. Defendant failed to appear at either of the pretrial hearings and the trial court ordered bail forfeited and denied appellant's motion to set aside summary judgment and exonerate the bond. Appellant claimed defendant had not been "lawfully required" to appear pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1305. The court affirmed the judgment because it found that defendant had actual notice of the hearings, that Cal. R. Ct. 227.6 required defendant's presence in court for a readiness conference (one of the pretrial hearings), and that defendant was therefore "lawfully required" to appear as that term was used in Cal. Penal Code § 1305(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of appellant bail surety company's motion to set aside forfeiture of the bail it posted, because it found that bail was properly forfeited after defendant's failure to attend two pretrial hearings. No express order to attend was necessary when defendant was lawfully required to attend a hearing by court rule, and had actual notice of hearing dates.

 

1334 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Nordlinger v. Lynch


No. B048719


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


225 Cal. App. 3d 1259; 275 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1264; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8777; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13895



December 3, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 28, 1991. Kennard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C738781, David A. Workman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is affirmed. Each party to bear respective costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the order of dismissal of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) following the sustaining of a demurrer interposed by respondents, county tax assessor and the County of Los Angeles, to appellant's first amended complaint in which she sought a refund of property taxes and a declaratory judgment pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 4808 that Cal. Const. art. XIII A was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Const. art. XIII A (Proposition 13) in her lawsuit against respondent county tax assessor and the County of Los Angeles seeking declaratory relief pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 4808 and a refund of property taxes. The court affirmed the order of dismissal, holding that Proposition 13 was constitutional, even though it created an arbitrary system of property valuation which imposed disparate tax burdens on owners of similarly situated properties. It held that Proposition 13 met the rational basis test and did not violate appellant's equal protection rights or her right to travel. Because the acquisition value approach used in valuations under Proposition 13 was neither wholly arbitrary nor irrational, it met the demands of the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The approach could have been upheld on the belief that taxes should be based on the original cost of property and unrealized paper gains in value should not be taxed. Because the right to migrate was not burdened by Proposition 13, a rational basis test rather than a heightened scrutiny standard was sufficient for analysis of constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant taxpayer's declaratory judgment action against respondents, county tax assessor and the County of Los Angeles, holding that, although the acquisition value approach used to determine tax liability imposed disparate tax burdens on California property owners of similarly situated properties, it was not unconstitutional.

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT

Score Family Fun Ctr. v. County of San Diego


No. D010929


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


225 Cal. App. 3d 1217; 275 Cal. Rptr. 358; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1455; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8816; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13631



November 30, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 14, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N33603, Anthony C. Joseph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant arcade owners challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of respondents, county and sheriff's department, which confiscated appellant's video games as illegal slot machines. Appellant asserted that the video games were not slot machines and that the statutory scheme allowing confiscation violated due process.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, county and sheriff's department, successfully asserted that the video games it confiscated from appellant arcade owners were illegal slot machines. The court affirmed. First, appellants contended that the video games were not slot machines within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 330b(2). The court held, however, that the trial court correctly determined the video games were slot machines. Second, appellants contended that the judgment must be reversed because Cal. Penal Code § 335a, which provided for the confiscation and destruction of slot machines, violated the constitutional guaranty of due process by not providing for a hearing before destruction of slot machines. The court held that it did not need to reach that issue because even assuming that section were unconstitutional, reversal would not follow. The court noted that appellants received a hearing and a judicial determination that the video games were illegal slot machines and that the determination was upheld on appeal. To reverse as appellants suggested would not change that determination or result in the return of the video games.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that appellant arcade owners' video games were slot machines within the statutory meaning. The court did not reach appellants' claim that the confiscation and destruction by respondents, county and sheriff's department, violated due process because reversal would not follow even if the court found unconstitutionality.
