Regents of University of California v. Superior Court


No. B051229


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


225 Cal. App. 3d 972; 276 Cal. Rptr. 197; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1233; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8628



November 28, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 28, 1991. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 607748, David P. Yaffe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged, and the petition for writ of mandate or prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state university system challenged the decision of respondent superior court (California) that granted real party in interest university employee's request for a summary ruling that Cal. Educ. Code § 68062 was correctly interpreted by the state attorney general and was constitutionally valid.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state university system adopted a provision, contained in Cal. Educ. Code § 68062, that permitted aliens to be classified as state residents for tuition purposes. The state attorney general took the position that undocumented aliens were precluded from qualifying as state residents for tuition purposes. The trial court rejected the interpretation, enjoined petitioner from treating all undocumented aliens as nonresidents, and held that § 68062 was unconstitutional. Petitioner did not appeal. Real party in interest university employee, who was assigned to determine the residency status of students at one state university, filed a separate action to require petitioner to comply with § 68062 as interpreted by the attorney general. Respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County issued a summary ruling in favor of real party in interest. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that § 68062 did not deny undocumented aliens their equal protection rights because such rights did not extend to undocumented aliens. The court held that § 68062 precluded undocumented aliens from being classified as residents for tuition purposes because undocumented aliens presence was unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling, holding that the statute permitting the treatment of aliens as state residents for tuition purposes precluded undocumented aliens from being treated as state residents and that such treatment did not violate constitutional equal protection rights.

 

1338 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Williams v. County of San Joaquin


No. C006487


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


225 Cal. App. 3d 1326; 275 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1238; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8719; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13561



November 28, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. SJC 208955, Stephen G. Demetras, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer and bail bondsmen's association appealed an order from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), where plaintiffs' complaint was dismissed. Plaintiffs brought suit asserting that defendant county's own recognizance release program violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(e) and Cal. Penal Code § 1274.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer and bail bondsmen's association brought an action against defendant county to enjoin operation of the county's own recognizance release program (the OR release). The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' complaint. On appeal, plaintiffs asserted that the OR release violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(e) and Cal. Penal Code § 1274 because the district attorney was not given notice and an opportunity to participate in decisions to grant OR releases. The court affirmed the judgment. First, the court held that the California Supreme Court recognized that release on bail and the OR release were separate and distinct procedures. The court then held that Cal. Const. Art. I, § 28 (e) did not require that the prosecuting attorney be notified and given the opportunity to be heard before granting an OR release. The court stated that the notice and hearing provision applied only when an applicant for bail was arrested for a serious felony. The court concluded that if the enactors of section 28 (e) had intended a notice and hearing requirement to apply to OR releases, they could have said so. Thus, the court concluded that notice was not required in OR releases.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff taxpayer and bail bondmen's association's complaint because the notice requirements applied only when an applicant was arrested for a serious felony. The court held that the prosecuting attorney was not required to be notified prior to granting own recognizance releases.

Allstate Ins. Co. v. Gillespie


No. B047071.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven.


225 Cal. App. 3d 798; 275 Cal. Rptr. 525; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1243; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8634; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13582



November 27, 1990 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing December 26, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1356; Review Denied February 21, 1991 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1991 Cal. LEXIS 771.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, LASC No. C744670, Miriam A. Vogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance commissioner sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued a writ of mandate compelling appellant to permit respondent insurer an increase in rates for assigned risk policies, and a preliminary injunction prohibiting appellant from interfering with respondent's increase, all conditioned on respondent refunding the increase if it were ultimately found to be unreasonable.


OVERVIEW: Before appellant insurance commissioner completed rate-setting procedures required by Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.01, respondent insurer sued to compel appellant to permit an interim rate increase for assigned risk policies. Respondent alleged that it was incurring large losses from automobile insurance it provided to individuals in the assigned risk pool. Respondent also claimed that appellant imposed confiscatory rates in violation of the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and caused respondent to suffer irreparable harm because the loss of a fair and reasonable return could not be recovered from future rate increases. The court reversed a trial court judgment that compelled appellant to permit an increase in respondent's rates for assigned risk policies. Respondent's failure to exhaust administrative remedies barred it from seeking a writ of mandate. Appellant did not clearly abuse her discretion because that discretion was not finally applied to the ratemaking issues. Appellant's decision to protect respondent from confiscatory rates by permitting recoupment through future rate increases was not clearly erroneous or unauthorized by Cal. Ins. Code §§ 1861.02, 1861.05.


OUTCOME: The judgment compelling appellant insurance commissioner to permit respondent insurer an increase in rates for assigned risk policies, and prohibiting appellant from interfering with respondent's increase, was reversed. Respondent failed to exhaust its available administrative remedies, and exhaustion would not have resulted in irreparable injury where appellant had authority to allow respondent to recoup losses through future rate increases.

People v. Mixon


No. B036192


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


225 Cal. App. 3d 1471; 275 Cal. Rptr. 817; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1236; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8666; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13554



November 27, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1991. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J. were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A563870, Eric E. Younger and Michael A. Tyan, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California) that convicted him of attempted murder, two counts of forcible rape, forcible oral copulation, kidnapping, burglary, and felony assault. Defendant was convicted after he was certified competent following his stay in a state hospital, which followed an earlier trial court decision that defendant was incompetent to stand trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted murder, two counts of forcible rape, forcible oral copulation, kidnapping, burglary, and felony assault. The trial court found him incompetent to stand trial. After a stay in a state hospital, defendant was certified as competent by the hospital. During a restoration of competency hearing requested by defendant, defendant was found competent and subsequently convicted. On appeal, the court affirmed his convictions. The court held that defendant had the burden of proof in the restoration of competency hearing because he was the one who requested the hearing. The court held that the statutory procedures for determining competency and the restoration of competency, contained in Cal. Penal Code §§ 1367-1375.5, did not violate defendant's right to due process. The court also held that there was sufficient evidence to convict on two separate rape counts and that Cal. Penal Code § 654 did not bar separate punishment for burglary and the sex offenses because the evidence supported the theory that defendant had a separate intent to commit each of the acts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, holding that defendant had the burden of proof in a challenge to his competency certification, that the statutory proceeding for determining competency and the restoration of competency did not violate defendant's due process rights, and that the evidence supported a finding that defendant had committed two separate rapes.

 

1341 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. McLead


No. E006753


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


225 Cal. App. 3d 906; 276 Cal. Rptr. 187; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1245; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8724; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13645



November 27, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, IIIB & C, IV, V, VI, IX and X.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1991.
Review denied by, 02/14/1991
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re McLead, 2002 Cal. LEXIS 2017 (Cal., Mar. 27, 2002)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Magistrate's recommendation at McLead v. Galaza, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 104751 (C.D. Cal., Jan. 26, 2011)
Withdrawn by publisher


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-25725, Edward D. Webster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court is ordered to prepare an amended abstract of judgment to reflect the staying of the sentence for counts III and V for all three defendants. The remainder of the judgments are affirmed as to all three defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgments of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted defendants of three counts of conspiracy to murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182, and first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, with the special circumstance that the murder was committed for financial gain as provided for under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought review of their convictions for three counts of conspiracy to murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182, and of their convictions for first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, with the special circumstance that the murder was committed for financial gain as provided for under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(1). On appeal, the court affirmed all of the convictions. The evidence was sufficient to support the trial court's instruction to the jury on the felony-murder theory of first degree murder because the evidence showed that defendants had both an intent to kill and an intent to steal and that the robbery and the murder were part of one continuous transaction. The court ruled that the substantial evidence that showed that defendants committed the murder in order to eliminate their competition in the local drug market was sufficient to support the finding that the murder was committed for financial gain. The court determined that the evidence that showed that defendants agreed to kill three specific individuals and that defendants had different motives for eliminating each individual supported convictions for three counts of conspiracy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions, holding that that there was sufficient evidence to support a jury instruction on felony-murder because the robbery was not completed before the murder, that the finding that the murder was committed for financial gain was supported where the victim was a competing drug dealer, and that the evidence supported convictions for three counts of conspiracy because defendants agreed to kill three individuals.

 

1342 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Imperial Irrigation Dist. v. State Wat. Res. Control Bd.


No. D008521


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


225 Cal. App. 3d 548; 275 Cal. Rptr. 250; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1211; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8504



November 21, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 12, 1990, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied March 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, No. 58706, James A. Malkus, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant irrigation district sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California), which denied a writ of mandate to overturn a decision of respondent water resources control board, which had held that appellant's irrigation practices were wasteful and unreasonable. Appellant contended that respondent had exceeded its jurisdiction and its order exceeded the bounds of its discretion.


OVERVIEW: Respondent water resources control board issued a decision that concluded appellant irrigation district's practices were wasteful and unreasonable, and required appellant to submit a plan for reservoir construction and to take other actions. Appellant sought review of respondent's action by writ of mandate. The court held that the writ was properly denied. Cal. Const. art. X, § 2 required water uses to conform to a standard of reasonable use, and respondent had the authority to adjudicate whether appellant's use of water satisfied the constitutional standard. The concept of separation of powers did not apply because both parties were administrative agencies, and any objective of separateness was not violated when one regulated the other. The interference by respondent with appellant's use of water did not constitute a transgression of a vested right because appellant did not acquire a vested right to the unreasonable use of water. Respondent was the expert in the field of water waste, and did not have to defer to appellant's decisions. Respondent's findings were supported by the evidence, and its specific order did not exceed reason or the bounds of its discretion.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying a writ of mandate to overturn a decision of respondent water resources control board was affirmed. Respondent had jurisdiction to rule on the reasonableness of appellant irrigation district's irrigation practices, and did not have to defer to the decisions of appellant. Appellant had no vested right to misuse water, and respondent's specific order did not exceed reason or the bounds of respondent's discretion.

 

1343 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Glenn


No. E007173


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


225 Cal. App. 3d 618; 275 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1209; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8516



November 21, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Parts 2 through 7 are not published; they do not meet the standards for publication contained in California Rules of Court, rule 976.1.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 30, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 007173, Jerome E. Stevenson, Judge. +


+ Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction entered by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), for committing lewd and lascivious acts on a minor under Cal. Penal Code § 288(a), for attempting such acts under Cal. Penal Code § 664, 288(a), and indecent exposure under Cal. Penal Code § 314(1). Appellant contended that there was error in the constitution of the jury.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted for committing lewd and lascivious acts on a minor under Cal. Penal Code § 288(a), for attempting such acts under Cal. Penal Code § 664, 288(a), and indecent exposure under Cal. Penal Code § 314(1). During trial, the trial court announced that it intended to swear in all 14 jurors on the panel and have them hear the case. After evidence had been presented, the clerk would select two jurors at random to serve as alternates, and only the remaining 12 would participate in deliberations. Appellant made no objections. After the trial and convictions, appellant challenged the jury method as unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16, which provides for a jury of 12. The court affirmed appellant's convictions and held that only 12 jurors deliberated his case and he was entitled to nothing else under the constitution, and was not prejudiced. Although the trial court did not technically follow Cal. Penal Code § 1089, by designating alternate jurors immediately after the jury was sworn, the statute's purpose was to notify trial courts of their discretion to select alternates.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for committing lewd and lascivious acts on a minor, for attempting such acts, and indecent exposure, and found that there was no error in the constitution of the jury because the trial court's method of allowing 14 jurors to hear the case and then picking two to serve as alternates before deliberation did not prejudice appellant or interfere with his exercise of his right to peremptory challenges.

 

1344 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Samuel V.


No. D011428


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


225 Cal. App. 3d 511; 277 Cal. Rptr. 14; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1214; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8510



November 20, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 13, 1990, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J154803, Hideo Chino, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) convicting him of carrying a concealed dirk or dagger. Defendant contended that the statute, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 625(a), which permitted arrest without a warrant or without the offense having been committed in the presence of the officer, violated his equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was taken into custody without a warrant for brandishing a weapon, which was not done in the presence of an officer. Defendant admitted to the incident and was arrested for brandishing a weapon and carrying a concealed dirk or dagger. Defendant was convicted on the concealment charge. On appeal, defendant claimed that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 625(a), which permitted minors suspected of committing a crime to be taken into temporary custody without a warrant, violated his equal protection rights because a similar statute required that the warrantless arrest of an adult for a misdemeanor had to be for a crime committed in the presence of an officer. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that minors and adults were not similarly situated and, therefore, there was no violation of defendant's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for concealment of a dagger or dirk, holding that defendant juvenile's equal protection rights were not violated by a statute that permitted minors to be arrested without a warrant for a crime not committed in the presence of an officer, but imposed a different requirement on adults, because adults and juveniles were not similarly situated groups.

 

1345 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Upjohn Co. v. Brewer


No. C008232


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


225 Cal. App. 3d 353; 274 Cal. Rptr. 763; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1186; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8431



November 16, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Proceeding: Petition for Writ of Mandate. Writ denied.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied and the alternative writ previously issued is discharged. Defendants will receive their costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought a writ of mandate compelling defendants office of administrative law and department of finance to add to the formulary a prescription drug marketed by plaintiff. Defendants claimed that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14105.9 which mandated the addition of such a drug to the formulary never became law based on the governor's veto of a provision of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought a writ of mandate compelling defendants office of administrative law and department of finance to add to the formulary a prescription drug marketed by plaintiff. Plaintiff claimed defendants had a mandatory duty pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14105.9 to add such drug to the formulary and the governor's veto of a provision adding § 14105.9 was invalid. The court denied the writ and held that the veto was valid and § 14105.9 never became law. As such, the court held that absent a valid statutory exception, plaintiff's drug could only be added to the formulary after testing and approval pursuant to the normal regulatory process. Plaintiff, however, never requested that its drug be added to the formulary pursuant to the normal regulatory process, and therefore, plaintiff had no cause for complaint in this court.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and held that the governor's veto was valid and the provision mandating the addition of plaintiff's drug never became law. As such, plaintiff's drug could only be added to the formulary pursuant to the normal regulatory process. Plaintiff never requested that its drug be added pursuant to the normal process, and therefore, plaintiff had no cause for complaint in the court.

 

1346 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Long Beach Unified Sch. Dist. v. State of California


No. B033742


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


225 Cal. App. 3d 155; 275 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1198



November 15, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petitions for review by the Supreme Court were denied February 28, 1991. Lucas, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C606020, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that because the doctrines of collateral estoppel and waiver are inapplicable to the facts of this case, the trial court should have allowed State to challenge the decisions of the Board. However, we also determine, as a question of law, that the Executive Order requires local school boards to provide a higher level of service than is required constitutionally or by case law and that the Executive Order is a reimbursable state mandate pursuant to article XIII B, section 6 of the California Constitution. Former Revenue and Tax Code section 2234 does not provide reimbursement of the subject claim. Based on uncontradicted evidence, we modify the decision of the trial court by striking as sources of reimbursement the Special Fund for Economic Uncertainties "or similarly designated accounts." We also modify the judgment to include charging orders against certain funds appropriated through subsequent budget acts. We affirm the decision of the trial court that the Fines [***2] and Forfeitures Funds are not "reasonably available" to satisfy the Claim. Finally, we remand the matter to the trial court to determine whether at the time of its order, unexpended, unencumbered funds sufficient to satisfy the judgment remained in the approved budget line item account numbers. The trial court is also directed to determine this same issue with respect to the charging order. The judgment is affirmed as modified. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) stating that it was required to reimburse cross-appellant school district for mandated expenditures to integrate the schools, and cross-appellant challenged that part of the order stating that certain funds were not available for this reimbursement.


OVERVIEW: The California Department of Education issued an executive order mandating expenditures to integrate the schools, and when the legislature deleted the requested funding from its budget, cross-appellant school district filed a petition to compel reimbursement after the Board of Control approved the claim. The trial court stated that appellant state was required to make these reimbursements and designated specific funds as reasonably available for the payments, but also ruled that certain funds were not available for these payments. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision as modified, holding that the doctrines of collateral estoppel and waiver were inapplicable and that the trial court should have allowed appellant to challenge the initial decisions of Board of Control in this matter. However, the court concluded that as a matter of law the executive order was a reimbursable state mandate pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6, not pursuant to former Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 2234. The court modified the decision by striking certain funds as sources of reimbursement and affirmed that portion of the order stating that certain funds were not available for the payments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order stating that appellant state was required to reimburse cross-appellant school district for mandated expenditures to integrate the schools because the executive order was a reimbursable state mandate under the California constitution and modified the designated funds for payment. The case was remanded to determine if unexpended, unencumbered funds existed in the approved budget line item account numbers.

 

1347 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Smith v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A046622


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


225 Cal. App. 3d 38; 275 Cal. Rptr. 17; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1189



November 14, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 806-788, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appellants do not suggest any facts they could allege which would remedy the defects discussed above. To the contrary, they ask that we reverse to allow them to proceed with their action "as pleaded." After three attempts, appellants have still failed to state a cause of action against the City. The demurrer was therefore properly sustained without leave to amend. ( Pinheiro v. County of Marin, supra, 60 Cal.App.3d at pp. 328-329.) The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that sustained a demurrer without leave to amend to their second amended cross-complaint against respondent city for inverse condemnation, promissory estoppel, breach of the covenant of good faith and fair dealing, negligence, breach of fiduciary duty, civil rights violations, and breach of oral contract.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city and appellant property owners settled respondent's complaint for eminent domain to acquire property for park use. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer to appellants' second amended cross-complaint for inverse condemnation, promissory estoppel, breach of the covenant of good faith and fair dealing, negligence, breach of fiduciary duty, civil rights violations, and breach of oral contract without leave to amend, and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that appellants had failed to state a cause of action for inverse condemnation because they had not alleged a denial of all economic use of their property in a final determination of the land's permitted use, nor did they show that respondent's conduct had diminished their property value. The court also found that appellants had not demonstrated their detrimental reliance on respondent's promises, thereby precluding a claim for promissory estoppel. Since there was not contract between the parties, the court found no breach of implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing, nor was there a tort duty to appellants either from Cal. Gov't Code § 7267.1 or from a special relationship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order sustaining respondent city's demurrer without leave to amend appellant landowners' amended cross-complaint against respondent for inverse condemnation, promissory estoppel, breach of the covenant of good faith and fair dealing, negligence, breach of fiduciary duty, civil rights violations, and breach of oral contract because appellants failed to allege facts sufficient to sustain those causes of action.

 

1348 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Yee v. City of Escondido


No. D010307


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


224 Cal. App. 3d 1349; 274 Cal. Rptr. 551; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1139



October 31, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 24, 1991. Baxter, J., did not participate therein. Lucas, C. J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N42268, Don Martinson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mobile home park owners appealed a Superior Court of San Diego County (California) judgment that dismissed plaintiffs' action against defendant city challenging a rent control ordinance. Plaintiffs asked the appellate court to reconsider its prior holding that such ordinances did not amount to a compensable "taking" within the meaning of U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, in light of a recent federal court opinion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mobile home park owners filed suit against defendant city challenging the constitutionality of a mobile home rent control ordinance on its face and seeking compensation for the interest that they alleged had been taken. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer to the complaint and dismissed the action. On appeal, plaintiffs asked the appeals court to reconsider its prior holding that such ordinances did not amount to a compensable "taking" within the meaning of the U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, in light of a recent federal court opinion. The court affirmed. The court noted that a rent control ordinance is a valid exercise of the police power if it is reasonably calculated to eliminate excessive rents and provide landlords with a just and reasonable return of their property. The court further ruled that when government acts to restore fair rents by imposing rent control, the fact that the price of used mobile homes rises has not unreasonably "taken" anything from the landlord or caused a "permanent physical occupation" of the landlord's property. The court reasoned that landlords were not required to rent their property to mobile home owners.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the trial court judgment dismissing plaintiff mobile home park owners' challenge to a mobile home rent control ordinance. The court ruled that the ordinance did not amount to a compensable "taking," even though mobile home prices increased after passage of the ordinance, because there was no showing that the ordinance reduced rents more than required for purposes of defendant city's police power.

 

1349 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Shell Western E & P, Inc. v. County of Lake


No. A047416


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


224 Cal. App. 3d 974; 274 Cal. Rptr. 313; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1126



October 23, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Lake County, No. 23509, Robert L. Crone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Lake County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent taxpayer in its action against appellant to recover for overpayment of taxes, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 1604(c).


OVERVIEW: Appellant county assessed the value of respondent taxpayer's interests in geothermal resources located within appellant. Respondent paid the assessed taxes, then applied to appellant's board of equalization for reduction of the assessments. More than two years later, the board held a hearing and denied respondent's application. Respondent filed a complaint for recovery of taxes overpaid. The trial court entered judgment for respondent, and appellant sought review of the decision. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court found that the trial court could have concluded as a matter of law that respondent was not a party to a stipulation to extend the two-year period after which a taxpayer's valuation of taxable property was accepted as proper under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 1604(c), either because the stipulation was unambiguous or because extrinsic evidence required the same conclusion. The court held that either or both bases supported the trial court's decision to overturn the board of equalization's contrary determination, and that § 1604(c) established a mandatory duty that appellant accept respondent's valuations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent taxpayer, in its action against appellant county for recovery of taxes overpaid, because appellant's delay in holding a hearing created a statutory duty to accept respondent's valuation of taxable property.

 

1350 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Vega


No. F012660


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


224 Cal. App. 3d 506; 273 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1058



October 3, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 4, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, Nos. 330481-3 and 399139-5, Ralph Nunez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the provisions of part III of this opinion. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his criminal conviction by jury in the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), where the prosecution alleged enhancements under Cal. Penal Code §§ 667.5, 12022.1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of petty theft with a prior theft-related conviction and a third count of burglary of the second degree, along with all enhancements. The prosecution alleged an enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.1 on the count III burglary charge because it occurred while defendant was released from custody on counts I and II. A prior prison term enhancement under § 667.5(b) was alleged for a 1979 burglary conviction. This was the same prior conviction alleged as a part of the charges in counts I and II. The court found no violation in the use of defendant's 1979 burglary conviction as a basis for increasing his petty thefts to felonies and in enhancing his sentence under § 667.5(b). Defendant's argument that § 12022.1 was amended while he was a fugitive was without merit. The act of burglarizing an establishment, from which count III arose, occurred long after the new statute's effective date and triggered its application. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the matter to the trial court for a statement of reasons for the consecutive sentence on count II. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court for its failure to state reasons for defendant's consecutive sentence on the second count of petty theft with a prior theft-related conviction. Defendant's convictions and sentence were affirmed in all other respects because the prosecution was allowed to use defendant's prior burglary conviction for enhancing defendant's petty theft to a felony and increasing his sentence.

San Francisco Taxpayers Assn. v. Board of Supervisors


No. A048363


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 1159; 273 Cal. Rptr. 503; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1039



September 27, 1990, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified October 17, 1990. Review Granted December 13, 1990.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 224 Cal. App. 3d 536 and 231 Cal. App. 3d 1191.)] (See 2 Cal. 4th 571)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 901018, Raymond J. Arata, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is reversed. The trial court is directed to enter judgment for the Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Supervisors of the City and County of San Francisco sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent taxpayers association in respondent's action challenging appellant for excluding annual appropriations to the city's employee retirement fund from the spending limitations imposed by Cal. Const. art. XIII B.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Board of Supervisors of the City and County of San Francisco funded the city's employee retirement system from annual appropriations and began excluding the contributions from its "appropriations subject to limitation" calculation under Cal. Const. art. XIII B. Respondent taxpayers association filed a petition for writ of mandate and for injunctive and declaratory relief to compel appellant to include the retirement fund contributions within the constitutional spending limitation. Respondent's summary judgment motion was granted. On appeal, the court reversed the grant of summary judgment and directed the trial court to enter judgment in favor of appellant because appellant's contributions to the retirement fund were a legally authorized, contractual "debt service" under Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 8(g), and expressly exempted from the spending limitations. The court found that, due to the contradictory language in § 8(g) and Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 5, appellant's contributions were "debt service" as that interpretation was in keeping with the manifest purpose of the voters in art. XIII B and would not violate the federal contract clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondent taxpayers association and directed the trial court to enter judgment for appellant Board of Supervisors of the City and County of San Francisco. The court held that, because the city's contributions to the voter-approved retirement fund constituted a legally authorized "debt service," it was expressly exempted from the constitutional spending limitations provision.

 

1352 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Department of Developmental Services v. Ladd


No. A046065


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


224 Cal. App. 3d 128; 273 Cal. Rptr. 485; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1041



September 27, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied December 20, 1990. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 117385, William H. Stephens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient, committed to a state hospital after a jury verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which upheld the constitutionality of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7275, insofar as it required appellant to be responsible for the costs of her institutional care, support, and maintenance.


OVERVIEW: In response to respondent Department of Developmental Services' attempt to obtain payment for the medical services rendered appellant patient, committed to a state hospital after a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity, appellant brought suit and claimed that the statute providing for payment violated equal protection. Appellant's argument was that imprisoned inmates were treated differently, since a statute provided that their medical treatment would be paid for by the state, and this disparity was illegal. In affirming the judgment of the lower court, the court found that there was a rational basis for both statutes. The statute governing individuals committed to state hospitals was of principal benefit to the patient; while society also benefited from the commitment, this benefit was secondary to that derived by the patient. Also, those individuals, since they have not been convicted of a crime, have a lesser degree of stigma attached to their commitment than do prison inmates. On the other hand, the state could rationally decide that once it sent an individual to prison, it would bear the cost of incarceration, including the medical care of the inmate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, holding that there was a rational basis for the statute which required individuals who had been found not guilty by reason of insanity to pay for their care, support and maintenance at state hospitals. There was also a rational basis for the statute which provided that the state would bear the costs of medical treatment for prison inmates. Thus, there was no equal protection violation.

Hill v. National Collegiate Athletic Assn.


No. H005079


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


18 Cal. App. 4th 1290; 273 Cal. Rptr. 402; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1012



September 25, 1990, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 26, 1990. Review Granted December 20, 1990 (S018180), Reported at: 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5741.
 [Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 223 Cal. App. 3d 1642 and 230 Cal. App. 3d 1714 and 1 Cal. App. 4th 1398 and 7 Cal. App. 4th 1738 and 13 Cal. App. 4th 434.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 619209, Conrad Lee Rushing, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Appellant's motion to produce additional evidence is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant National Collegiate Athletic Association appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) permanently enjoining it from enforcing its drug testing program against Stanford University students, including plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Defendant National Collegiate Athletic Association (NCAA) developed a drug testing program in response to concern about the use of drugs in college athletics. Students were required to sign consent forms at the start of each school year or they were rendered ineligible to compete. Plaintiffs, student athletes at Stanford University, filed a complaint alleging that the program violated their right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment permanently enjoining defendant from enforcing the program against Stanford students. The court first found that the NCAA, a private, voluntary association of institutions, was subject to the limitations of Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The court then found that the NCAA policy violated that constitutional provision because the testing procedures were not related to the alleged purposes of the requirements conferring the benefit of association participation, the program's utility did not manifestly outweigh the infringement on the students' rights to privacy, and there were less offensive alternatives to the program.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for plaintiffs, holding that defendant National Collegiate Athletic Association's drug testing program violated plaintiffs' state constitutional right to privacy. The program was not related to the benefits conferred by association membership, the program's utility did not manifestly outweigh the impingement on the students' right, and there were less offensive alternatives.

 

1354 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Leslie's Pool Mart v. Dep't of Food & Agric.


No. B027715


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


223 Cal. App. 3d 1524; 273 Cal. Rptr. 373; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1011



September 24, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 24, 1990, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied January 16, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C395664, consolidated with No. C431395, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to defendants' jurisdiction to regulate and impose assessments with respect to Leslie's swimming pool chemicals; as to the amount of assessments and refund thereof; as to refund of registration fees relating to Leslie's swimming pool chemicals; and as to attorney's fees under Government Code section 800. The case is remanded to the trial court to enter a new and different judgment declaring the rights and duties of the parties consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own cost on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Food and Agriculture (California) appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), which declared that the Economic Poisons Act, Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12751, did not give defendant jurisdiction to regulate or tax products sold by plaintiff retailer and that plaintiff was entitled to a tax refund, found that plaintiff's due process rights were violated, and awarded plaintiff attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff retailer sued defendant Department of Food and Agriculture, seeking a declaration that products used to kill swimming pool algae and bacteria were not "pesticides" subject to defendant's regulatory jurisdiction under the Economic Poisons Act, Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12751, that defendant had no authority to assess taxes on those products, and that defendant's seizure of those products without a hearing violated plaintiff's due process rights. The trial court held for plaintiff and awarded a tax refund and attorney's fees. The appellate court affirmed, stating that, in the absence of a threat to public health, defendant's seizure of products without a hearing violated plaintiff's due process rights. However, the court held that products sold by plaintiff were "pesticides" subject to defendant's regulatory and taxation authority under § 12751, and the tax assessed on those products was not arbitrary, unreasonable, or unconstitutional. Plaintiff was not entitled to attorney's fees under Cal. Gov't Code § 800 because plaintiff's challenge to the seizure of products without a hearing was not an appeal from a determination of an administrative proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment that defendant Department of Food and Agriculture did not have jurisdiction to regulate or tax products sold by plaintiff retailer and awarded plaintiff a tax refund was reversed. The products were "pesticides." The judgment that plaintiff's due process rights were violated was affirmed. Plaintiff was entitled to a hearing before its products were seized. The award of attorney's fee was unauthorized and was reversed.

 

1355 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Moores v. Edelbrock


No. A044267.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five.


223 Cal. App. 3d 941; 272 Cal. Rptr. 919; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 981



September 10, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for review GRANTED December 20, 1990 [S017981], Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5742.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 54638, Conrad L. Cox, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, water district and its board members, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), that summarily rejected a constitutional challenge to the landowner voting system used to select the water district's board of directors.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, water district and its board members, challenged the system that allowed each landowner to vote in proportion to the assessed value of his or her land holdings in elections to select the water district's board of directors. The lower court rejected appellants' constitutional challenge. The issue on appeal was whether the purpose of the water district was sufficiently specialized and narrow, and its activities bearing on landowners so disproportionately, as to distinguish the district from public entities whose more general governmental functions demanded application of the one person, one vote rule. The court found that the water district possessed some attributes of a governmental entity but concluded that the principle of one person, one vote was inapplicable to the district. The court found a rational basis for the restricted franchise because the state legislature could have concluded that the number of landowners with sufficient amounts of acreage whose consent was necessary to organize the water district would not have subjected their land to the lien of its possibly very substantial assessments unless they had a dominant voice in its control.


OUTCOME: The rejection of the constitutional challenge raised by appellants, water district and its board members, to the landowner voting system was affirmed because there was a rational basis for the practice.

 

1356 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Bjornestad v. Hulse


No. C007526.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 507; 272 Cal. Rptr. 864; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 958



September 5, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted December 20, 1990 (S017918) and cause trans. to Ct. App. 3d Dist., with directons to vacate its opn. and reconsider in light of Assembly Bill No. 3548. (See 229 Cal. App. 3d 1568.)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Placer County, No. 86397, James D. Garbolino, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a county water district, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Placer County (California), which held that Cal. Water Code § 30700.6 was unconstitutional under the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. 14, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7.


OVERVIEW: Respondent non-landowners filed suit seeking a declaration that Cal. Water Code § 30700.6 was unconstitutional under the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. Section 30700.6 provided that only one landowner per parcel could vote in a district election. The trial court ruled in favor of respondents and postponed a November election so that an election could take place in compliance with the Cal. Elec. Code § 30068, which would govern in the absence of § 30700.6. Appellant, county water district, sought review, contending that the trial court erred in issuing the writ of mandate postponing the election and in failing to require that certain indispensable parties be named and served in the case. The court affirmed, holding that appellant did not show that the classification served a compelling governmental interest, the arguments concerning the postponement of the election were moot, and the interests of the indispensable parties were adequately represented.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that held that the statute that limited voting to one landowner per parcel of land violated the equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The court found that the statute did to serve a compelling governmental interest.

 

1357 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Pitts


No. F006225


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 606; 273 Cal. Rptr. 757; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1004



September 5, 1990  
September 5, 1990


NOTICE:

[EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 3 OF 3. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied November 28, 1990. Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5479 


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from judgments of the Superior Court of Kern County Super. Ct. Nos. 27641, 27774 and 28244. Gary T. Friedman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed. The trial court is directed to enter its order dismissing the following counts: Counts 11, 13, 16, 20, 24, 27, 29, 31, 37, 39, 44, 51, 52, and 53 of Second Amended Information No. 27641; Counts 4, 11, 12, 13, 16, 19, 20, 22, 24, 25, 27, 37, 39, 42, 43, 44, 45, 47, 49, 50, 51, 52, 53, 55, 57, and 58 of Second Amended Information No. 27774; Counts 4, 10, 18, 20, 24, 29, and 31 of Amended Information No. 28244.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged orders of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted them of conspiracy, forcible lewd and lascivious acts on children under the age of 14, use of children for purposes of pornography, child endangerment, and assault. Defendants contended that prosecutorial misconduct, trial court misconduct, and biased evidentiary rulings had combined to deprive them of a fair trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought review of their convictions for conspiracy, forcible lewd and lascivious acts on children under the age of 14, use of children for purposes of pornography, child endangerment, and assault. Defendants alleged numerous instances of prosecutorial misconduct, trial court misconduct, and biased evidentiary rulings. The court stated that the overzealous prosecutors who, in their blind quest to convict, forgot or ignored their constitutional and ethical duties and were guilty of gross misconduct. The court found that the trial court's failure or refusal to rule on most of defendants' objections to the misconduct or to curb the prosecutors' behavior in any way had compounded the prejudicial effect of the prosecutors' misconduct. The court concluded that the sheer volume of questionable evidentiary rulings indicated that the strength of the state's evidence had probably not succeeded in overcoming the prejudicial effect of the misconduct. Therefore, the court reversed the convictions of defendants, ruling that the numerous instances of misconduct and judicial error had combined to deprive defendants of a fair trial.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions for conspiracy, forcible lewd and lascivious acts on children under the age of 14, use of children for purposes of pornography, child endangerment, and assault were reversed. The court found that defendants had been denied a fair trial due to numerous acts of both prosecutorial and judicial misconduct during the proceedings.

 

1358 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Knight v. Board of Administration


No. C005934, No. C005622.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 527; 273 Cal. Rptr. 120; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 949



September 4, 1990 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 20, 1990 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5674.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Proceeding; applications for peremptory writs of mandate. Writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the recipients of retirement allowances, sought a peremptory writ of mandate from the Court of Appeals of California, Third Appellate District, asking the court to determine the validity of Cal. Const. art. III, § 7, which dealt with the retirement benefits of elected state officials.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, the recipients of retirement allowances, filed for a writs of mandate asking the court to determine the validity and application of Cal. Const. art. III, § 7, a legislative constitutional amendment that was added to the California Constitution when the people passed proposition 57. Proposition 57 reduced the retirement benefits of elected state constitutional officers. Prior to the passage of proposition 57, certain former elected state constitutional officers, or their survivors or beneficiaries, had received retirement allowances that, in effect, included double cost-of-living increases. Petitioners requested the writ alleging that the amendment was an unconstitutional infringement of their vested contractual rights in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl.1, and that the amendment did not apply to the petitioners that had both served as members of the legislature and as elected constitutional officers. The court denied the writs and held that proposition 57 was valid, that it did not impair any contractual rights of petitioners in violation U.S. Const. art. III, § 7, and that it applied to all of the petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petitions for writ of mandate because after considering proposition 57 from several perspectives, the court concluded that the proposition was valid and did not violate the contract clause of the United States Constitution. In addition, the court concluded that proposition 57 applied to all of petitioner recipients, even those who had served both as members of the legislature and as elected constitutional officers.
