Rider v. County of San Diego


No. E006999.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


230 Cal. App. 3d 1676; 272 Cal. Rptr. 857; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 960



September 4, 1990, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing September 28, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1069; Respondents' petition for review GRANTED November 15, 1990, Reported at: 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5190. (See 1 Cal. 4th 1)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 194690, Gordon R. Burkhart, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of August 30, 1990, previously Reported at 223 Cal. App. 3d 415.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, county and agency, appealed the order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found in plaintiff taxpayers' favor and declared that a sales tax to finance justice facilities violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 (Proposition 13).


OVERVIEW: A bare majority of defendant county's voters approved a sales tax ordinance, Proposition A, which was adopted by defendant agency to finance the construction and operation of county justice facilities. Plaintiff taxpayers challenged the validity of the tax. The trial court declared that the tax violated the requirement of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4 (Proposition 13), that local special taxes be approved by a two-thirds vote. Defendants, county and agency, appealed. The court reversed, holding that the tax was valid. The court held that defendant agency did not have the power to levy a property tax and was not a special district subject to § 4. The court held that Cal. Gov't. Code § 53722 (Proposition 62), if found applicable, would constitute a referendum on local taxing power that violated Cal. Const. art. II, §§ 9(a) and 11, and that could not be permitted to invalidate the tax or Proposition A. The court held that the invalid requirement in § 53722 was expressly severable and did not necessitate the rejection of the entire San Diego County Regional Justice Facility Financing Act, Cal. Gov't. Code §§ 26250-26285.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for plaintiff taxpayers and upheld the sales tax ordinance in favor of defendants, county and agency, because there was no constitutionally valid requirement that the tax be approved by an extraordinary majority of voters.

 

1360 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Firestone Tire & Rubber Co. v. County of Monterey


No. H004193


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


223 Cal. App. 3d 382; 272 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 934



August 31, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 81738, John N. Anton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the judgment upholding the Monterey County Assessment Appeals Board's valuation of Firestone Tire and Rubber Company's Salinas plant for tax purposes for the 1980 tax year is affirmed; that part of the judgment remanding for an appropriate reduction in valuation to reflect the cost of toxic cleanup is reversed. Each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), that upheld defendant county's valuation of plaintiff's property for tax purposes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant county valued plaintiff property owner's manufacturing plant based upon its use as an operating industrial facility. Plaintiff closed the plant and sought review of the assessment, contending that defendant should have been aware of the closure and that the cost of hazardous waste cleanup at the site should be deducted from the property value for tax purposes. The county assessment appeals board upheld the property tax valuation, but rejected the argument that the assessment should be reduced due to cleanup costs. Plaintiff filed a complaint for the recovery of taxes, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5140. The trial court held that substantial evidence supported the property tax valuation, but not the board's finding regarding cleanup costs. Both parties sought review. The court affirmed in part and held that the valuation was supported by substantial evidence. However, the court reversed the part of the judgment remanding for an appropriate reduction in valuation to reflect the cost of toxic cleanup because the weight of the evidence supported the conclusion that a potential purchaser would not have been aware of the contamination at the time of the assessment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the portion of the trial court's judgment that upheld defendant county's valuation of plaintiff property owner's land because it was supported by substantial evidence. The court reversed the portion of the trial court's judgment that remanded the case for a determination as to the costs of toxic cleanup because plaintiff was not entitled to a reduction in the assessment value due to cleanup costs.

 

1361 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Britt v. City of Pomona


No. B041918


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


223 Cal. App. 3d 265; 272 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 933



August 30, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied November 28, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC66108, Thomas F. Nuss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed, and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinions expressed herein. Costs on appeal to plaintiffs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed plaintiffs' action challenging a tax ordinance as unconstitutional after it sustained defendant city's demurrers without leave to amend on all causes of action in plaintiffs' complaint.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city issued an ordinance that imposed a municipal tax on persons living in transient accommodations regardless of the length of time a taxpayer lived in or intended to live in the transient living quarters. Plaintiff taxpayers sought to invalidate the amended ordinance as unconstitutional. The court reversed the order which dismissed plaintiffs' action and held that the amended ordinance violated state and federal equal protection requirements and was unconstitutional because it taxed persons who resided only in certain designated types of shelter, like hotels, but not persons who resided in others such as apartments, houses, and boarding homes and because it permitted some transients to entirely escape the tax. The court found that there was no rational basis for the sub-classification of persons living in transient-type accommodations. The court also found that the subclassification was not rationally related to the achievement of legitimate government objectives because the tax paid for services for all residents who occupied housing not owned by them, whether they lived in transient housing or not, but was imposed only on only a portion of those people.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of dismissal of plaintiff taxpayers' action which alleged that an ordinance was unconstitutional because there was no rational basis for the subclassification of people living in transient accommodations. The court held that the ordinance violated state and federal equal protection laws because it taxed only the subclassification although the tax paid for services to all people who lived in housing not owned by them.

 

1362 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Lillebo v. Davis


No. C006009


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


222 Cal. App. 3d 1421; 272 Cal. Rptr. 638; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 880



August 20, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 30, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 311329, Ronald B. Robie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which ruled that various organized labor statutes were constitutional and did not violate appellants' U.S. Const. Amend. I rights. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the state. The state supreme court had remanded the court's previous opinion.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employees brought suit against respondent state, which challenged the ability of the legislature to allow recognized employee organizations, to exact any fair share fee from nonunion members of the represented unit who desire to exercise their statutory right to represent themselves individually in their employment relations with the state. Appellants raised facial constitutional challenges to the mechanism by which objecting nonunion members of the represented unit may seek the return of that portion of their fair share fee and to the designation as nonrefundable the costs of support of lobbying activities designed to foster policy goals and collective negotiations. The court found that employees exercising the right of individual representation did not usurp the authority of the union to act as the authorized representative of the unit. Thus, appellants could lawfully be compelled to support union efforts on their behalf. Also, the statutory authorization under Cal. Gov't Code § 3515.8 to lobby for policy goals, properly construed, was limited to activities germane to collective negotiations, contract administration, and employment benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which ruled that the challenged statutes were constitutional and did not violate appellant employees' rights under U.S. Const. Amend. I. The court found that employees exercising the right of individual representation did not usurp the authority of the union to act as the authorized representative of the unit. Thus, appellants could lawfully be compelled to support union efforts on their behalf.

 

1363 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



County of Sonoma v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. A047126


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


222 Cal. App. 3d 1133; 272 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 869; 55 Cal. Comp. Cases 286



August 14, 1990 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county challenged an order from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), that denied reconsideration of the decision of a workers' compensation judge that respondent municipal court judge was an employee of petitioner and not of the state.


OVERVIEW: Respondent municipal court judge filed a claim with respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board and received a determination that he was an employee of petitioner county, not of the state. Petitioner filed a request for consideration that was denied, and the supreme court granted review and ordered the matter returned to the court. On review, the court annulled the decision of respondent board, holding that for the purpose of workers' compensation benefits, a municipal judge was an employee of the state, not of the county where he or she sat. The essential characteristics of the employment relationship were the right to control and direct the activities of the person rendering service or the manner and method in which the work was performed. The court found that under the California constitution and statutes, the state retained the authority to administer, control, direct, censure, discipline, remove, retire, and admonish judges of the municipal courts. There were no provisions delegating any authority for these functions to the various counties in which municipal court judges sat. Thus, municipal judges were employees of the state for the purpose of workers' compensation.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision of a workers' compensation judge that respondent municipal court judge was an employee of petitioner county and not of the state because under the California constitution and statutes, the state retained the authority to direct and control the activities of municipal judges.

 

1364 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Aiu Ins. Co. v. Gillespie


No. B045007


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


222 Cal. App. 3d 1155; 272 Cal. Rptr. 334; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 849



August 14, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 30, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C724177, Miriam A. Vogel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent shall recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurer appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent insurance commissioner to set aside a decision which concluded that plaintiff failed to comply with the restrictions on the nonrenewal of policies contained in Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.03(c).


OVERVIEW: After respondent insurance commissioner ruled that plaintiff insurer failed to comply with the restrictions on the nonrenewal of policies contained in Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.03(c), and the trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate seeking to compel to set aside respondent's decision, plaintiff sought review. The court affirmed. The court determined that application of the nonrenewal restriction to the subject policies was not an unconstitutional impairment of contract. Plaintiff's claim that it was subjected to an ex post facto law was unfounded. Respondent simply ordered plaintiff to cure its violation of that section by rescinding its invalid nonrenewal notices. The court ruled that plaintiff's assertion that the nonrenewal of a policy was complete once the notice was mailed was not well-founded. The court explained that until the existing coverage expired, nonrenewal was incomplete because a contractual relationship still existed and the policy was in full force and effect. Thus, simply because a notice of nonrenewal was mailed prior to the election did not preclude application of the statute to the subject policies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the writ of mandate because application of the statutory nonrenewal restriction to the subject policies was not an unconstitutional impairment of plaintiff insurer's contract rights.

 

1365 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Billig v. Voges


No. B044211


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


223 Cal. App. 3d 962; 273 Cal. Rptr. 91; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 965



August 13, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for Publication September 7, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 66404, Harry E. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens challenged the denial by the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) of their petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent city clerk to process their referendum petition and submit it to the city council.


OVERVIEW: A city council adopted an ordinance encompassing a rezoning measure approving mixed-use development of certain city-owned property in the downtown area. Appellant citizens submitted their referendum petition opposing the ordinance to respondent city clerk for processing. Appellants did not include in their petition various sections of the ordinance, and respondent rejected the petition for failure to comply with Cal. Elec. Code § 4052, which required that such petitions contain the text of the ordinance or portion thereof that was the subject of the referendum. The trial court denied appellants' petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent to process the petition. The court on appeal affirmed the denial. The defective form of appellants' petition violated the purpose of § 4052 because it did not furnish the information the statute required, i.e., the text or language of the challenged law. The offices of city clerks were mandated by the constitution to implement and enforce the statute's procedural requirements. Respondent had the clear and present ministerial duty to refuse to process appellants' petition because it did not comply with the procedural requirements of § 4052.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant citizens' petition for writ of mandate because it was respondent city clerk's duty to reject the petition for failure to comply with the procedural requirements for referendum petitions.

 

1366 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Vehicular Residents Ass'n v. Agnos


No. A045105


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


222 Cal. App. 3d 996; 272 Cal. Rptr. 216; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 859



August 6, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 894135, Richard P. Figone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought reversal of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted judgment on the pleadings to appellees, municipality and municipal officials, in appellants' equal protection challenge to a municipal ordinance banning the use of motor vehicles for human habitation within the city limits during overnight hours.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged a trial judge's order granting judgment on the pleadings to appellees, municipality and municipal officials, in appellants' lawsuit seeking to overturn a municipal statute. The court affirmed. Appellees had adopted a city ordinance prohibiting the occupancy of motor homes overnight within city limits. Appellants brought a declaratory judgment action, arguing the statute unconstitutionally violated equal protection rights, inasmuch as the statute discriminated against poor people unable to afford better housing. The court held that the ordinance was not unconstitutional inasmuch as it did not discriminate between poor and nonpoor. Rather, the court held, it barred both the poor and nonpoor from living in such housing during overnight hours.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment in favor of appellees, municipality and municipal officials, was affirmed. The municipal ordinance prohibiting residence in motor homes during overnight hours was not violative of equal protection, as it did not discriminate between the poor and the nonpoor where it barred both groups equally from residing in such vehicles.

 

1367 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. State Bd. of Accountancy


No. A046279.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three.


1 Cal. App. 4th 1305; 272 Cal. Rptr. 108; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 818



August 1, 1990 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified August 29, 1990.
Review Granted October 18, 1990 (S017399) (See 2 Cal. 4th 999).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 863037, Thomas J. Dandurand, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of August 1, 1990, previously Reported at 222 Cal. App. 3d 919 and 230 Cal. App. 3d 877.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a non-licensed individual and association of accountants, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted defendant State Board of Accountancy an injunction restraining plaintiffs from referring to themselves or their services as "accountant," "accounting," and "accounting services."


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, unlicensed individual and association of accountants, sought review of the lower court's grant of an injunction to defendant State Board of Accountancy restraining them from referring to themselves or their services as "accountant," and "accounting services," claiming defendant could not constitutionally prohibit plaintiffs from using those terms. Plaintiffs were not licensed as public accountants and defendant alleged those terms would mislead or confuse the public. The court found that other cases involving the Accountancy Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5000 et seq. had not considered the constitutional limitations in enjoining unlicensed persons from calling themselves accountants. Here, an independent survey was admitted into evidence showing more than 50 percent surveyed believed the terms "accountant" or "accounting services" required a license, therefore, the court held the terms standing alone were misleading and could not be utilized by unlicensed persons without a modifier, qualifier, or disclaimer. The court reversed and remanded holding the injunction must be modified to prohibit only the use of the terms "accountant" or "accounting" without a modifier.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded. Plaintiffs, an individual and association of accountants, could not be absolutely enjoined from using the terms "accountant" and "accounting services," because the use of those terms by unlicensed individuals or organizations had to be permitted if qualified by a warning or disclaimer stating that they were not licensed or that the services did not require a license.

 

1368 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Tibbetts v. Van De Kamp


No. A043289


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


222 Cal. App. 3d 389; 271 Cal. Rptr. 792; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 811



July 27, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 18, 1990. Lucas, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 634742-2, Robert K. Byers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, attorney general, manager of the state gaming registration program, district attorney, and chief of police, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which ruled in favor of respondent card room owner, finding that Texas Hold'em did not fall within the definition of stud-horse poker proscribed by Cal. Penal Code § 330.


OVERVIEW: Respondent card room owner filed a complaint for declaratory relief after appellants, attorney general, manager of the state gaming registration program, district attorney, and chief of police, informed respondent that the poker game known as Texas Hold'em was proscribed by Cal. Penal Code § 330 as a form of stud-horse poker. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent. Appellants sought review, arguing that there were only two broad categories of poker, draw poker with no exposed cards and stud poker with exposed cards, and that § 330's prohibition of stud-horse poker made illegal all forms of poker except draw poker. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that there were several different recognized categories of poker, each having its own distinct format and strategy, and that stud poker was but one such category. The court held that by its own explicit terms, § 330 proscribed stud poker and no other category of poker games. The court further found that Texas Hold'em did not fall within the stud poker category. Therefore, the court held that Texas Hold'em was not a form of stud-horse poker proscribed by § 330.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found in favor of respondent card room owner, finding that Texas Hold'em did not fall within the definition of proscribed stud-horse poker. The court found that stud poker was but one category of different recognized categories of poker, that the statute only proscribed stud poker, and that Texas Hold'em did not fall within the stud poker category and, thus, was not a prohibited game.

 

1369 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Taylor


No. H006114


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


222 Cal. App. 3d 612; 271 Cal. Rptr. 785; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 765



July 26, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 15, 1990, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 11, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. CR 2348, Thomas A. Black, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which denied his motion to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(m). Defendant asserted that a search by private security guards which yielded LSD and marijuana was illegal.


OVERVIEW: Asserting that a search by private security guards which yielded LSD and marijuana was illegal, defendant appealed from the denial of his motion to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(m). Defendant argued that since private individuals may not search each other for contraband under California law, the evidence was illegally seized and should be excluded. Second, he argued that since the security guards were private individuals who arrested him without complying with statutes defining how private citizens may exercise the power to arrest, the court should refuse to give binding legal effect to his consent. The court rejected defendant's arguments. The court held that the protection of U.S. Const. amend. IV did not extend to searches carried out by private persons. The court also held that the facts presented by defendant failed to establish that the state had established such an interdependence with the security guards involved in this case that it must be recognized as a joint participant in the challenged activity. The court held that substantial evidence supported the finding of the trial court. Accordingly, the court affirmed the decision of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied defendant's motion to suppress. The court held that the protection of the Fourth Amendment did not extend to searches carried out by private persons, that the facts presented by defendant failed to establish that the state must be recognized as a joint participant in the challenged activity, and that substantial evidence supported the finding of the trial court.

 

1370 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. O'Keefe


No. D008658


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


222 Cal. App. 3d 517; 271 Cal. Rptr. 769; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 776



July 25, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976.1 and 976(b), this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I, II, III and V.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied August 20, 1990, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 30, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR93206, Charles R. Hayes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The sentence on count 1 (battery) is stayed. The conviction on count 5 (first degree burglary) is reduced to second degree burglary. Counts 11, 12, 14, 15, 17, 18, 22, 23, 24, 25, 27, 32, 36, and 37 are reversed for insufficiency of evidence and the trial court is instructed to enter a judgment of acquittal on those counts. The remaining judgment is affirmed. The case is remanded for resentencing in compliance with this decision, and the trial court is directed to file an amended abstract judgment and forward it to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of multiple counts of burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant had entered various dormitory rooms of a female residence hall where he stole pictures and sexually assaulted a woman.


OVERVIEW: After defendant entered into a female dormitory house for the purpose of stealing photographs and assaulting a resident, he was convicted of multiple counts of burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant appealed, contending that the dormitory rooms within the residence hall were not separate dwellings within the meaning of § 459 and that there was insufficient evidence to show separate entries in support of the multiple burglary convictions. The court disagreed and affirmed all but one of the burglary convictions, holding that individual student dormitory rooms were separate inhabited dwellings for the purpose of § 459 and that unauthorized entry into each room presented a separate danger to each of the occupants and a separate burglary violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed all but one of defendant's multiple burglary convictions because the unauthorized entry into each separate dormitory room of the residence hall constituted a separate offense because each room was a separate dwelling for the purpose of the burglary statute.

 

1371 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reynolds


No. E006889.


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two.


222 Cal. App. 3d 458; 271 Cal. Rptr. 522; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 773



July 23, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review DENIED October 11, 1990 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports, (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4512.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, Nos. 28763 and 29954, Patrick F. Magers, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which denied defendant's motion to withdraw and change defendant's guilty plea.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to the manufacture and possession of methamphetamine. A trial court denied defendant's motion to withdraw and change his guilty plea. Defendant appealed the trial court's judgment. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court found that defendant's agreement did not require defendant to testify in any particular way, and thus did not create the danger of perjury. The court determined that there was no per se prohibition against a police agreement to pay an informant for undercover services upon a condition that the informant testify at a criminal trial. The court concluded that the plea agreement, which called for information, not testimony, did not contravene public policy. Thus, the plea agreement was valid and enforceable. The court determined that any Marsden error, which did not go to the legality of the proceedings resulting in the plea was waived by defendant's guilty plea. Because defendant did not assert that his guilty plea was not made voluntarily and intelligently, nor contend that he received inappropriate advice from his attorney concerning the plea agreement, defendant was foreclosed from raising the alleged Marsden error on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a trial court's judgment denying defendant's motion to withdraw and change his guilty plea because the plea agreement was valid and enforceable where it did not contravene public policy and did not create a danger of perjury. A police agreement to pay an informant for undercover services was not pro se prohibited. Defendant waived any Marsden error, which did not go to the legality of the proceedings, by his guilty plea.

 

1372 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Alcala


No. A043385


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


222 Cal. App. 3d 345; 271 Cal. Rptr. 674; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 758



July 23, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 22, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 117925, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is vacated, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to deny the petition for relief and to discharge the order to show cause.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison warden challenged the order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which enjoined enforcement of restrictions on the right of prisoners to possess and wear certain items of civilian clothing.


OVERVIEW: In response to attempted and successful prison escapes, appellant prison warden restricted the items of civilian clothing that the prisoners in the main institution were entitled to possess and wear. Appellee inmate filed an application on behalf of himself and all others similarly situated seeking judicial invalidation of appellant's deletions from the approved list of clothing on the ground that they violated prisoners' constitutional and statutory rights to personal expression through clothing choice. The trial court found that appellant did not consider the alternative of tightening security measures, which would have preserved prisoners' rights to personal clothing and, thus, preserved the human dignity of each prisoner. The trial court held that the revised rules were invalid under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2600, 2601 because they were an impermissible infringement upon prisoners' constitutional right to personal expression. The court reversed because restrictions were not an unconstitutional infringement on the right of prisoners to self-expression through clothing choice, and they did not violate prisoners' statutory rights as they were a valid response to prison escapes.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the trial court that enjoined appellant prison warden from enforcing restrictions regarding prisoners ability to wear certain types of clothing because appellant's clothing restrictions did not violate the constitutional or statutory rights of the prisoners and were permissible.

 

1373 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Alicia T. v. County of Los Angeles


No. B042169


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


222 Cal. App. 3d 869; 271 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 1128



July 23, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 10, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SCC 15477, Arthur H. Jean, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The T. family to bear costs on appeal. Benjamin P. Wasserman, counsel for the T. family, is ordered to pay sanctions of $ 750 forthwith to the clerk of this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, minor and parents, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a judgment of dismissal in favor of respondents, county and social workers, in appellants' suit for damages against respondents resulting from respondents' conduct of dependency proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, minor and parents, brought an action for damages against respondents, county and social workers, under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging that respondents wrongfully removed appellant minor from the custody of appellant parents upon a misdiagnosis of sexual abuse, and further alleging that respondents kept her from the custody of appellant parents by placing her in foster care for seven weeks. The trial court entered judgment ordering the action dismissed without leave to amend. Appellants sought review of the decision. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that respondent social workers enjoyed absolute immunity from suits alleging the improper investigation of child abuse, removal of a minor from the parental home based upon suspicion of abuse, and the instigation of dependency proceedings. The court held that because a local government could not be sued under § 1983 for an injury caused solely by its employees or agents, and appellants had not alleged an injury inflicted by the execution of respondent county's policies, appellants had failed to state a cause of action against respondent county.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court dismissing an action for damages by appellants, minor and parents, against respondents, county and social workers, arising out of respondents' conduct of a dependency proceeding, because respondent social workers enjoyed absolute immunity in such cases and respondent county could not be sued in such cases for injuries caused solely by its employees.

People v. Harlan


No. E006499


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


222 Cal. App. 3d 439; 271 Cal. Rptr. 653; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 780



July 23, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied October 17, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. ICR 11960, James H. Herrin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), after a jury found defendant guilty of committing a lewd act with a child under 14 in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a), and found that he had engaged in substantial sexual conduct as referenced in Cal. Penal Code § 1203.066(a)(8). Defendant claimed error in the admission of evidence and testimony, and in failing to instruct the jury on a lesser offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of committing a lewd act with a child under 14, and was found to have engaged in substantial sexual conduct. The court ruled that California law did not require corroboration of the testimony of a child sexual abuse victim, that the victim was qualified to testify notwithstanding his age, and that the victim's testimony was sufficient evidence to convict defendant. The court found that proof of penetration or of ejaculation was not required in a sexual assault case, and that evidence of the victim's past sexual conduct was properly excluded as irrelevant. The court sustained the judgment of the trial court, and ruled that the testimony of a previously undisclosed expert was admissible on rebuttal. The court held that to give an instruction on a lesser offense, there must have been some basis, other than an unexplainable rejection of prosecution evidence, on which the jury could have found the offense to be less than that charged. The court found that there was no lesser offense, because if the jury did not believe the testimony of the victim, it should have acquitted defendant.


OUTCOME: The court determined that no error was committed by the trial court in its admission of certain evidence and in its selection of jury instructions. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for committing a lewd act with a child under 14.

Searle v. City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Bd.


No. A044761.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five.


222 Cal. App. 3d 131; 271 Cal. Rptr. 437; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 746



July 18, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied October 25, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4918.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 638429-8, Mark L. Eaton, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenge a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) denying them a peremptory writ of mandate, thus affirming the city rent stabilization board's decision on their petition for an individual rent ceiling adjustment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlords challenged the trial court's refusal to grant a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus, claiming that the net operating income inflation-indexing regulation, regulation 1264, was unconstitutional. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that the rent control regulations violated due process because they provided an inflation adjustment of net income by an arbitrary percentage of the increase in the consumer price index, prescribed an inflation adjustment of net income only from the middle of the year after net income was frozen to the month before a landlord applies for the adjustment, required each landlord to file and pursue an individual application to obtain the uniform inflation adjustment, and failed to establish guidelines as to what constituted a fair return on investment. The court remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandate ordering the city rent stabilization board to conform regulations 1264 and 1275 to the principles set forth in its opinion and setting aside the challenged decision, adjust appellants' rents accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandate ordering appellee city rent stabilization board to conform its regulation limiting inflation indexing of net operating income and its regulation limiting a fair return on investment to the principles set forth in its opinion, and setting aside the challenged decision, adjust appellants' rents accordingly.

Lund v. Cal. State Employees Ass'n


No. C004052


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


222 Cal. App. 3d 174; 271 Cal. Rptr. 425; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 748



July 18, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rule 976.1 of the California Rules of Court, the Reporter of Decisions is directed to publish all portions of this opinion except parts IV, V and VI of the Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified July 24, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 351927, James L. Long, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed only to the extent it sustained without leave to amend Lund's administrative mandamus claim for promotion, as discussed in section V of this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents are awarded their costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee sought review of an order by the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California), which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate and granted respondent state personnel departments a demurrer as to appellant's claims for denial of wage compensation. Appellant claimed that he was not compensated for work performed at a higher civil service level.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state employee claimed that he was performing duties at a higher civil service level but was not compensated, so appellant filed a grievance requesting a wage settlement for the out-of -class work. The grievance was denied, and appellant sought review of the denial to respondent department of personnel administration, which also denied the claim. Appellant then petitioned for writ of mandamus with the trial court. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer, holding that appellant had not exhausted his remedies and that out-of-class compensation claims did not invoke the merit principle as set out in Cal. Const. art VII, §§ 1-3. Appellant sought review, arguing that respondent department of personnel administration lacked jurisdiction. The court affirmed, holding that judicial review of appellant's claims was precluded because appellant had not exhausted his administrative remedies. The court also held that respondent department of personnel administration was explicitly granted jurisdiction to hear out-of-class compensation claims by Cal. Gov't Code § 19818.16, and that the statute was not unconstitutional because it was only a personnel administration procedure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of judgment to respondents, state personnel departments and state employees association, holding that appellant state employee was not entitled to a writ of mandamus compelling respondents to compensate him because he had not exhausted all administrative remedies. The court additionally held that the statute dealing with out-of-class-compensation claims did not infringe upon the civil service merit principle.

People v. Alvas


No. C005511


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


221 Cal. App. 3d 1459; 271 Cal. Rptr. 131; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 716



July 6, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 3338C, J. Kenneth Ferguson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order recommitting the defendant) is reversed, and the matter is remanded for proceedings consistent with our opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which found defendant to be mentally retarded and a danger to himself and others pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 6500,and involuntarily committed defendant for one year.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to be mentally retarded and a danger to himself and others and involuntarily committed. On appeal, the court reversed the order recommitting defendant and remanded for further proceedings, holding that due process and equal protection required that the trial record show defendant was advised of the right to a jury trial before adjudicating a Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500 proceeding because defendant was being deprived of personal liberty, and there was no compelling reason for the statutorily provided disparate treatment between two classes of persons with mental deficiencies on the advisement issue. The court declined to make the advisement requirement retroactive as retroactivity would have seriously disturbed the administration of justice. The court found that § 6500's failure to define "dangerousness" for all individuals within its scope was not fatal to its constitutionality given that § 6500 could have utilized the meaning of "danger" from another statute enacted for a similar purpose. The court ruled that the issue of the sufficiency of the evidence for commitment was moot as the periods for the holding and recommitment orders had expired.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's recommitment order and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court found that the equal protection and due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions were violated by the failure to advise defendant of his right to a trial by jury during defendant's involuntary commitment proceedings which found him to be mentally retarded and a danger to himself and others.

Guardianship of Ethan S.


No. A041445


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


221 Cal. App. 3d 1403; 271 Cal. Rptr. 121; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 722



July 5, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certification for Publication August 2, 1990.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 20, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 247906 and 874023, Ina Levin Gyemant, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant putative father sought review in a consolidated Uniform Parentage Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 7000 et seq., and guardianship proceeding of the orders of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent natural father and terminated appellant's guardianship.


OVERVIEW: The wife of appellant putative father had an affair with respondent natural father, resulting in a child. Respondent filed a complaint under the Uniform Parentage Act (UPA) seeking sole custody as the natural father and a determination that appellant was not the legal father. Appellant applied for guardianship. Respondent's summary judgment motion was granted and the guardianship terminated. On appeal, the court affirmed both orders because appellant was estopped from arguing he was the legal father, and appellant abandoned his guardianship appeal. To determine whether appellant was entitled to appointed counsel in the UPA action, the court balanced the interests involved, the possible consequences of an erroneous adjudication, and the nature of the proceedings against the state's interests. None of these factors supported a right to counsel finding. The court affirmed the grant of respondent's summary judgment motion, finding that appellant was estopped from making parental claims because at all times preceding the UPA action, appellant told the child and others that respondent was the natural father, and the child believed appellant and formed parental bonds with respondent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders terminating appellant putative father's guardianship and granting respondent natural father's summary judgment motion in a consolidated Uniform Parentage Act proceeding. The court ruled that appellant abandoned his guardianship challenge as he failed to argue it. The court found that appellant's parental claims were estopped because appellant had acted and told others that respondent was the natural father.

 

1379 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Porter


No. A045382


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


221 Cal. App. 3d 1213; 270 Cal. Rptr. 773; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 719



June 29, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 26, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 15667C, John J. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), convicting him of two counts of first degree burglary, one count of auto theft, and one prior prison term allegation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree burglary, one count of auto theft, and one prior prison term allegation. He claimed that the trial court committed reversible error by admitting his confession which was improperly obtained after he invoked his Miranda rights. The court reversed and held that if an individual indicated in any manner, at any time prior to or during questioning, that he wished to remain silent, the interrogation must cease. Defendant indicated early on during the interview with detectives, after he was advised he would be extradited to California, that he would "save it for when I get there," yet the interview continued, and he finally confessed. The court ruled that the detectives had not "scrupulously" honored defendant's request to terminate the interrogation, and therefore the confession violated Miranda. Under California law, improper introduction of a confession constituted reversible error per se, even though federal law allowed application of the harmless error standard, which in defendant's case, would have resulted in defendant's conviction notwithstanding the illegal confession.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions because detectives violated his Miranda rights in continuing to question him after he indicated he no longer wished to talk. The error was reversible per se as a matter of California law.

Bullock v. City and County of San Francisco


Nos. A043073, A044386, A046047


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


221 Cal. App. 3d 1072; 271 Cal. Rptr. 44; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 703



June 28, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of respondent City and County of San Francisco for review by the Supreme Court was denied September 20, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 809721 and 898704, John E. Dearman, Lucy Kelly McCabe and Stuart R. Pollack, Judges.



DISPOSITION: In Nos. A044386 and A046047, the order granting the preliminary injunction, and the order denying plaintiff's motion to modify the injunction, are reversed. Plaintiff shall recover his costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a judgment, preliminary injunction, and several orders in favor of defendants, entered in the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), asserting that the superior court abused its discretion in issuing an injunction restraining him from renting rooms to tourists except during a designated tourist season, and then refusing to dissolve the injunction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff owned residential hotel units that he wished to rent to tourists. Defendant city enacted the San Francisco Residential Hotel Unit Conversion and Demolition Ordinance, San Francisco Admin. Code ch. 41, in an attempt to limit the conversion of residential hotel units for use by tourists except during the tourist season. The ordinance provided that owners could not exit the residential housing business without obtaining a conversion permit, which was granted only if the owner made a one-for-one replacement of the units by certain methods. The trial court entered an injunction prohibiting plaintiff from using the units for tourists. The court affirmed the judgment against plaintiff except on his claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, finding that although his pleading on this claim may have been insufficient, a consideration of the entire record cured any problems. The court reversed the injunction, finding the ordinance preempted by the Ellis Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 7060-7060.7. Because the ordinance required landlords to do more than was required under the Ellis Act to get out of the residential housing business, it was invalid as applied to that extent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against plaintiff, except to the extent it held that plaintiff had no civil rights damages action because any defect in plaintiff's pleading on this issue was cured by the entire record. However, the court reversed the order granting the injunction and the other orders, finding the city ordinance prohibiting landlords from going out of the residential housing business preempted and partially invalid.
