Canyons Neighborhood Ass'n v. Monterey Peninsula Regional Park Dist.


No. H005929.


Court of Appeal of California, Sixth Appellate District


221 Cal. App. 3d 1033; 270 Cal. Rptr. 885; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 670



June 27, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for review DENIED September 13, 1990 Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4501.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]  

Superior Court of Monterey County, No. M 20783, William M. Wunderlich, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, neighborhood association and individual, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandate and dismissed their case against respondents, park district and manager, in an action challenging a resolution to purchase land for a park.


OVERVIEW: The board of directors of respondents, park district and manager, passed resolution number 88-05 which declared the purpose of respondents to purchase a parcel of land and specified the ways and means of accomplishing that purpose. After a public hearing, the board of directors passed resolution number 89-03 authorizing respondents to purchase the land. Appellants, neighborhood association and individual, sought a writ of mandate to order repeal of resolution number 89-03 or its submission to the voters. The trial court denied the petition for a writ and dismissed the action, and the court affirmed holding that the resolution was an administrative act and not subject to referendum. The court held that resolution number 88-05 was the legislative act which could have been challenged by appellants but that resolution number 89-03 was merely an administrative act which implemented the already-declared policy of resolution number 88-05.


OUTCOME: Judgment denying appellants, neighborhood association and individual, their petition for writ of mandate and dismissing their case against respondents, park district and manager, upheld because the resolution passed by respondents' board of directors to buy land for a park was an administrative act and not subject to referendum.

 

1382 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Fort Emory Cove Boatowners Ass'n v. Cowett


No. D009997


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


221 Cal. App. 3d 508; 270 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 637



June 18, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 602984, Robert E. May, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff boatowners sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), dismissing on defendant attorneys' demurrer plaintiffs' complaint seeking to enjoin the San Diego Unified Port District from appointing defendants as deputy district attorneys to prosecute Port District ordinance violations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff boatowners challenged the trial court's dismissal on demurrer of plaintiffs' petition to enjoin defendant attorneys from being employed by the San Diego Unified Port District as deputy district attorneys to prosecute Port District ordinance violations. Plaintiffs objected to Port District ordinances adopted in 1986, which prohibited anchorages in certain parts of San Diego Bay. When plaintiffs failed to move their boats from the prohibited anchorages, plaintiffs were served with misdemeanor citations. In a separate civil action, plaintiffs had failed in their attempt to have the ordinances declared unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that the Port Authority was authorized by Cal. Harb. & Nav. Code § 22, 35, and 55 to regulate anchorages and enforce rules governing the use of navigable waters. Under Cal. Gov't Code § 24101, the district attorney was empowered to appoint defendants as deputies in order to discharge the district attorney's enforcement duties under Cal. Harb. & Nav. Code § 59. Therefore, the Port Authority was permitted to pay defendants in their capacity as deputy district attorneys. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff boat owners' suit to enjoin a port authority from paying defendant attorneys who had been appointed to serve as deputy district attorneys for the purpose of enforcing the port authority's anchorage ordinances, because both defendants' appointment and the port authority's reimbursement were authorized by statute.

 

1383 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Saille


No. F011046


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


229 Cal. App. 3d 1376; 270 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 627



June 14, 1990.


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for review GRANTED October 11, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4570. Rehearing Denied January 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 12650, George C. Barrett, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of June 14, 1990, previously Reported at 221 Cal. App. 3d 307.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Merced County (California) that convicted him for the second time of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 and attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664, 187, having found he used a firearm during the commission of each crime in violation of Cal. Penal Code §12022.5.


OVERVIEW: Intoxicated defendant killed one victim and attempted to kill a second individual. He was convicted of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 and attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664, 187 with a firearm in violation of Cal. Penal Code §12022.5 and, after an appeal, he was convicted a second time. On second appeal, the court affirmed and specified the conviction for attempted murder as second degree by operation of law, holding that voluntary intoxication was not available under current California law as a way to reduce murder to voluntary manslaughter. The court determined that, under Cal. Penal Code §§ 22, 25, and 29, the defense of diminished capacity had been abolished, returning California to a "strict mens rea" approach when dealing with voluntary intoxication. Therefore, the trial court was not required sua sponte to give instructions relating intoxication to each crime level. The court concluded that the state was not precluded by double jeopardy from using evidence of the second degree attempted murder to prove the first degree murder when the second degree was applied by operation of law under Cal. Penal Code § 1157, not by a factual jury acquittal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder and attempted murder and specified the attempted murder conviction as second degree by operation of law because voluntary intoxication was not available under current California law as a way to reduce murder to voluntary manslaughter.

 

1384 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Farajpour v. University of Southern California


No. B037903.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three.


221 Cal. App. 3d 19; 270 Cal. Rptr. 356; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 602



June 7, 1990.


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: September 13, 1990 Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4502.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C498789, Harold J. Ackerman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent filed suit against appellant university, alleging assault and battery, intentional infliction of emotional distress, negligence, and malicious prosecution Appellant moved for nonsuit and directed verdict on the malicious prosecution count. After denying appellant's motions, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered judgment in accordance with the jury's general verdict for respondent. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: After an altercation with appellant university's security guards, respondent was charged with criminal battery. However, the prosecution's motion to dismiss the case "in the interest of justice" was granted. Respondent then filed suit against appellant for assault and battery, intentional infliction of emotional distress, negligence, and malicious prosecution. After the trial court denied appellant's motions for a nonsuit and directed verdict, the jury returned a general verdict in respondent's favor. Appellant sought review. The court reversed and remanded, holding that appellant was entitled to a directed verdict on the malicious prosecution count because respondent did not show that the dismissal of the underlying criminal case was based on a determination of respondent's innocence. Nor could a favorable termination reflecting innocence be inferred from other elements of malicious prosecution. Moreover, error in instructing on malicious prosecution was prejudicial, which required retrial as to remaining counts. Furthermore, because evidence as to the remaining counts was conflicting, the general verdict could not be upheld. Rather, special verdicts or findings were required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a judgment for respondent in a civil suit, holding that the trial court erred in failing to enter a directed verdict for appellant university on the malicious prosecution count because respondent could not prove that the prosecution's dismissal of the underlying criminal case was due to respondent's innocence. Because the jury rendered a general verdict, a retrial was required as to remaining counts.

 

1385 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Henderson


No. D008371


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


220 Cal. App. 3d 1632; 270 Cal. Rptr. 248; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 589



June 5, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR90677, J. Perry Langford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the superior court with directions to vacate Henderson's guilty plea if he makes an appropriate motion within 30 days after this opinion becomes final. In that event, the court is directed to reinstate the original charges against Henderson, if the People so move, and to rehear the matter in light of this opinion. If no such motion is filed by Henderson, the trial court is directed to reinstate the original judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him upon his guilty plea of manufacturing methamphetamine in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379.6(a). Defendant alleged that the trial court improperly denied his pretrial motion to suppress evidence that was obtained through an unconstitutional search and seizure within the meaning of the U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for manufacturing methamphetamine (meth) under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379.6(a), arguing that videotape surveillance of and confiscation of items from the condominium where he manufactured the meth constituted an unconstitutional search and seizure and that a Drug Enforcement Agency (DEA) informant did not have nor reasonably appear to have authority to consent to the search. Although the informant was found to be a cotenant, the court noted that the evidence showed he telephoned and rang the doorbell before giving his consent when defendant and his associate were present. Under these circumstances, the court held that it was reasonable for defendant to entertain a subjective expectation of privacy in the premises. The court held that the communications recorded and transmitted to the DEA via the video cameras would have been valid only so long as the informant was a party to them, aural or visual. The court reversed and remanded the matter for the trial court to determine whether the consensual physical entry into the condominium was tainted by the earlier illegal video surveillance and, if so, what evidence had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for manufacturing methamphetamine and remanded the matter to the trial court to determine what evidence was tainted and had to be suppressed. The court held that the cotenant could only have consented to the warrantless videotape surveillance of the condominium so long as he was a party to it, aural or visual, because defendant entertained reasonable expectations of privacy in the premises.

 

1386 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Horan v. Cal.


No. C006113


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


220 Cal. App. 3d 1503; 270 Cal. Rptr. 194; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 582



June 4, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 28, 1990, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 22, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 349381, Rothwell B. Mason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lottery ticket retailer appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), finding that plaintiff was not entitled to the proceeds from an instant game lottery ticket which was stolen from her inventory by an employee who discovered it was a winner and then returned it to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff lottery ticket retailer's employee stole a lottery ticket, discovered it was a $ 100,000 winner, and gave it to his mother to redeem. The employee then returned the ticket to plaintiff, hoping plaintiff would not report the ticket as stolen. Plaintiff reported the ticket as stolen and then attempted to redeem it. Defendant California Lottery Commission refused to pay, and plaintiff filed a claim for damages with the State Board of Control and then with the trial court. The trial court found plaintiff was not entitled to the proceeds. The court affirmed, finding that the sine qua non entitling one to the proceeds of a winning lottery ticket was that the ticket was played in a manner consistent with fair chance. An understanding, agreement, or expectation that the distribution would be determined by chance was an essential element to the game of lottery. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that found that plaintiff lottery ticket retailer was not entitled to the proceeds from a lottery ticket which was stolen from her inventory by an employee and then returned. The court held that plaintiff did not play the winning ticket consistent with the principle of fair chance as required by the lottery commission's rules and the game of lottery itself.

 

1387 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Aden v. Lynch


No. B038046


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


220 Cal. App. 3d 1429; 270 Cal. Rptr. 174; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 580



May 31, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied August 16, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 3637.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C679188, R. William Schoettler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.

Respondent, Marian C. Aden, shall recover her costs on appeal, including reasonable attorney fees as costs pursuant to Revenue and Taxation Code section 5152.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant tax assessors appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted declaratory relief to plaintiff taxpayer by holding that only 50 percent of plaintiff's property was subject to reassessment for a change in ownership.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer owned half of a corporation that owned a building. The corporation conveyed an undivided one-half interest in the building each to plaintiff and a third party. Later that day, the third party conveyed his interest to plaintiff. Plaintiff sought declaratory relief from a reassessment, contending that only half of the property was subject to reassessment for change of ownership. The trial court found for plaintiff. On appeal, the court affirmed. Under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 1605.5, the trial court properly conducted a trial de novo because plaintiff challenged the method of valuation used to determine that a change in ownership occurred. Independent economic reasons for both transfers of the property made the step doctrine inapplicable. Defendant tax assessors' unwritten guideline, that the step transaction doctrine should apply to such transfers if both conveyances occurred less than six months apart, was arbitrary and was used in violation of Cal. Gov't. Code § 11347.5 because the guideline was unpublished. Attorney's fees were properly awarded pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5152.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a declaratory judgment against defendant tax assessors, which held that plaintiff taxpayer's building was only subject to a 50 percent reassessment for a change in ownership. The court concluded that the step doctrine was inapplicable and that plaintiff was entitled to attorney's fees under the governing statute.

 

1388 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Common Cause v. Fair Political Practices Com


No. C005458


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


221 Cal. App. 3d 647; 269 Cal. Rptr. 873; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 561



May 30, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 16, 1990.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied. Respondents and real parties in interest are to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner political action groups petitioned for a writ of mandate for a determination that Cal. Gov't Code § 85300, which prohibited the use of public money in political campaigns, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In a general election, voters approved two initiatives regarding public financing of elections. Since Proposition 73 which prohibited the use of public money in all political campaigns received the greater number of votes, under Cal. Const. art. II, § 10(b) that proposition prevailed. As a result a section was added to the Political Reform Act, Cal. Gov't. Code § 81000 et seq. The new section was Cal. Gov't Code § 853008(1988). Petitioner political action groups then filed a petition for writ of mandate, seeking a determination that Cal. Gov't. Code § 85300 was unconstitutional. The court denied the petition finding that § 8530 was enacted by vote of the majority of voters in an election. The California Constitution did not vest exclusive authority over the financing of political campaign in the legislature. Moreover, no express demands or prohibitions were made as a result of § 85300, it just forbid the expenditure or use of public monies for the purpose of seeking elective office.


OUTCOME: The court denied petition for writ of mandate filed by petitioner political action groups, which sought a finding that a statute that prohibited the use of public money for political campaigns was unconstitutional. The court found the statute was passed by a majority of voters, and the California Constitution did not prohibit the people from determining the issue of public subsidy of electoral office seekers.

 

1389 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sanders


No. B030989, No. B037473


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


220 Cal. App. 3d 1227; 271 Cal. Rptr. 534; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 570



May 29, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Opinion Previously Reported at this citation has been modified and reprinted, the new Opinion is Reported at: 221 Cal. App. 3d 350.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. B030989, Madge S. Watai, Judge.

Previously reported at 270 Cal. Rptr. 115.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied and the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury found him guilty of second degree murder, appellant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) contending that the trial court should have ordered a new trial once it learned that his brother had confessed to the crime. Appellant petitioned for habeas corpus on the ground that he was denied effective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of second degree murder in the shooting death of a victim related by marriage. On appeal, the court rejected appellant's claim that the trial court on its own motion should have ordered a new trial when it learned that appellant's brother had confessed to firing the fatal shot because the trial court lacked that authority and appellant's counsel chose against making the motion. On appellant's petition for habeas corpus based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the court adopted the findings from the reference hearing that allegations of the incompetency of counsel were speculative and that there was no likelihood of a more favorable result to appellant based on his attorney's conduct. The court found acceptable reasons why counsel did not seek admission of statements by appellant's brother including the negation of the alibi defense, the statements' inconsistencies, the likelihood of perjury, and the possibility that the jury would find appellant guilty of abetting the murder if it believed the brother's statement. Concluding that appellant did not prove ineffective counsel, the court affirmed his conviction and denied his petition for habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for second degree murder holding that the trial court lacked authority to order a new trial on its own motion based on a confession by appellant's brother. The court dismissed appellant's petition for habeas corpus because he did not show his counsel was ineffective for not seeking admission of the brother's statements that would have negated the alibi defense and could have been perjurious.

 

1390 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sanders


Nos. B030989, B037473


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


221 Cal. App. 3d 350; 271 Cal. Rptr. 534; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 884



May 29, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1990. Kennard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. B030989, Madge S. Watai, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied and the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant murderer challenged a conviction from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for second degree murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187 and 189, based upon appellant's contentions that the jury instructions were improper, the prosecutorial comments appealed to the jury's passion, use of a victim impact statement in the probation report during sentencing was error, and there was ineffective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: At appellant murderer's second trial, appellant was represented by new counsel who refused to allow appellant's brother to testify that he shot and killed the victim. Appellant's conviction of second degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187 and 189, was affirmed. The trial court properly gave Cal. Jury Instructions -Criminal No. 2.11.5 (4th ed. 1979), that the jury, in deciding appellant's fate, not consider why any other person was not being prosecuted or whether such person could be. Prosecutorial comments relating to victim's wounds were proper as those comments went to explaining a basis for witness misidentification of appellant as the shooter, and in any event the issue was waived for failing to object at trial. Cal. Evid. Code § 353. Use of victim impact statement in a probation report was specifically permitted under Cal. R. Ct. 419. Appellant's counsel's decision to not use the brother's confession was not ineffective assistance of counsel when the confession could obliterate the complete alibi defense already presented and there was the potential that the confession was manufactured. Thus, appellant failed to meet his burden of proof by preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant murderer's conviction because the jury instructions were a correct statement of the law, the prosecutor did not appeal to jury passion during closing arguments, court rules authorized the use of victim impact statements in probation reports at sentencing, and appellant's counsel was not ineffective.

 

1391 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Terminals Equip. Co. v. City


No. A046428


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


221 Cal. App. 3d 234; 270 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 636



May 25, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 893093, Stuart Pollak, Judge, and Anne Bouliane, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant stevedoring companies sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed appellants' complaint for failure to state a cause of action and stayed discovery, in appellants' inverse condemnation action against respondents, city and county.


OVERVIEW: Appellant stevedoring companies filed suit for inverse condemnation after respondents, city and county, denied appellants' building permit on land owned by one appellant and leased to the other. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer, dismissed appellants' action for failure to state a cause of action, and stayed discovery in the matter. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that appellants did not state a cause of action for inverse condemnation or pre-condemnation damages. There was no allegation in the complaint that either respondent had commenced negotiations with appellants or made any effort to purchase the property, or had made any statements indicating a present intention to acquire the property by condemnation or otherwise. There is no allegation in the complaint that appellants challenged the denial of their building permit, either by appeal to the city's board of permit appeals or by petitioning for a writ of mandate in superior court. As appellants themselves made clear, no condemnation had taken place, and respondents had never taken any steps to commence eminent domain proceedings.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of appellant stevedoring companies' complaint was affirmed because the facts alleged in the complaint failed to indicate that respondents, city and county, had done anything with respect to appellants' property beyond the planning stage, and the denial of appellants' building permit and respondents' subsequent delay in acquiring the property did not amount to a taking.

 

1392 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Stephen W.


No. C006249


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


221 Cal. App. 3d 629; 271 Cal. Rptr. 319; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 699



May 25, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for Publication June 22, 1990.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 22, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. J-17240, Loyd H. Mulkey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which declared her infant son a dependent child of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300. Appellant argued that the trial court erred when it denied her motion to dismiss a petition filed by respondent, Butte County Child Protective Services, which alleged, among other things, that her son was born under the influence of opiates.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after appellant mother gave birth to her son, he displayed symptoms consistent with drug withdrawal. Tests revealed opiates in his urine. The matter was referred to respondent, Butte County Child Protective Services. The minor was taken into temporary custody. Respondent filed a petition which alleged, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300, that the minor's parents were unable to provide for his care, custody, and control due to their substance abuse problems. Appellant filed a motion to dismiss the petition, which the trial court denied. The trial court found that the minor was subject to its jurisdiction, declared him to be a dependent child of the juvenile court, and placed him with his paternal grandparents. On appeal, the court affirmed the denial of appellant's motion to dismiss the petition. The court held that the facts alleged in the petition were legally sufficient to state a cause of action to have the minor declared a dependent of the juvenile court since the petition concisely described his medical condition at birth, that his parents had a longstanding substance abuse problem, and that they were unable to provide for his care, custody, and control.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant mother's motion to dismiss a petition filed by respondent, Butte County Child Protective Services, to commence proceedings to declare appellant's son a dependent child of the juvenile court. The court held that the petition described that he was born under the influence of opiates and other facts that were legally sufficient to state a cause of action to declare him a dependent.

Coutin v. Lucas


No. A045392


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


220 Cal. App. 3d 1016; 270 Cal. Rptr. 93; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 549



May 24, 1990  
May 24, 1990


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to rules 976 and 976.1, California Rules of Court, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions directed to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied June 21, 1990, and appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 15, 1990. Lucas, C. J., did not participate therein.
Review denied by Coutin v. West America Bank, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 3603 (Cal., Aug. 15, 1990)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 138376, Gary W. Thomas, Judge.
Coutin v. President of Hastings' College of Law of University of California, 878 F.2d 1438, 1989 U.S. App. LEXIS 10034 (9th Cir. Cal., 1989)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondent is to receive his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which dismissed appellant's suit after sustaining a demurrer filed by respondent Chief Justice of California, in appellant's action to stop the use of public funds to support a state law school. .


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer filed an application for injunction and declaratory judgment regarding the use of public funds at a state law school. Appellant named respondent Chief Justice of California as a defendant based on appellant's contention that although legislation had repealed respondent's position as chairman of the board of trustees, that repeal had been unconstitutional and respondent remained ex officio president of the law school. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed him from the case. Appellant challenged the trial court's judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the decision to eliminate the requirement that the Chief Justice be chairman of the board of trustees did not violate the law school's autonomy guarantees pursuant to Cal. Const. art. IV, § 9, because the matter of limiting the Chief Justice's outside responsibilities was of statewide concern and because there was no impairment to the board of trustees and no harm to the educational decisions of the institution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant taxpayer's action against respondent Chief Justice of California because respondent was no longer ex officio president of the state law school and the legislation that removed respondent from the position was valid.

 

1394 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Suk


No. B033455


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


220 Cal. App. 3d 952; 269 Cal. Rptr. 676; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 514



May 23, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 23, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A036674, Richard A. Lavine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction, by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for selling a vehicle identification number with intent to defraud under Cal. Veh. Code § 10752. Appellant asserted the statute was unconstitutionally vague, and that the trial court impermissibly amended the information after appellee state rested.


OVERVIEW: At the close of his trial for violating Cal. Veh. Code § 10752, by selling a vehicle identification number (VIN) with intent to defraud, appellant moved for acquittal under Cal. Penal Code § 1118.1, asserting that the VINs were genuine, not counterfeit as charged in the information. Following his conviction, appellant appealed, claiming that § 10752 was vague as to what was proscribed, and that the court erred in amending the information, from the sale of a counterfeit VIN, to the improper sale of a genuine VIN, after appellee state had rested. On appeal, the court affirmed appellant's conviction, holding that the section was not unconstitutionally vague, and there was no error in amending the information. The court found that the section defined the offense in terms of the sale of a VIN for a fraudulent purpose, and the amendment was not prejudicial as the offense was the same whether the VIN was genuine or counterfeit.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for sale of a vehicle identification number (VIN) for fraudulent purposes was affirmed because the law was not vague, and amendment of the information from sale of a counterfeit VIN to sale of a genuine VIN after appellee people rested was not error, as the intent to defraud focus of the offense was the same.

 

1395 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Colony Cove Assocs. v. Brown


No. B042961


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


220 Cal. App. 3d 195; 269 Cal. Rptr. 234; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 463



May 10, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of all appellants for review by the Supreme Court was denied August 10, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. SCC21936 and SCC21937, Arthur H. Jean, Jr., Judge, and Anita Rae Shapiro, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed. Respondent's request for sanctions is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenants sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent mobile park owner in respondent's unlawful detainer action to remove appellants who did not comply with respondent's age restriction rule in the park.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mobile home park owner enacted a rule restricting residency to people 55 or older and included a grandfather clause for tenants under 55 at the time of the enactment. Appellant tenants, each of whom had children born after the age rule was enacted, were evicted. Respondent then brought an unlawful detainer action against appellants and was granted summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the age rule did not violate constitutional proscriptions, as the rule was neither arbitrary nor irrational and served an important purpose in meeting the needs of senior citizens. The court held that the rule also did not violate relevant state statutes, observing that amendments to the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51 et seq., specifically excluded mobile home parks from its broad antidiscrimination policy. The age rule did not violate the Fair Housing Amendments Act of 1988, as the rule fell within the exception for "housing for older persons" in which discrimination on the basis of familial status was not prohibited pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 3706(b)(1). As there were no triable issues of fact, summary judgment below was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent mobile home park owner against appellant tenants because respondent's age restriction rule did not violate constitutional proscriptions because it was neither arbitrary nor irrational, nor did it violate state discrimination laws or federal fair housing laws given specific exceptions for mobile home parks and housing for older persons respectively.

 

1396 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Southern Cal. Rapid Transit Dist. v. Bolen


No. B032265.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two.


235 Cal. App. 3d 134; 269 Cal. Rptr. 147; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 435



May 1, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted August 16, 1990 (S015986) (See 1 Cal. 4th 654)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C656503, Billy G. Mills, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of March 1, 1990, previously Reported at 219 Cal. App. 3d 1446 and 228 Cal. App. 3d 906.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant transit district's secretary challenged the order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which issued a writ of mandate directing defendant to certify the resolution that created special benefit districts in order to fund a transit system. The trial court found that the districts had been validly created.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff transit district filed suit against defendant transit district's secretary to compel validation of two special benefit assessment districts created to fund the transit system. Defendant refused to certify the resolutions on the ground that Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 33000 et seq., which authorized the creation of the districts, was constitutionally infirm, and the board of directors had not complied with the law in imposing the assessments. The trial court found that the districts had been validly created and granted a writ of mandate directing defendant to certify the resolution. The court reversed the order and held that the statute was violative of constitutional equal protection. There was no relationship between votes awarded and assessments paid. Nonlandowners shared an equal interest in the creation of a transit system, and voters who did not own property could not have been constitutionally excluded from voting on bonds. The benefits and burdens of the assessment fell indiscriminately on property owner and nonproperty owner alike. The election provisions of the statute were not separable; therefore, the statutory scheme in its entirety was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted a writ of mandate directing defendant transit district's secretary to certify the resolution that created special benefit assessment districts in favor of plaintiff transit district. The statute was violative of equal protection because there was no relationship between votes awarded and assessments paid. The election provisions were not separable, so the entire statutory scheme was unconstitutional.

 

1397 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Bullock v. City and County of San Francisco


No. A043073, Nos. A044386, A046047.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four.


219 Cal. App. 3d 1331; 269 Cal. Rptr. 123; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 425



April 30, 1990.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted May 30, 1990 (See 221 Cal. App. 3d 1072)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]  

Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 809721, 898704, John E. Dearman, Lucy Kelly McCabe, and Stuart R. Pollak, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hotel owner sought review of an order of the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California), which granted intervenor hotel tenants association's motion for judgment on the pleadings in a suit brought by plaintiff for an exemption from an ordinance of defendant city prohibiting the conversion of residential hotel units for overnight guest use.


OVERVIEW: Prior to plaintiff hotel owner's purchase of a hotel, defendant city enacted an ordinance prohibiting the conversion of any residential hotel unit into a unit intended for the use of transient overnight guests and denied plaintiff's application for an exemption. The trial court in that case denied plaintiff's petition for administrative mandamus. The trial court granted intervenor hotel tenants association's motion for judgment on the pleadings and awarded intervenor's attorney fees. The court held that the decision of the first trial court was res judicata as to all issues except plaintiff's new 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim. The court held that it was error for the trial court to grant intervenor's motion for judgment on the pleadings as to plaintiff's § 1983 claim because the claim alleged actions by defendant such as "spot-zoning," retaliation, administrative deficiency, and a denial of equal protection. Therefore, the court reversed the portion of the trial court's judgment granting intervenor's motion for a judgment on the pleadings as to § 1983 claim and the award of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the trial court's judgment granting intervenor hotel tenants association's motion for judgment on the pleadings as to plaintiff hotel owner's federal claims and the trial court's award of attorney fees to intervenors. The court otherwise affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1398 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Floystrup v. City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Bd.


No. A040752


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


219 Cal. App. 3d 1309; 268 Cal. Rptr. 898; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 426



April 30, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 30, 1990, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Poche, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 18, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 627123-9, Jacqueline Taber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The Board is to pay costs and attorney fees. The matter is remanded to the trial court: (1) to issue a writ of mandate directing the Board to vacate its decisions in case numbers RWN 554, 578, 579, 581 and 582 and ordering the Board (and/or the tenants) to repay the rents withheld and the registration fees paid by landlords pursuant to the stipulation; and (2) to determine the costs and reasonable attorney fees payable to landlords ( Gov. Code, § 800; Plumbing etc. Employers Council v. Quillin (1976) 64 Cal.App.3d 215, 224 [134 Cal.Rptr. 332]; Campbell v. Residential Rent Stabilization & Arbitration Bd. (1983) 142 Cal.App.3d 123, 128-130 [190 Cal.Rptr. 829]).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlords challenged an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), denying their petition seeking reversal of defendant City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Board's rent withholding orders for the tenants, the real parties in interest. Plaintiffs sought review of the constitutionality of the Berkeley Rent Stabilization and Eviction for Good Cause Ordinance, Berkeley, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 13.76(A) (1) and (2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Board sued plaintiff landlords for failure to register their properties under the Berkeley Rent Stabilization and Eviction for Good Cause Ordinance. The parties stipulated that plaintiffs would pay the registration fees and defendant would not impose rent withholding prior to seeking a permanent injunction and obtaining resolution of the issues raised by the registration requirement. The tenants, the real parties in interest, requested rent withholding, which defendant granted. Plaintiffs petitioned for a writ of mandate seeking reversal of the rent withholding and a declaration that the registration requirements, Berkeley, Cal., Municipal Code § 13.76.150(A)(1) and (2), were unconstitutional. The trial court denied the petition and plaintiffs appealed. The court reversed and directed defendant to vacate its rent withholding orders, which were a violation of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1, the judicial powers provision. Defendant was estopped by its prior stipulation from issuing rent withholding orders. The withheld rent was to be paid to plaintiffs, along with their registration fees. Plaintiffs were awarded attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and directed that the rent withholding order of defendant City of Berkeley Rent Stabilization Board be vacated because it violated the judicial powers provision of the California Constitution. Defendant was estopped from issuing rent withholding orders as it breached the parties' prior stipulation. Plaintiff landlords were to be paid the rent withheld by the tenants, the registrations fees, and attorney fees.

 

1399 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Turnbull & Turnbull v. ARA Transportation, Inc.


No. C002975


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


219 Cal. App. 3d 811; 268 Cal. Rptr. 856; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 361; 1990-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P69,073



April 17, 1990 


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III through VIII and IX-B and IX-C.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied May 16, 1990, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of appellant and respondent for review by the Supreme Court was denied July 11, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 164610, James P. Darrah, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court for a new trial limited to a determination of the amount of damages in accordance with part VIII of this opinion. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the decision of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which awarded plaintiff compensatory and punitive damages in an action alleging violations of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17043.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and plaintiff were competitors who offered bus transportation service. After defendant had been awarded contracts for several consecutive years, plaintiff brought suit alleging that defendant had sold its transportation services at below cost, in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17043. On appeal the court held that § 17043 survived defendant's due process challenge, because the statute was rationally related to the legitimate purpose of safeguarding against monopolies and encouraging competition. Defendant's argument that § 17043 was preempted by the Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq., was rejected, because an inference of preemption was inappropriate where the basic purposes of the state and federal statutes were similar. The court reversed the award of punitive damages to plaintiff, because plaintiff was limited to the treble damages provided by Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17082.


OUTCOME: The judgment awarding punitive damages to plaintiff was reversed, because where a new right not existing at common law was created by statute and statutory remedy for infringement thereof was provided, the remedy was exclusive of all others unless the statutory remedy was inadequate.

 

1400 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Adams v. Murakami


No. B031380.


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five.


228 Cal. App. 3d 885; 268 Cal. Rptr. 467; 1990 Cal. App. LEXIS 347



April 12, 1990 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 17, and May 4, 1990; Review granted June 27, 1990 (S003530) issue to be argued shall be limited to whether a plaintiff or a defendant must prove a defendant's net worth to recover punitive damages. (See 54 Cal.3d 105)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C418409, Leon S. Kaplan, Judge.

This Opinion has been Reprinted for the Original Opinion of April 5, 1990, previously reported at 219 Cal. App. 3d 647.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which entered judgment and damages against appellant for medical malpractice and intentional infliction of emotional distress pursuant to a jury verdict in an action brought by respondent, patient's conservator.


OVERVIEW: Appellant physician challenged the judgment and damages entered by the trial court for medical malpractice and intentional infliction of emotional distress. Appellant claimed that his conduct was not outrageous, that the claims were for parental consortium, and that the patient was incapable of knowing that she was being injured. The court affirmed the judgment and damage award because appellant's conduct was outrageous and done with a conscious disregard for the consequences, patient was capable of acknowledging the harm, and the punitive damage award was reasonable. The court held that appellant was responsible for the patient's care, chose to disregard patient's physical well-being, and failed to take even the simplest measure to ascertain her status or protect patient from the inevitable. Appropriate medical care would have prevented patient's injuries. The court held that patient's injuries were those regularly held recoverable, as the claims were based on appellant's numerous and egregious failures. The record was replete with evidence from which evidence of malice was reasonably inferred and supported the punitive damages award.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and damages award because the evidence was clear that appellant physician's action were outrageous and malicious, and appellant consciously disregarded the consequences of his failure to adequately perform responsibly with regard to the patient.
